KILLS FLIES
The Red Page
Fiction
Irhomas Burke makes au astonishing
>ut in English literature. Limehouse
ghts: 2' ales of Chinatown (Grant
chards, pev Dymoek’s Liorary) definitely
ices Thomas Burke in the front rank of
tglish novelists. These remarkable short
n-ies present a new genius in literature.
English fiction there is no writer with
10m one can satisfactorily compare him.
i has the warm romance of Conrad ; but
is not of the Conrad school. He seems
stinctively to have acquired the brilliant
jhnique that made O. Henry famous; but
ere is not in his work that occasionally
■Rating feeling felt in O. Henry that the
>ry lias been cleverly constructed for its
expected climax. In Limehouse lights
e last paragraph is the logical completion
the story’s structural growth: after all,
e gorgeous blossom of poetry may have its
ots in manure. -Thomas. Burke has the
uminative realism of the ffcflTms Russian
velists; but he interprets that realism in
mis of poetry and splendor. Amid the
immest sordidness and cruelty and horror
the dregs of Limehouse he discovers the
umphant beauty of the human soul.
To a White Australian it. may seem re-
irkable that Thomas Burke has made lit-
ature out of such a subject as white girls
ring with Chinese. Most of hbs stories
M with this repellent fact, and are set
the opium houses of Limehouse’s China-
wn. Yet “The Chink and the Child” is
tale for tears, and should you hear it in
e lilied tongue of the yellow men, it would
vaken in you all your pity.” Lucy was
child of 12, whom Battling Burrows, a
igilist, horribly ill-treated. Cheng, a
tiinese sailor and a poet, met her in an
hum den. “She knew well what she had
be afraid of in that place; but she was
it afraid of Cheng.” He took her to his
10m, but merely to worship her white per-
ction. He decorated his room for his
imaculate goddess. Then Battling broke
1, took the protesting child away, and
hipped her to death. The final tragedy is
?yed in the highest tone of beauty and
athos. In another story there is humor,
his time the Chink was not immaculate.
1 his narration Thomas Burke permits him-
>lf the traditional row of dots that
> the hardened fiction-reader always means
ne thing; but the author disdains these
olite subterfuges. After his row of dots
e cheerfully continues: “When Marigold
Live herself to Tai Ling, as I have explained
' that row of dots ” And when Mari-
old was about to become a mother, to Tai
ling’s surprise there arose rivals, yellow
nd white, from all quarters of Limehouse,
1 claim the paternity. “There, then, Mari-
old lay in a comfortable bed,, comfortably
ttended, awaiting her time; while four men,
nly politely recognising each other’s exist-
nce, sat below and wrangled for the honor
f the fatherhood. Was ever a woman in so
bameful and so delicious a situation?”
Into the gruesome Thomas Burke plunges
dth Poe’s command of the shudder and the
brill. “The Paw” is a horrible story of
revenge, in wdiich a brutal father, whose
rife had cleared with a Chinaman, beats
Is child until he beats into her dazed mind
he necessity for her to murder that China-
nan. The long-continued horror of those
ratings is only partially redeemed, by the
loetic justice of the climax. And in “The
Jorilla and the Girl,” a sickening shudder,
n which a brutal father shuts up his
laughter in a room with a gorilla, the
lorror is dissolved in pathos.
Burke’s command of atmosphere is every-
where apparent. He makes us see Lime-
louse as if we lived there. Take this beauti-
:ul passage, which affords us a fine impres-
sion of Burke’s colorful style. “Against the
savage purple of the night rose a few wisps
if rigging and some gruff. funnels: lyrics
n steel and iron, their leaping lines as cor-
rect and ecstatic as a rimed verse. Under
the cold glare of the arclights, gangs of
(Asiatics hurried with that impassive swift-
ness which gives no impression of haste....
Hardly the place to which one would turn
as to the city of his dreams; yet there are
those who do. Hearts are broken in Black-
wall Gardens. The pity and terror and
wonder of first love burn in the blood and
limbs of those who serve behind the counters
of East India Dock or load up cargo-boats
at the landing-stages. Love-mad hands have
buried knives in little white bosoms in
Commercial-road, and songs are written by
the moon across many a happy garret-win-
dow in Cable-street.” And in another story
the author takes a well : known incident in
Lotidon, -where a houseful of desperate, well-
armed men held up the rest of England for
some days; and this is why he tells this
story. “It is an episode in the life and
death of Beryl Hermione Maud Chudder
and of Croucher Stumpley, and it is told
because it is beautiful and because the rest
of England arose in its fat, satin’d. Bays-
water wrath and called it beastly.” As
Burke tells it, the story has the high pathos
of the last act of Romeo and Juliet. In
contrast to this, “Gina of the Chinatown”
is one of the most delightful idylls of youth
and the music-hall. The descriptions of his
heroines are as beautiful as they themselves
must have been. They have not the beauty
that is classical and Greek; for Burke
rightly remarks of a visitor to Chinatown
that her beauty, being classical and Greek,
meant “that she was about as interesting
as a hard-boiled egg.” His heroines have
the wayward beauty of wistful youth and
passion ; though for most of them .“life was
just a dark house in a dark street.”
Thomas Burke will be worth watching.
When William J. Locke discards his im-
possible grotesques and determines to write
a simple story he can do it with a light and
truthful touch that is almost inimitable.
The Wonderful Year (Lane) is a charming
story of a meek young man and three maids.
The dismissed school-teacher is sent wander-
ing through France with a self-possessed
girl—a most unsentimental journey. But
the man, spending his last sou on his glori-
ous adventure, discovers his soul, though
he has to become a waiter and other things
in order to find mmself. Ultimately the war
happens along, but Locke differs from most
novelists by making his reader feel that the
war has not been introduced merely to
settle the affairs of his hero. The three
girls are delightfully characterised, and
Locke, wise old bird, knows how to keep
his reader guessing which will get the. hero.
The value of the nove 1 to the reader is the
intimate picture of provincial France and
the provincial French that Locke, in the
guise of fiction, so enthusiastically and
appreciatively paints. The Wonderful Year
is a fine product of the Entente.
J*
A. S. Neill, author of A Dominie's Log,
continues his engaging humor, shrewdness
and humanity in its sequel, A Dominie Dis-
missed (Jenkins, per Sydney Book Club,
and the publishers). So delightful a peda-
gogue as Neill was bound to be dismissed;
and it was only to be expected of so
original a personality that when ordered
by his doctor to recuperate his nerves by
vegetating in the country, he should take a
situation as a cattle-man with a farmer
in the village where he had been dominie.
The humors of the bairns are delicious, and
there is humor —or pathos—in the situation
of the dismissed teacher watching his suc-
cessor undo all that Neill fought for; but
Neill is serious beneath his fun. Educa-
tion, he says, aims frankly at turning out
highly efficient wage-slaves. “If the plans
of the Paris Economic Conference are car-
ried out true education is interned for a
century. Millions have lost their lives in
the military war: millions will lose their
souls in the trade war.... The first thing a
child should learn is to be a rebel. If a
man isn’t a rebel by the time he is 25, God
help him!” Neill has a pleasing theory
that the Calvinistic attitude to the public-
house is the chief cause of Scots’ drunken-
ness. “When a Scot enters a bar he knows
that he won’t have the courage to be seen
coming out again, and he very naturally
says to himself: ‘Ach, to hell! Aw’ll hae
another just to fortify mysel’ for gaein’
oot!’ ” Returning to education: “It is
more important for a boy to know some-
thing about feminine psychology than about
Henry the Eighth. He will one day be
called upon to choose a wife, but he’ll never
bo called on to choose a king... .Arithmetic
is the trump card of the man who wants
a utilitarian education. I can do lots of
sums.. ..1 could almost do a cube root. So
far as I can remember I have never had
occasion to use arithmetic for any purpose
other than adding up money or multiplying
a few figures by a few figures....l know
nothing about the process of law; I don’t
know what steps one takes to get married
or divorced or cremated or naturalised....
From a utilitarian point of view it is much
more important to know how to get married
than how to find the latitude and longitude
of Naples.”
Neill’s excellent theory is to allow a child
to learn what he wants to learn; and he
found some who wanted to learn arithmetic.
His ideal school would have no classes. His
teachers would merely act as guides to be
consulted when necessary, and they would
learn with their pupils. A teacher should
not be an encyclopaedia of facts; he should
be ab inquirer. When Neill’s successor con-
sults him about the allocation of prizes, and
wants to know if Dave was good at any-
thing, Neill replies: “Well, he holds the
record for spitting further than any boy in
the school; I think he deserves a prize 'for
that. Believe me, every boy in the class
would rather hold that record than carry off
the prize for arithmetic.” Even reading
penny-dreadfuls is education. Magistrates
trace all juvenile crime to penny dreadfuls
and the cinema. Penny-dreadfuls give a boy
a vocabulary and exercise his imagination.
Comparing the penny-dreadful with Dickens,
Neill remarks that the penny-dreadful goes
straight to the point. “Harold looked
straight into the blue barrel of a Colt auto-
matic.” Dickens would have given way to that
Victorian nuisance, circumlocution, like this:
“Harold contemplated with extreme appre-
hension the circular muzzle of a cerulean
blue automatic pistol of the kind specifically
manufactured by the celebrated world-famous
American firm of Colt.” “The romantic way
to look at crime is to fix your attention on
drink and penny-dreadfuls and cinema
shows; the realistic way is to look bravely
at the economic division of wealth that
causes poverty and disease, the father and
mother of crime.”
All through this text-book for teachers
runs the delightful love-story of Margaret.
The dismissed dominie falls in love with the
simple Margaret. '“Do you know that popu-
lar song, ‘You Made Me Love You’? That’s
the only popular song I ever struck that lias
any philosophy in it. ‘You made me love
you ; I didn’t want to do it’... . Margaret,
that’s the true story of love. I didn’t want
to love you; my mind kept telling me that
you were not the right woman. . . . and here
I sit in paradise because your head is on my
shoulder.”
The Navy
Henry Newbolt’s Tales of the Great War
i ijongmans. Green,, per Dymock’s Library)
are authentic .history, brilliantly written.
And as three of them deal with the Sydney-
Emden fight, with Von Spee’s two naval
actions in the Pacific, and with the Jutland
battle, the fact that Newbolt guarantees
them as accurate gives them a value even
greater than the author’s manner of telling
them. Newbolt states that he laid his
sketches before those who appear in his
stories. ‘'Two great Admirals, one great
General, and several junior officers, have
read my MS. and made corrections and addi-
tions of the utmost importance. I need
hardly tell you that, with such evidence at
my disposal, I have not invented one word
of the book. Even when I have ventured to
comment or praise I have followed the
opinions expressed by eyewitnesses.” He
apologises for not having given a bbok of
“Deeds of-Daring”; but he adds: “Is it
great moments that you want? Think then
of the moment when the Australian boys
began to feel the Emden’s shot; when
Sturdee sighted the Gneisenau; when
Beattie saw the Queen Mary sunk with all
her men; the lonely moment of the night
when Smitli-Dorrien decided to stand and
fight, against all odds and orders.” So
Newbolt wisely says that tne only way. to
tell these true tales is the plain way, “with-
out embroidery.” He leaves his reader to
put in the embroidery.
So with Sturdee editing Newbolt’s
proofs, we have the authentic stories of
Coronel and the .Falkland Islands. Newbolt
is sure that Craddock, who went down in a
fight that was hopeless from the outset,' was
not at all likely to have underestimated the
strength of his enemy. Craddock had asked
for reinforcements: the Admiralty merely
sent him the slow old Canopus. If Crad-
dock could catch the Germans before they
were reinforced by the Schamlwrst and the
Gneisenau, he was a match for their inferior
ships; if the enemy fleet junctioned and he
met them before the Canopus could come up,
he was sure to be defeated. Anyhow,
Craddock meant to tight. “He had asked
for reinforcements, and the Admiralty had
sent him what they thought sufficient. It
was not for him to hold back after that.”
Now t , Lord Fisher had come back to the
Admiralty the day before the defeat of
Coronel. He had no doubts about the man
to command the expedition he was sending
out. It was evident that if anyone was to
be criticised for the Coronel disaster it
would be those officers at the Admiralty
who had advised the sending of the old
Canopus to reinforce Craddock. The chief
of these was Admiral Sturdee. “Now, as
Admiral Sturdee might some day be held
partly responsiole for Craddock’s fate, it
was only reasonable that he should be given
the chance to ‘clean-up’ the situation him-
self.” So Sturdee went to his trial and
triumph. Fisher insisted on the immediate
departure of the Invincible and the In-
flexible, ready or not ready. If they had
waited the two days the dockyard officials
demanded, Von Spee would have reached
the Falklands' before they did, and there
would have been a different battle. The
secret of the departure was wonderfully well
kept, and the precautions taken on the
voyage, including a wireless message that
bluffed the Germans, were extraordinary.
As one of the German prisoners said, when
they approached the Falklands, the Germans
laughed “till their sides ached,” for all they
saw were some small British vessels and
the old Canopus on her mudbank. It looked
easy. But the next moment they “cried till
their eyes ached.” They had caught sight,
over the land, of the tripod masts of the
two Invincibles. Even then they thought
they were Japanese and not British —how
could British battle-cruisers have come
there?
Comparing Von Spee with Craddock,
Newbolt, after a brilliant account of the
fight, points out that each commander knew
his inferiority from the first. Craddock
determined to fight. Von Spee thought it
his duty to run. “But it is clear that run-
ning, whether it was his own policy or that
of his Service, was not only a losing game,
but the game of the losing side. You have
only to consider what it would have been
worth to Germany to have had a Craddock
flying his flag in the Scharnhorst on that
December 8. You can imagine him. when
the great battle-cruisers came out of harbor,
signalling (as Craddock did). ‘I am going
to attack enemy now,’ and going straight
to meet them at full speed. Their steam was
not yet up—he could have' closed them then
and there. What a fight that would have
been! No impotent scattering flight, with
ships turning vainly this way and that, to
bring their guns to bear upon an enemy
beyond their reach; but a desperate short-
range action with every shot telling—a
chance of dealing the enemy a heavy blow
before the end, and the certainty of leaving
a great tradition to the Service. Beyond all
doubt, that would have been Craddock's way
—the way of Coronel.”
Summing up the Emden fight, Newbolt
says: “The action was the first ever fought
by the new Australian Navy, and it is of
great importance to a new service that the
personnel of it should stamp themselves at
once as fighting men of the first class. This
the whole company of the Sydney did —in
less than two hours they had established a
tradition.”
New bolt writes a lucid account of the
intricate strategy of the naval victory of
Jutland; and points out that for a fresh
battle during the month of June Jellicoe
could have brought into action a fleet the
exact equivalent of his original fleet, minus
only four battle-cruisers. The Kaiser could
have sent out 14 battleships instead of 22,
no battle-cruisers, two armored cruisers and
perhaps 10 light cruisers—a force which
could with absolute certainty be destroyed
by two squadrons of our Battle Fleet and
their light cruisers. “The German Navy,
which on May 30 ranked second among the
navies of the world, on May 31 sank to
fifth place, leaving ours in a greater superio-
rity than before. A deadlier loss still is
the doubt cast upon the efficiency of their
gunnery. Its failure seems to have been
due to the worst of all causes, a defect not
of material but of personnel.” Under pun-
ishment our gunnery was just as efficient;
their efficiency became rapidly reduced.
As an eye-witness aptly observed :
“We shook the German Fleet to its heart;
they will neA T er be the same fleet again.”
Space does not permit anything but the
mere mention of the admirable narratives
of the sister Service. Indubitably the book
is one to be read and kept.
Jt
For -a complete and up-to-date account of
the inner life of the Royal Navy the reader
is cordially referred to Pincher Martin, 0.D.,
by “Taffrail” (Chambers; 3s. 6d.). O.D.
means ordinary seaman. The author takes
his typical bluejacket right through all his
training; explaining, in the guise of fiction,
everything about the Navy that the civilian
is likely to Avant to know. The latter half
of the book deals interestingly with Pincher’s
life in the war, first on a cruiser that Avas
torpedoed and then in a destroyer. Pincher’s
adventures on “The Day” are ably described;
and from this book one understands how
much that is dramatic lies behind those curt
Admiralty phrases that appear in its reports.
The author declares that bis incidents are
true, but they have necessarily been given a
fictitious coloring. He need not have
troubled: the incidents he describes so
accurately are quite obviously true.
d*
John Buchan’s account of The Battle of
Jutland (Nelson, 4d. here) is a concise
and lucid analysis of the most confused
naval scrap in history. Unfortunately he
furnishes only one plan of the battle, and
that is a rough and merely approximate
sketch. Even a brief essay like this should
be illustrated Avith a dozen diagrams. But
Buchan makes much of the strategy plain.
When Beatty, fighting against heavy odds,
determined to nurse his pursuers into the
arms of Jellicoe, though technically he Avas
the pursued his superior speed made him
the pursuer. “Once the northerly course
had been entered upon the enemy could not
change direction, except in a very gradual
curve, without exposing himself to enfilading
fire from the British cruisers at the head
of the line.” To the layman, one of the
unexplained things about this _ battle is the
escape of the German fleet in the night.
“The whole British Fleet lay between the
enemy and his home ports. It was a stra-
tegic situation which, but for the fog and
the coming of night, would have meant his
complete destruction.” But Jellicoe could
not risk his fleet in the darkness in a sea
swarming with torpedo craft and sub-
marines; though, of course, the battle, went
on during the night, waged by the lighter
craft. Yet they failed to keep touch.
Buchan mentions a fleeting glance one of
our ships had of “some of the enemy battle-
ships slipping past the British stern in the
fog and darkness.” And at dawn the enemy
had disappeared. A large amount of him
had disappeared to the bottom; but of the
remainder, “partly he had slipped in single
ships astern of our fleet during the night;
partly he was then engaged in moving home-
wards like a flight of wild duck that had
been scattered by shot.” Without any sug-
gestion of criticism or any knoAvledge of
naval tactics, this page Avonders what would
have happened if Jellicoe had risked break-
ing up his formation and ordered each of
his ships to choose her nearest antagonist
and stick to her till she sank her. However,
the results are satisfactory enough. As
Buchan says, “Germany was overjoyed at
having escaped annihilation, and the magni-
tude of her jubilation may be taken as the
measure of her fears.”

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort and good
health artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the
perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-
where. Post free, 1/3. Free sample posted.

The pagan shades,”
Then finding tame
The endless ball,
He met a dame
Superb and tall.
She came unasked
With languorous grace.
Iler face was masked —•
Her shadowed face.
He danced content.
The room grew dim.
The lady bent,
Caressing him.
“Here’s life!” he cried.
“Here’s leaping breath !”
“Not life,” she sighed....
“My name is Death!”
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.
Love, the Fowler.
Cease your wild singing, O unrestful bird,
Fluttering your frightened pinions in my
heart,
As though you felt the fowler’s closing
hand,
And in anticipation said farewell
To all the daisied meads, the yellow
streams—■
From which the noon sun steals the cloudy
blues
To make a mirror for his radiant face —
Fringed with the summer faintness of the
fern !
Cease your wild trembling, O prophetic
bird !
Across the street I hear a wooden blind
Stir in the breeze against a. balcony,
As though it knew the fingers of the wind.
Stretched forth from river gardens cool and
wet,
Felt ’midst the slats, that once were living
part
Of some pine forest, for vain memories
Of other winds that woke long since and
trod
The hidden perfumes from pale violets.
Flames by the domed grey House of Parlia-
ment—
The clustering poinsianas, where the red
Of blossom is a magic carpet laid
Across the crest of those close-kissing leaves,
And, pearl and silver, springs another day
A shimmering searchlight from the under-
world. '
A sparrow, commonplace and pert and
brown,
Housewife of some vined eave or shrubby
nest,
Twitters as though to rival my wild bird.
Till that sad singing shames its cheeping
dumb.
And yet to-morrow this smug speckled elf
Will have its way, maybe, of roof and tile
And burgeoning -tree and shadowy caves of
grass.
While mv wild bird who drearped an eagle’s
flight v
May lie a bunch of feathers on the floor
Of some cramped cage that makes sarco-
phagus,
Or. with its soaring voice forever still.
Peck slavishly the grain the Fowler gives!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
A Song of Gladness.
I glimpse the plume of joy and catch the
beat
Of woodland music piped for fairy feet.
Blue sea, green trees, and butterflies at play
Quiver with laughter in the golden day.
Cleaving the air a little brown bird flings
Backward to earth the thrill of mounting
wings.
And Summer goes her way with white-limbed
grace,
Dropping a flower in every desert place.
Radiant my theme. A minor note of woe
I dare not press—my heart broke long ago!
N.S.W.
NORA McAULIFFE.
Youth and Age.
Youth, the prospector, goes.
Searching the wide world o’er;
Lust in his bright eye glows;
Eager, from shore to shore,
Seeking what youth can find ;
Leaving the dross behind.
Only the best of gold
His high soul satisfies.
New dreams and manifold
See, still, his questing eyes
Down young adventure’s track ;
Never his feet turn back.
Ever his restless mind
Matches his onward stride;
Seeking some newer find
In vale or mountain side
Youth, the prospector, goes;
Patience but small he knows.
Age with a wearied limb
Treads back the path he came.
There is some gold for him
In each abandoned claim ;
Grains left, in very truth,
In the old dust of youth.
Where, with impatience, passed
Youth, with his heart a-spring.
There, his fire quenched at last.
Age does his fossicking.
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, FEBRUARY 8, 1917.
THE BILL.
How is the wav going to be paid for? The
probability is that no one on earth knows.
The figures have already swollen to such
a siz3 that the human mind cannot grasp
the significance of them; and the fight is
still raging furiously. But the problem is
weighing heavily on every combatant
country. Whatever the result of the
struggle may be in a military sense, the
fighting nations are going to wake up to the
realisation of a holocaust in which millions
of lives have been broken, industrial sys-
tems turned upside down and inside out,
and obligations created which can no more
be met by following the old lines than by
merely walking about on bare feet.
* * * *
In England there are people thinking
about this —and thinking hard. One of the
results has'been the formation of an Empire
Resources Development Committee, and from
it last week there emerged a statement
“advocating the conservation of the natural
resources which are under the ownership
or control of the Imperial, Dominion and
Indian Governments.”
The development of this great and varied wealth
for State purposes under State’ auspices would lift
from the peoples of the Empire the burdens due to
the war. The national debt after the war will pro-
bably amount to £4,000,000,000, and the Govern-
ment will need an income of £500,000,000. The
committee’s scheme would enable the Empire to pay
the interest and also to secure unearned increment
out of which the whole of the national debt could
ultimately be paid. The State should assist in
providing such public facilities as electric power,
and thus cheapen the cost.
It is obviously impossible to criticise a
statement so general in its terms as this.
'To find fault with it, as one of the Sydney
newspapers did, on the ground that it
merely consists of a recommendation to
“extend the Government stroke” and lose
money is futile. What is the alternative to
great' measures of organisation and conser-
vation? By what other means than by
diverting the national riches .to the State
under conditions which protect the peoples
from exploitation can even the fringe of
the war-charges be grappled with? In the
vear before the war Britain carried a
national debt of £661,473,765 on which the
annual charge amounted to £24,500,000. A
revenue of £195,825,000 was enough. Those
days have gone and the talk is of a debt ot
£4,000.000,000, and a yearly revenue almost
as great as the value of all the exports sold
by the United Kingdom. To-day the taxa-
tion in Britain is' stupendous. To carry it
to the point of meeting a charge on a debt
of £4.000,000,000 in addition to the ordinary
cost of government (even if this were cut
to the bone) will surely be to
an immense impetus to the commerce ot
neutral countries free from such exactions..
To make matters worse, the earning power
of the country has been greatly reduced.
Many millions of income came from
foreign investments. The bulk of these
have been made valueless by the war or
the securities have been purchased by the
Government and converted into permanent
debt. Trade openings have been seized by
competitors, and it is safe to say that the
earning power of every countrv engaged in
the war has undergone tremendous shrink-
age. When the war stops and the people
turn to pick up the threads of industry
again—to live on earnings instead of bor-
rowings and repay what they had been sub-
sisting on in the meantime —they will do
so under conditions in which the disordered
old “muddle through somehow” would pro-
voke bankruptcy.
* * * *
To Australia this question of how the
nation’s war-created burden shall be carried
is no less important than it is to Britain
and France and Germany. By the middle of
the year the indebtedness of the States and
the Commonwealth will be in the vicinity of
£500,000,000 —almost on a level with the
pre-war debt of Britain and twice the size
of the Imperial funded and unfunded debt of
Germany prior to Armageddon. What is
going to be done? The brutal fact is that
the necessity of coming to some conclusion
on the subject is only being avoided by the
borrowing trick —by plunging deeper and
deeper into the quagmire. It is a mournful
truth that in this country not five minutes’
serious consideration has yet been given to
the crumbling foundations of our economic
being. If we escape disaster it will be by
the help of others, not in consequence of
anything, that may be done to avoid it by the
reckless pigmies who infest our public life.
This debt on Australia’s shoulders is .an
appalling reality. To imagine that we can
go on increasing it is madness. Australia
will directly be faced with the stern, naked
facts of existence, and it seems as if not
until the shock strikes her will the problem
of what we are going to do about it have
any consideration. Our Parliaments are
playing at war and playing at peace. It
may be that the Australian democracy is
incapable of handling that large measure of
State Socialism which alone seems to this
association in England to offer a way of
escape there from the consequences of war.
Certainly such experiments as have been
made with the animal hitherto are lament-
ably different from the kind of Socialism that
might keep the country from ruin. But that
State Socialism has been prostituted by
party wire-pullers and political loan-mong-
ers, and converted into a sort of disorgan-
ised charity, is no reason for believing that
these calamitous happenings are the last
words on the subject. If they are, the ex-
periences of the Commonwealth are most
certainly going to be variegated and numer-
ous. The population includes many thou-
sands of people who have spent a lot of
valuable time mouthing about their admira-
tion for public enterprise and Socialism, and
all the rest of it. This paper would like to
remind them now that the time is closing in
when something more than talk is wanted —•
when all the public enterprise and personal
industry that the people are capable of
will be in urgent demand. If they fail, Aus-
tralia will be drawn into entanglements from
which escape will be impossible. Have the
political parties any ideas on the subject, or
are they waiting to try to borrow them, too?
ON THE FOLLY OF TRYING TO
WEAR THE WRONG CLOTHES.
W. M. Hughes has been yelling with the
vigor that once charmed the duchesses that
Australia must arise and put on the toga
of nationhood; and his fine classic reference
opens up whole worlds of thought. The
toga, as worn by select and patrician Home,
was about the most astonishing garment any
section of mankind ever attempted to
stagger into. Somewhere about the age of
16 the boy who had the hard luck to belong
to the aristocracy of the Eternal City put
off the garb of childhood and assumed the
amazing tangles, creases and folds of the
toga of manhood. The Prime Minister’s idea
appears to be that Australia is now of the
right age to be swathed up in an affair of
a similar kind —a garment of infinite dignity
in which it is possible to talk, but almost
impossible to work. The toga varied a lot
at different periods, but in its sternest form
it was an enormous semi-circle of woollen
cloth, Without arm-hole or leg-hole or any
set place for the neck, which was hung
about the patrician by expert slaves, and
only stayed in its place by dint of
acrobatic' stateliness on the part of the
wearer. To call upon Australia to get into
a thing like that would, on the face of it,
a ppear to be a kind of freak lunacy; but
it is a lunacy quite common to statesmen,
kings and political writers. The toga is, in
their minds, the emblem of nationhood ; the
ideal of Imperial Rome is always in their
eyes; and the make and shape of men is
entirely forgotten in the game of statecraft
as it is unscientifically played. The Roman
Empire in its world sweep was the best
ordered, most dignified and. in a general
way, least troublesome organisation of man-
kind ever known; therefore a nation to be
any good must be something like it. It is
an idea that has held for a thousand years
or more and still grips. Even statesmen
whose knowledge of history is as vague as
their hold on the Ten Commandments have
got the notion in a crude way and endeavor
to put it into practice. The nation must get
inside the toga somehow with all its restric-
tions and its scheduled and ordered creases
or things can’t go right.
The attempted application of the idea is
at the root of most national failures. Ihe
make and shape of manhood varies and the
shape of nationhood must vary to fit its
manhood. The Greek tunic —even if it did
run to ballet skirts —was all right in its
place; so were the Celtic trousers; so was
the toga that made the Forum pure merino.
But the garments were not interchangeable.
Athens could n ver have been Rome, nor
could Rome ever have laid aside its dignity
and risen to th 2 frivolous mental exaltation
of Athens. Examine the history of the
English progi iss towards nationhood in the
light of Rom.Ji method and thought. Rome
early caught the idea that every citizen
owed a du y to every other citizen and
must support him in peace and war. The
natural outcome of that idea was a road by
which to reach the other citizen. Roads
and Romt were forever linked together. How
did that idea affect the development of Eng-
land? The Anglo-Saxon came to a country
where fine, straight, paved roads ran in
every direction; but their straightness and
utility merely annoyed him. He made no
effort to keep them in order; and in the
course of a century or two they were
covered so deep in mud and dead vegetation
and forgotten cats that their very course
was unknown. Thereafter the rising Eng-
lish nation drove its carts along twisted lanes
of rut and bog, and lost time and sweat and
horses in the process. It lost more than
that. Village communities that were part
and parcel of the nation and one with it in
all vital interests were allowed to die of
famine during years of plenty because there
were no roads by which they could be
reached. Yet the English mind saw no
more need for reviving the old Roman system
than do the minds of the Sydney suburban
aldermen in charge of the goat tracks of
Mosman and Cremorne. For a thousand
years England, as a nation, made no attempt
to construct a country thoroughfare of other
material than dust or mud ; and when John
Loudon McAdam, after proving in Ireland
that the thing could be done, brought a
satisfactory road-making system to the land
of ruts and bog-holes, he had a big job to
convince Englishmen that there was any
need for the thing. Yet roads, in the Roman
view, were important creases in the toga of
nationhood; and their straightness was not
merely a matter of fashion but something
of almost religious importance. England
couldn’t wear, a toga like that, though, even
in her roadless condition, she had already
climbed ahead of the best Roman effort at
some points. The fact was she had largely
adopted Celtic trousers —individualistic gar-
ments allowing separate action to each limb.
The Roman model was not for her; and
anybody who fancies that a suit of clothes
wliich proved too restricted for Britain would
sit nicely on Australian hips and shoulders
has still a number of things to learn.
* * * *
It is quite true that Australia at the
present moment has a curious political like-
ness to the Roman republic about the time
it cracked up and became a,n empire.
Marcus Antonius Hughes has a super-
ficial resemblance to the other person who
lost his heart at sight of an eastern queen,
much as he himself lost his head at the
smile of the English duchesses. Lepidus
Cook still believes he has a say in the triple
division of leadership; and Octavian
Tudor fences deftly and plans for the
empire which he feels sure will slip into
his hands. It is not so much that the men
themselves and the parties are like those
in the Roman struggle, but that the Aus-
tralian democracy is standing at gaze much
as the democracy of Italy did, and watching
its own power being stolen away by the
contestants. For all that, the toga is not
the sort of garment Australia can wear.
Hughes’s yell to us to get the nicely-creased
and folded thing over our shoulders makes
no real appeal. We do not understand
nationhood presented to us in his terms; and
it is tolerably evident that we never shall
understand it. What we do understand is
that the free people of Australia are getting
a bad deal at the hands of small politicians
whose desire to cling to office outweighs all
their regard for the public good. What we
understand, too, is that under pretence
of prosecuting our share of the war
effectively those small politicians have kept
us so much in the dark in matters vital to
Australia that our democracy has become , a
mere farce. And, over and above all this,
we have firmly grasped the truth that our
share of the war has not been prosecuted
efficiently, but that shameful muddle has
marked the from the start.
Of what use is it to us to bleat about putting
on the ancient and amazing Roman toga?
What we need at this juncture is the strong
man who will arise and say in the plainest
of plain Australian : “Lace your boots! The
small politicians are standing between you
and the Kaiser, and the time has come to
kick !”
THE IMPORTATION OF YANKS TO
CATCH BEETLES.
This paper knows nothing about Illing-
worth, an entomologist who is to be paid
£lOOO a year by the Federal and Banana-
land Governments for taking charge of an
entomological bureau at Cairns, except that
he is an American. But it does know that
the circumstances of his appointment are a
disgrace to both Governments. The facts
are that when the Federal Government was
carrying on the negotiations with Bananaland
which ended in the sugar crop of 1916 being
commandeered. Hughes, hearing about the
damage caused by the cane-beetle, offered to
subsidise the State if it would extend its
efforts to get rid of the pest. In order to
provide further funds the growers agreed
to contribute 3d. per ton on the cane
produced. Being thus armed with additional
cash to get rid of, the Bananaland Ministry
decided that the first step taken should be
to make an importation, acting apparently
upon the principle that no Australian scien-
tist is worth considering for. a job carrying
wages in excess of those paid to a laborer.
All this has been done at a time when the
atmosphere has been full of windy talk
about “giving encouragement to science in
Australia” and establishing a Federal Coun-
cil of Science and Industry, and after
degrees in science, equal in value to any in
the world, have for years been conferred by
Australian universities. Whoever may be
directly responsible for carrying to its logical
conclusion the policy, distinctive of Aus-
tralia, of starving scientific research and
bemoaning the absence of scientific effort in
industry, is maintaining his anonymity,
though Lennon, the Bananaland Minister
for Agriculture, seems to have had the
administrative handling of the business. But
neither of the Ministries concerned can
escape collective censure for this contemptu-
ous treatment of Australians. Not only is
an American surreptitiously appointed to a
position from which Australians were barred,
but Lennon has been unctuously talking
about his “hope that Illingworth would
bring with him several promising students
who might later on prove of considerable
assistance,” and condescendingly observing
that “facilities would be given to local
youths who wished to study entomology.”
Are there no promising students in Australia
already? No well-eauipped scientific
workers? No local youths already anxious
to specialise ■ in zoology, and with oppor-
tunity to do so under masters of equal
calibre to Illingworth ? This appointment
is a slap in the face for every Australian
scientist and student, and another illustra-
tion of the promptitude with which the
mostly-imported politician can be relied upon
to bang the door against the native-born.
On the sugar plantations at Hawaii
where Illingworth has been working,
some successful results have followed from
experiments in parasitology—and many
negative results. Enormous sums of money
have been expended in research and in print-
ing copious reports. But there is no reason
to suppose that a man from Hawaii will
solve the problem of how to establish a per-
manent moratorium for cane-beetles in
Bananaland any more readily than one
drawn from Australia —or even one acquired
through the Imperial Bureau of Entomology
in London. What there is reason to believe
is that if Australian Governments could
bring themselves to adopt a less niggardly
attitude towards scientific work carried on
by Australians, even the political mind would
find proof that there is ample material
within the Commonwealth to make importa-
tions unnecessary. Private undertakings
have many times discovered this, and scien-
tific men have learned from bitter
experience that the most . close-fisted,
unimaginative patron of science in the Com-
monwealth is a Government. The whole
public policy of the country is a warning to
every young man against making scientific
study his life’s work, or, if he does, to pro-
ceed somewhere else and do it. In that case
he might some day hear of an Australian
Ministry being about to make a decent sort
of appointment, and at least have a chance
of applying for it. The last beetle “expert”
imported from America remained four years
and produced a report of four pages! Then
he returned to the States with his knowledge.
When an entomologist was appointed, in
Papua a year ago the first that Australians
heard about the matter was from English
newspapers. This importing disease is get-
ting: into the nation’s bones.
* * * *
The blunder of the Federal Government in
this business is inexcusable. It should be a
formula in a case involving Commonwealth
expenditure for professional service that no
money would be forthcoming to meet. the
appointment of an outsider unless the Minis-
try was satisfied that there was no Aus-
tralian capable of taking the job. If a
carpenter was imported from Chicago to
build a monkey-cage or something there
would be a yell loud enough to scare the in-
habitants of Mars. The cane-beetle problem
is upon the same plane. It comes within
the ordinary scope of entomology, like dozens
of other problems relating to insect life; and
if Australia is going to deny the competence
of the men trained in her schools and labora-
tories to apply their knowledge and skill to
Australian problems and test their patience
and initiative in research, what possible hope
can there be for the future of science 111 the
Commonwealth? Would it not mean that
all the facile speech-making of the hour and
the scholarships at universities and all the
rest of it were parts of an elaborate sham.
Whether the Yank and his “promising
students” emerge victorious from then
struggle with the Cairns beetle is not the
test of whether their appointment is wise or
foolish. Science is not business. Ihe
Yankees may fail and yet be most earnest
and capable specialists in their particular
field. The point of the thing is that the
selection of a man outside Australia is
poisonous in its very nature. Instead ot
there being this- malignant differentiation
against Australian scientists there should be
readiness to utilise their talent, and pride m
having them on the premises. Science knows
no boundary of race or country, but it can
get no footing in a land which treats its
scientific workers as aliens. And so far as
The Bulletin is aware, there is no place
on earth where this has been done so system-
atically as in Australia.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Fury of Despair.
The British calm in the face of the new
German policy of super-frightfulness is not
hard to understand. The first outburst o
submarining was effectively stopped by tne
British navy. Germany countered by buiia-
ing larger underwater craft and going a ,.
again. The result has been pretty bad 5
the naval authorities have assured us that
- this also will be stopped. It is at the yer
hour when such stoppage is about due that
the Teuton announces to the world ms into
tion of playing the mad dog. Brl J ai °
answer is practically to the effect that
was playing it anyhow, and that a mue
more or less foaming doesn t alt
balance of things to the extent of four P^ e ‘
We can only guess what the German m
may be in officially and with all due s . g
effects proclaiming that the I\a iser s seamen
will henceforth go blindly raging acio ,
seas with no consideration for friend :
It is either a vital turn in the comedy o


Despair or a confident move on the part of
over-reaching ambition still bombastically
playing the tragedy of Victory. If the
latter, it means that Germany is so sure of
winning that she wants to bring all the
world into the fight to heighten the glory of
her success; if the former, it means that she
is so fearful of speedy and crushing defeat
that she will risk any crime or enormity on
the bare chance of turning the tide. All
through the war there has been a lot of bad
guessing on both sides; but the evidence at
present does not point to German confidence.
A great moral question is, however, involved
in the new Teuton departure from even its
former pretence of virtue. Already it has
been shown that Germany has no respect
for treaties; now it is being proved that she
has no regard for those fundamental and
unwritten principles of humanity which
alone have made civilisation possible. Can
a people with such views be safely allowed
to exist as a nation? It is a grave problem.
Kindly white men, with no feeling other
than that of pity, were compelled to delib-
erately exterminate the Apache Indians of
America because they were ruthless mur-
derers who could neither keep faith nor
understand mercy. No one wants to exter-
minate Germans as individuals; but the wis-
dom of ever making peace with a nation
capable of the suoer-frightfulness now out-
lined is very problematical. Possibly it is
a good thing that the Teuton has decided to
do his worst. It will enable some future
peace conference to understand the thing it
has to deal with : and even the British cocoa
conscience will hardly have qualms about
putting some restriction on the wild beast
that dripped innocent blood from all its
claws.
Territorial Spoils.
The statement of a British Cabinet Min-
ister that the colonies taken by force from
the Germans will not be restored is the
brightest ray of light shed on the war situa-
tion _ for a very long time. On their view
of his measure, it is now evident that they
have come to the conclusion that they can
lick him sufficiently to dictate terms of
peace. That is the only thing Walter
Long’s declaration can mean. As far as
the German colonies are concerned, none of
them are colonies in the sense that they are
populated by Germans and filled with Ger-
man homes; and as they merely represent
so much territory to exploit, it is fitting
enough that they should be part of the fine
inflicted on-tlie Teuton for his international
crimes. At the same time their possession
by Britain must enormously increase the
burden of nigger which that country already
carries; and the prospect of the future load
might stagger the broadest-shouldered nation.
Maoriland’s Prime Minister has already
stated his intention of hanging on to Samoa ;
but, then, Maoriland likes the control of
brown brother and makes the business a
comparative success. It is the territory
seized by Australia that fills us with the
greatest misgiving. The Australian ideal
has been a Commonwealth—not an empire.
Our problems are big enough on this con-
tinent without adding to them; and terri-
torial extension, with three-fourths of our
own area undeveloped, looks like merely
adding to the height of our difficulties. At
the same time, unless Britain took those
territories over from us, they might fall
into the possession of any one of half-a-
dozen Powers which at some far future date
might make strategic use of them for attack
on Australia. We seem to be somewhere
between the Devil and the vasty deep on
the question. Must we waste our small
national strength forever hanging on to
country which we cannot effectively occupy
for the sake of other country which we
might develop only for that waste of
strength? The fact is, Providence put far
too many islands in the Pacific.
•
The Waste in the Defence Department.
It is admitted now by the Department
itself that, in addition to the £66,000 which
one man, it says, has succeeded in embez-
zling. a very large sum has been stolen from
the Naval side. Even if the persons thus
far charged with the crimes are not guilty,
enough has been disclosed to indicate tlie
awful chaos of the Pay Office. The confu-
sion and muddle there are indeed absolutely
indescribable. And that has been its condi-
tion throughout. Right back in the days
when The Bulletin was still muzzled,
proof, came to it that, because the pay-
sheet of a camp had been rendered in dupli-
cate, some at any rate of the men had
been paid twice. It had proof that the
pay of one portion of the service had been
collected by an entirely different portion,
and that the first lot was left without money
• —some of the men actually stranded. But
while the chaos in the Pay Office has pro-
bably been worse than it is anywhere else,
the whole Defence organisation, so far as it
relates to the handling of money, has long
been a scandal shrieking for inquiry. On
the Works side hundreds of thousands of
pounds have been as utterly wasted as if
the money had been thrown into the sea.
Take the Warren, for instance, for some
time past the Artillery camp in N. S.
Wales, but now handed back to its private
owners. Thousands of pounds have been
spent there, on this private property,* rented
only from week to week—spent on buildings
and fences, etc., and at the very moment
when one lot of officers were arranging to
leave it another lot were considering a
scheme, which had practically been decided
upon, to spend nearly a thousand pounds
upon drainage. It is almost impossible to
dig anywhere into the operations of this
Department without discovering appalling
waste. The Pay Office is awful; the Works
side almost as shocking. But the tragedy
does not end there. Tens if not hundreds
of thousands of pounds’ worth of thoroughly
good equipment has been sent to the garbage-
tip and the destructor because there was
no formal authority to send it to a cleaner.
Australia has a right to have these things
inquired into. It must pay the bill pre-
sently ; and the longer the awful waste con-
tinues the more handicapped we shall be
in lighting this war to a finish. There are
two men at least from whom the country
is entitled to have a full and frank state-
ment of their experiences. Brig.-Generals
Ramaciotti and Williams will leave their
commands in N. S. Wales and Victoria next
week. Both are citizen soldiers; both are
business men. Both have been for 12 months
inside the awful machine. They have been
Australia’s servants there, and Australia is
entitled to know from them what that
machine is like. They will not. of course,
tell Australia unless they are placed under
formal compulsion to do so. Their patriot-
ism can then he relied upon to do the
rest. A commission ought to be appointed
to hear the truth from these two officers
and from anybody else who knows any-
thing about the management or misman-
agement of the Department. The in-
quiry should be confined to the business
administration for the present. On the
military side there have been scandals, too;
but they must wait. If Pearce is not
afraid of what an inquiry can produce, he
will welcome it. If be blocks it, or fails to
authorise it, then the Senate should be heard
from.

Poor Old Ma in a Spider’s Web.
There are nine-and-ninety ways of railing
the wind, and the Holman Government is
learning and practising them all. With the
sublime assurance of the confidence man
Holman confesses, almost boasts, how, in
the battle of wits, the Norton Griffiths
syndicate was beaten :—•
During the first two years of the contract’s cur-
rency it was agreed that £6,000,000 should be
raised, and also that the same sum should be spent
with the firm upon public works. The advantage
which the Government got was the guarantee of
this financial group that the money should be forth-
coming. This has been done. The guarantee has
been carried out. The £6,000,000 has been obtained.
On the other hand, the Government, instead of
carrying out its obligations to the firm, and giving
it £6,000,000 worth of work to do, has given it
only a very much smaller sum’s worth.
So the Government has collared some of
the money which Norton Griffiths raised
in London and has used it for odl jobs with
which the syndicate has had nothing to do.
But, though that may have been smart, it
merely postpones the evil day ; for it throws
upon the Government the obligation of finding
an equal sum of money outside Norton Grif-
fiths in the future. No matter what hap-
pens now, no matter what the future may
hold, the Government has thus put itself in
this position: it must make good the money
which it has—well, we will call it diverted —
or pay the syndicate compensation. If it
was in the toils before, the State has been
thus put still deeper into the mesh. It said
originally to Norton Griffiths : “If you
can succeed in borrowing £10,000,000 for us
you shall have the spending of it.” Now
it says: “i\o matter what we have to pay
for the money, we will get you £10,000,000
to spend.” The original agreement looked
just about as bad as an agreement could be.
With almost superhuman- ingenuity' the
“National” Government has shown that it
is possible to make one that is still worse.
Surely the “National” party must see how
completely this puts the State in the hands
of the syndicate; and surely it will speak
plainly and tell these reckless Ministers that
it will not suffer one penny more of this
Norton Griffiths money to be misappropri-
ated ! That the Norton Griffiths syndi-
cate doesn’t object is neither here nor there.
Why should it object? It knows that it is
not going to lose anything. It is in the
position of a moneylender who doesn’t care
two straws how his money is wasted so
long as there is a good name on the back
of the bill. He leaves the worrying to the
man who owns that name; and in this case
the name is N. S. Wales.
What the German Submarine Has
Done for Us.
About the most interesting exhibit at the
A.N.A. exposition in Melbourne is that of
mineral earths with which this island is so
plentifully endowed. Here is-manufactured
talc or soapstone from Heathcote (Vic.),
commonly known as French chalk, a com-
modity used in enormous quantities in a mul-
titude of industries. Here are kaolin, silica,
lime carbonates, ochres, iron oxides, in-
fusorial earth, gypsum, magnesite, alabaster,
barytes and fluor spar. Soecimens of commer-
cial products simply and easily made from
these minerals and earths are shown rang-
ing from alabaster casts down to Epsom
salts,_ and including plaster of paris, common
whiting, putty, ceiling ornaments, ■ telegraph
insulators, bricks to stand 4000 deg. of heat,
impalpable powder for nitro-glycerine, adul-
terants for leather, cloth and silk. Naturally
one asked when the factory which produced
these things was opened, and the reply came.
“Only since the war.” “But' why?”
“Because we had to stop shipping the raw
material to Germany” ! That meant closing
quarries and writing-off capital with the
alternative of working up these valuable
earths on the spot. So they opened the fac-
tory, and can undersell the importers who
are for the moment choked by dear freight
and high insurance. Given a proper Protec-
tive tariff, and there is no need ever to
import either putty or Epson! salts or many
other such things. But when the war is
over we shall be in precisely the same posi-
tion as we were before it started. We didn’t
have the factory then, and we should not
have had it now only for the Protection
which the German submarine imposes
on us. If we want to save that fac-
tory and dozens more we must supply some-
thing to take the place of the abnormal
freights, etc., which are protecting our in-
dustries for the time being; and the only
thing which can take its' place is a high
tariff—a sky-high tariff. Before the war
ends we threaten to have a public debt of
between £500,000,000 and £000,000.000. This
handful of people in Australia has to raise
not only the interest on that amount but a
tremendous annual sum for war pensions;
and it will perform that feat only in travail
unless it saves every saveable penny. We
can’t any longer afford to send away the
money to pay somebody else to make even
our packet of salts.

Joanna Bull’s Vote.
Women’s franchise of a sort appears to have
arrived in Britain. The Speaker’s Electoral
Reform Conference proposes that every
woman on the local government register and
the wife of every man thereon shall have a
vote? provided she has reached the age of 30
or 35. Women graduates of universities are
also recommended for a place on the rolls.
It is a poor “reform”; and the suffragette
view of it that it has merely complicated
things and increased existing inequalities
has good justification. Britain started out
with an insistence on large property qualifi-
cations for male voters, and gradually broad-
ened the franchise until most men had a
vote. The broadening process directly
benefited the people as a whole, and im-
proved the tone and tendency of legislation ;
and only a very dull person would now argue
that a restricted franchise was ever needed
or that legislation was not delayed by it.
The British political person, however, is
very dull; hence the suggestion that female
franchise should go through all the revisions
which marked male franchise, when, obvi-
ously, better results would be achieved by
granting electoral rights to all women.
People may make out a case why women
should not vote at all; but no One is entitled
to the view that Eleanob should go to the
polling place and Jane shouldn’t. The wife
of a man who owns a little property, or who
in virtue of that gets on the roll, is not
likely to know any more about political
issues than the woman who earns her living
in an office —or a factory, for that matter.
In many cases she knows a- lot less, and is
the inferior of the wage-earning person in
education, character and most things else.
Yet an arbitrary line is drawn which must
cause a lot of class feeling, and is of no
benefit to the nation. Still, England does
things like that. While a political war is
raging round the extension of the female
franchise a lot of other reforms must be held
up; and to hold up reforms is the great
business of English party politics.

One cry that stirred the eaters to wild
enthusiasm at the A.N.A. luncheon in Mel-
bourne was uttered by A. E. Seedsman in
proposing the toast of the State Ministry.
“Our hospitals are languishing, whilst right
at hand the Government has a means to help
them. Why not adopt the totalisator ?” The
speaker brought down the house, but he
didn’t bring any sort of reply from the
vociferous Peacock when that most evasive
of Premiers came to talk of everything save
local politics. Joe Cook, however, saw a
chance to be sopbistically satirical. Seeds-
man had remarked that, as he was not a
punter himself, the totalisator would not get
any of his money. Whereupon Joseph
alluded to him as a typical citizen who
favored taxation for other people and exemp-
tion for himself. Thus do politicians habitu-
ally distort the truth to make their little
joke. The totalisator taxes nobody. It
merely takes over from a mob of parasite
bookmakers their business of selling odds on
horse-races, and having taken over the busi-
ness it reduces the cost of the gambling to
those of the public who gamble. There is no
taxation for anyone to be exempt from, as far
as this change of odds-layers is concerned. The
new machine displaces the old established
robber for the benefit of the “investing” pub-
lic. One may fairly argue that Rechabites
get an unfair advantage from the taxes
levied on exhilarating liquors. But the
abstainer from betting has only an infinitesi-
mal financial benefit to gain by a Govern-
ment tax on totalisator revenue. And, instead
of being taxed, as the consumers of Derrink
are, the consumers of odds gain appreciably
by Government installation of a quiet, re-
spectable machine in place of a noisy, dis-
respectable body of bookmakers.
“Rio": As the N. S. Wales Nation il Rifle
Association is talking about a prize meeting
this year (the Government to finance it, of
course) I want to ask:—
If a man is too old, ill, important or married to
go to the war, why on earth should the Government
finance his Saturday-afternoon pastime and call it
patriotism ?
How many battles in the present war have been
won by long-range rifle-fire of the slow, lying-down
variety practised by rifle clubs?
How many by even the rapid, short-range fire of
the military-trained shot?
How many by the pick and shovel, bomb, bayonet
and “condition” ?
If l'ifleclub -men shoot for their country’s s;ike, why
do they not also do a bit of navvying, homhing and
baj-oneting ?
Are not the qualities developed by, say, Rugby
football more useful in a modern war tjian those
developed by rifle-shooting?
Have not a larger percentage of football-club
members enlisted than rifle-club members?
Why does not the Government subsidise football
with free grounds, caretakers, goals, footballs* etc.,
as it does riflemen with ranges, targets, rifles,
ammunition, etc.? And, in addition, give them a
large cash grant for prizes out of the taxpayer's
pocket ?
This cant about present-day riflemen shoot-
ing in order to learn how to defend their
country makes me tired. Ninety prv cent, of
the men firing would be unavailable in war.
They go shooting for the same reason that
others take up bowls or golf. It is a
pleasant, open-air, non-strenuous sport re-
tpiiring a modicum of skill, with most of
the expenses provided by a bulldozed tax-
payer, and with a nice chance of picking up
a few quids at the annual prize-meeting.

How deep has the dry rot eaten into the
Labor crowd! At a special conference of
N. S. AVales unions to devise ways and
means of getting work, the leaders of the
movement called upon the Parliamentarians
to go further into debt to the capitalist and
wade deeper into the bog of interest. An
addendum from the Rubber Workers’
delegate to prohibit unnecessary importa-
tions was ruled out, as it “would compli-
cate matters!” In 1915 the rubber indus-
try had over £1,000,000 invested, employed
22114 hands in 26 factories, and produced
goods worth £1,076,263. Rut in the same
year Australia imported rubber goods worth
£1.441,003 —mostly from German-Americans.
Raw rubber was shipped from Ceylon and
the Federated Malay States to London, from
there to the U.S., then back again to Aus-
tralia. At the present time 600 rubber-
workers are busy hunting for unskilled
workers’ jobs. With adequate Protection
the rubber people were prepared to get the
raw rubber required from New Guinea and
the Federated Malay' States (50 per cent,
of the rubber that the U.S. digests goes
from Ceylon and the Malay Statesl, to work
round the clock, to employ another 2000
hands and train them and make them effici-
ent rubber workers. The offer aroused no
interest —it was merely listened to. These
shiftless loan-eaters are now crying for
£0,000,000 of someone else’s money in order
that they may not starve. And last year
they supplied others, mostly foreigners, with
work worth £64,000,000.

In an earnest appeal to the nation to
grapple with the venereal plague the Eng-
lish Review (December) gives a warning
which the warward-bound Australian ought
to pin in his hat: —
The unpleasant truth is that never lias there been
so wide-spread a syphilitic taint in London as at
the present hour. In London there is no control
of prostitutes. . . .and war has broken down the
conventional sex barriers, so that men accost women
openly and meet with an easy response; in fact,
the whole face of London has changed in this con-
nection and now every girl has her soldier and
many of them several soldiers. Then there is the
flapper phenomenon, so that it is not surprising
that the flapper on the f-treets has ended in the
flapper syphilitic, who goes about infecting the
soldiers by the dozen....ln the town centres their
[the soldiers’] ranks are being far more than deci-
mated.
In this matter the first thing to be done
is to control prostitution and penalise un-
professional prostitution; and if it doesn’t
owe it to itself to do this, England at least
owes it to the Australians who are ordered
to England to train. “The disease is more
prevalent here than in Egypt,” an officer
writes to The Bulletin from London, “and
it is unchecked. I should say that at least
one-eighth of our men are already affected
more or less—mostly less, I believe, for the
type is certainly not as bad as it was in
Egypt.” In this matter England is not
playing the game with us.

old trouble between George Black and the
three imported skippers on the three im-
ported N.S.W. State trawlers will end in a
maritime hold-up. When the foreigners
arrived in Sydney, Black, as a good union-
ist, told them to join a union. They de-
murred ; Black insisted, and they obeyed.
Later on the Minister had a tiff with them
over the 1100th application for more pay
for somebody on the fish boats. In the ensu-
ing disturbance the Chief Sec. found him-
self up against his own joss. .Tust as things
were getting heated the political kaleido-
scope shifted and Black made way for
Fuller. The latter flatly refused to have
anything to do with the skippers, and laid
up his fleet. He has now got them going
again with scratch crews, but unionism is
on the warpath. The Marine Engineers, a
powerful body, has been prevailed on to
call out its members from the tubs, and
Fuller has been threatened that there’s
more to follow if the imported barracoutas,
whom Black forced into a union, are not
put back on the bridge. As a back-hander
Beeby. Minister for Labor, has moved to
cancel the registration of the Masters and
Engineers’ Association, to which body all
the trouble is attributed. It has been sus-
pected recently .that the Government de-
sires a trial of strength with militant
unionism, and if Labor fights there is the
chance of a big row. For which God be
praised! We haven’t had an industrial row
for nearly a week !

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miles Standisii stirs. Smiling, he long
endured
The insult of the universal foe.
Men deemed his ancient valor curbed and
cured
Hy gold that round him seethed to surge and
flow.
They mocked a rugged manhood run to seed,
With “bull” and “bear” replacing faith and
deed.
Miles Standisii stirs. The trumpet at his
lips
May send a shattering music down the
world,
Till, in the red flame of his battleships,
The banners of uneasy shame are furled.
As freedom’s foremost champion once he
strode;
World-harvests ripened from the blood he
sowed.
Miles Standisii stirs. Twice in his might
he rose
When all a carping world believed him dead ;
And twice, before his truth, his broken foes
Shuddered to hear his army’s mighty tread
Moving with awful purpose to its end —•
A staff of fire that might not break nor
bend.
Miles Standisii stirs. The large world
holds its breath.
■Trimly the Teuton smiles behind his guns.
Xot yet his power, enhanced by shame and
death,
lias met the stern, unyielding faith that
' runs
In long tradition from grey Plymouth
Rock....
Does it run still? The world awaits the
shock.

The Australian Farmers’ Opportunity.
Farmers’ insurance business is highly profitable. It helps to provide insurance
company shareholders with handsome dividends. And mostly these shareholders
have little further interest in the welfare of the farmer. In fact some are merely
investors who live thousands of miles from Australia.
If farmers’ insurance is so profitable, why should not the Australian farmer
himself secure the profits from his business? With that object in view, an
insurance co. founded on the best co-operative basis made its appearance a
couple of years ago.
S Settlers’ Co-operative Insurance Co. of A
This is THE Farmers’ Insurance Co.
A farmer Avho becomes a shareholder and places his insurances with this co. participates in the profits which his business creates.
Over 1000 farmers are already shareholders. The profits from the first year’s operations enabled the co. to pay shareholders a DIVIDEND
OF SIX PER CENT.

The German Dive Under Water.
After everything appropriate has been said
about German frightfulness and ’he barbar-
isation of war, which is a barbarous busi-
ness anyway, and the iniquity of the treat-
ment promised by Berlin to neutrals, the
best thing to do is have a look at the. job
on hand. It is with torpedoes and mines,
not with threats, that the German intends
to try to sweep the seas. And it is
by sinking his ships, not by making speeches,
that he will be prevented from doing it. If
the submarine can isolate Britain: then Ger-
many will not lose tlx, Such an
achievement would be VqdTvalent to the
destruction of the North Sea fleet.. Can
the Germa’n succeed? The-answer is hid-
den behind the days to come. In his favor
there is the very important fact that the pos-
sibilities of the submarine have been well
tested and that this latest enterprise lias
been long prepared for. On the other side
great encouragement to hope that the Ger-
man will be beaten at his own game is
to be found in the magnitude of the
preparations made to entertain him, in the
success of armed merchantmen at evading
disaster, the unplesantness handed out to
Zeppelins, and the calamity which overtook
the first submarine campaign by the enemy.
But that the shadow is dark is beyond dis-
nute. If the navy fails —good night. It
the German fails it may be good night for
him —quickly. For our encouragement it
is a fair thing to say that there are all the
indications, of despair in his last move. Fvery
line of the recent communication to W ash-
ington seemed to have been written by a
Hun conscious that his was
hard-pressed. Meanwhile Australia is battling
on, liopine- that even if sufficient reinforce-
ments are not despatched to help the army
fight, someone or other will be despatched to
help Lloyd George talk. In the view of
most of the daily papers and of all the
politicians it is infinitely more important to
recruit the spruikers than the fighters.
Edward J. Turton, who died at Sydney
the other day, had been 49 years in the
wool and hides trade, lie was a son ot that
S H. Turton who in the ’fifties was head-
master of Christ Church parish school,
founded by old-time merchant I. S. Mort.
The youngster went from the school into
Mort’s office and learnt the ABC of the
wool trade, passing thence to become a
partner with his old schoolmate, 1 .
Winchcombe, in the same line of business.

On the High Court bench Justice Isaacs
has been unable to agree with the majority
on 37 occasions in six years. In
he jarred his brother wigs 11 times. His
Honor has fought like a good Australian
for a liberal interpretation of the Constitu-
tion ; but Chief Justice Griffith is hard to
shift from the narrow path. The C.J. has
dissented on eight occasions on which the
more democratic interpretation of law has
prevailed—all in recent years, since Isaacs
has been joined by Rich and Duffy. Barton
his differed 10 times from the prevailing
opinion; generally lie has sided with the
Chief.
Henry St. George, whose death in London
is reported by cable, was well known in
Australia as examiner for one of the English
musical bodies to which this country un
necessarily pays thousands of pounds in fees
every year. He was an all-round musician,
though, like most of the moderns, lie had no
ambition to look the part. The fiddle was
his pet instrument, but he was also com-
poser, authority on the instruments of the
past, and a voluminous writer. He was a
charming companion, and only about o 0
when the end came.
“Six-and-Eight” makes a correction: —
The late Mrs. Emily Paul’s husband, Colonel
Alfred Paul, is still alive and residing in Bathurst.
He was the officer entrusted by N. S. Wales in pre-
Federation days with the establishment of the Public
School Cadet force, and the scheme he adopted was
practically identical with that for compulsory mili-
tary service outlined by Kitchener for the Australian
Government.

“Fair and Warmer,” by Avery Ilopwood,
which an imported English company pre-
sented for the first time at Sydney Criterion
on Saturday, is a great moral lesson in two
acts and a bedroom. There is nothing in it
that would shock the most hardened
debauchee: the bed is not even a twin-bed.
It is a stern indictment of the heinous sin
of wowserism : it is a sermon in cocktails. It
is full of wholesome uplift that will bring a
little ray of hope into the dullness of those
who are happily married. Billy and Laura
Bartlett were happily married. Billy was the
perfect husband. He did not drink, he did
not smoke, and. worst of all, he was merely
“FAIR AND WARMER,” AT SYDNEY
CRITERION.
Harry Sweeney and Mrs. Bartlett (Daisy
Atherton) discover Mr. Bartlett making his
debut as a drinker.
“a one-woman man.” He never went out at
night looking like one thing to come home
next morning looking like another. He had
no yearnings for a harem; he really felt
that he couldn’t love 20 women; and his wife
always knew where he was. Naturally his
wife got tired of the perfect husband, and, in
that casual way they have in N’Yark, she
determined to divorce him. Jack Wheeler
was the other sort of husband, who had early
learnt the first lesson in matrimony—to keep
his little Blanche always guessing where he
was and when and how he would come home.
Jack converts the wowser to bis creed, and
when the patient Blanche and the perfect
wowser are left alone in the wowser’s flat
they make up their minds to be found in a
compromising position when the errant hus-
band and the flighty wife come home. But
it is harder than they think, until the happy
inspiration comes to them to mix a cocktail.
They don’t know anything about cocktails;
but apparently you can mix cocktails any-
how and the result will be satisfactory.
That cocktail and a bottle or two of cham-
pagne that follow are the first steps up-
wards to the better life. The amateur sinners
get delightfully drunk, and the compromising
situation which, in their unregenerate sober
moments they had vainly sought, arrives of
its own accord. Next morning there is The
Headache. But no great moral reform is
effected without suffering; and the converted
wife, discovered under the wowser’s bed,
knows that for the future she will always
keep her husband guessing; while the
wowser, caught kissing the maid, learns with
delight that his wife has no further desire to
divorce him. The drama is one that is to be
commended to all those who are hopelessly
happily-married. No film-play has more
earnestly denounced the immorality of the
husband who is perfect or the wife who is
always there when she is wanted.
A pleasing innovation in the presentation
of this farce is the use of the English lan-
guage. The play is set in New York, but the
company that so brilliantly interprets it uses
our own speech. And the new English
Comedy Company has been admirably chosen.
Thomas A. Braidon. the immaculate husband
who learns all there is to know about cock-
tails, proves himself a subtle and experienced
actor. His wowserism is almost as delight-
ful as his drunkenness. His partner in cock-
tails, Elma K.oyton, is the most charming
and convincing female drunk that this paper
has ever had the privilege of meeting. She
takes her liquor like a lady, and neatly
shows in the earlier scenes the pathetic and
deplorable innocence of the devoted wife.
The contrast between her innocence and her
alcoholic exuberance is cleverly indicated.
Percy Marmont, the errant husband, is a
finished actor with a taking personality.
Daisy Atherton, the wife of the wowser,
manages a somewhat difficult part with
success; and May Nightingale does the same
with the obvious part of a flirtatious maid.
Harry Sweeney, the harmless but necessary
villain, is well placed. The only really
unmoral part of the show are the awful
color-effects of the furniture.
This fairly-warm morality play is evi-
dently booked for a long season of uplift.

The Taits plunge into the musical-comedy
trade on Saturday at the redecorated Sydney
Palace. “Very Good Eddie” is the first pro-
duction by a new company headed by Barry

12 months in New York and wore out 183
pairs of boots.
Sydney Tivoli is once more wearing plain
vaudeville; and, on the whole, it seems to
be the Tivoli’s best suit. Australian Fred
Bluett, back again after four lucky years
in England, sings at the head of the bill
and makes a profit in his own country by
virtue of the added credit he won by getting
away from it. Sydney baritone Malcolm
McEachern supplies an artistic turn, for
which everyone seems to be properly grate-
ful ; and Marie King Scott, -with suspenders
showing, obliges again with her quaintly-
humorous turn. Lady Sen Mei (singer),
Togos (Jap jugglers), and Belle Oliver are
also among those present.
