Carnival is now a consistent feature of
the White City, Sydney’s most attractive
playground, and the master of the revels is
hard put to it thinking out new schemes
for joy-nights. The Bulletin passes him
one from Nice—the battle of flowers, which
ought really to be adopted by Sydney itself.
“The Bells,” which is a very lurid little
melodrama, never looked more lurid than at
Melbourne Princess’s last Saturday. Allan
Wilkie’s behavidr at the end of each act
was rather more conventionally theatrical
than the old business associated with the
name of Irving—business in which 11.8.,
who played “The Bells” at Her Majesty’s
some five years ago, gave a precise imitation
of his more impressive parent. Blit, for all
its violent insistence on the hysteria of the
fearstruck Burgomaster, the drama that
Leopold Lewis translated from the French is
about the neatest and most concise of the
many dream-plays that have survived. It is
free from non-essentials. The minor comedy
characters give tbe atmosphere of crude pro-
vincial jollity in a snow-bound village. And
the dramatist keeps one interested always in
his main idea, viz., that a shrewd, ill-educated
man with a murder on his mind could, after
seeing people mesmerised on a public plat-
form, become obsessed with a dread of being
himself forced by hypnotic influence to own
up to his crime of long ago. Mathias was
fear-stricken,- not conscience-stricken. He
was not haunted by unpleasant recollections
until he had seen other respectable citizens
put to sleep and heard them talk. Then he
began to behold visions and suffer from imag-
inary sounds of sleigh-bells, and at last he
•' icd a dream that frightened him to
death.
There is only one possible reading of the-
part. Wilkie in most places is as like the
Irvings as his countenance and elocution
permit. He hasn’t a flexible, melodious
voice, or a wide range of facial expression
But he makes his points artistically in the
first two acts, without laying undue stress
on his recognition of a piece of blood-money
among the coins saved up for his daughter’s
dowry, lie is powerful, but not so strikingly
picturesque as Irving was. In the dream
scene he fairly held the audience, despite a
rather foolish attempt to add realism to the
situation by introducing nightmare groans
from the bedroom where lie had previously
retired. With Wilkie acting on the stage'in
front of them it was evident to the audience
that he couldn’t possibly be the person mak-
ing a. noise in the bedroom at the side. Cos-
grove, who is only a little too loud and broad
in style, and I)ore, who is hardly broad
enough,- have the best of the minor charac-
ter parts. Y. Edwards is an amiable young
lover in an unsympathetic uniform, and Miss
Marie Ney is quite charming as the unim-
portant ingenue Annette.
“The Bells” is preceded by a one-act
comedy of the calm, matter-of-fact husband,
the hare-brained wife and the serio-comic
lover. It is probably of French origin,
bright in dialogue and conventionally arti-
ficial in plot. Miss Hunter-Watts, Hunt and
Dore seemed strange to the work on Satur-
day, but are doubtless doing better now.
Allen Doone, still exercising his blarney
at Melbourne King’s, scarcely challenges
comparison with Andrew Mack, who intro-
duced Erin’s tamest poet to Melbourne about
10 years ago. Nature had fattened Mack for
the role of Tom Moore, and had given him a
manner that, despite its theatricalism, was
all butter and blandishment. Doohe, on the
other hand, is disqualified from the start by
physical shortcomings. He gives the Irish
Laureate the contours of an ironing-board
and a personality to match, and seems about
the last to occupy the foreground in a
maiden’s tender thoughts. To be sure, he
achieves a sort of prominence, but it is
artificially contrived by the old trick of re-
ducing the other characters to the rank of
spectators and knocking them silly with
subtle badinage. As a matter of fact, none
of his' supporters have very much to do, bar-
ring Edna Keeley and Onslow Edgeworth.
And Edna amounts to no more than a pair
of bright eyes and a procession of peevish
exclamations. Occasionally, however, she
jostles Allan for the* limelight by suddenly
exposing the genius of her dressmaker. As
for Edgeworth, designed as the lady-killer,
Percival Lovelace, he only becomes an im-
potent exquisite by holding the mirror up to
himself and leaving Percival out of it alto-
gether. It is a pity that Percival had not a
stronger exponent, for he makes the play by
ordaining Edna to be the head-liner on his
list of houris, and moving Heaven, earth and

The panto, at Melbourne Majesty’s pro-
ceeds as in its early days, except that the
Demon of Despair is now danced by Miss
Sylvester instead of by Charles Albert.
Despair is the person who dogs the footsteps
of Maggie Dickinson for awhile, and even
goes so far as to push her down when the
rest of the ballet isn’t looking. Eventually,
however, dear little Maggie rises triumphant,
on her toes, and the principal boy discovers
the glittering casket, or what-not, and Fairy
Goodenougli waves her wand, and all is well.
It is hard to discover the exact meaning of
the ballet that begins in the Tulip Farm and
lasts through several changes of scene. It
seems a beautiful mystery whereof Maggie
Dickinson may have lost the key. Or per-
haps it is too poetic to have any coherence
to speak of.
At a show of home-grown art
Numerous Australian artists have something to
say in the big A.N.A. display at Melbourne Exhibi-
tion. F. R. Crozier, now at the B’ront, hangs a
topical subject—-a war-smitten interior entitled
“Aftermath.” It would require an auctioneer’s clerk
to catalogue the details, but principal features are
an elderly lady reading a letter and a wounded
warrior who seems to be sharing with the spectator
a feeling of perplexity as to what it’s all about.
Somewhat mystifying also are the dim allegories of
Miss Christian Yandell. She pictures her fancies
with a Baudelairean spread of gangrenous hues. It
is a pleasure to turn to the nicely-handled “Grand-
mother’s Gown” of Sergeant Charles Wheeler, and
there is honest painting in the ( femininely effusive
canvases of Miss Cumbrae Stewart. Miss Violet
Teague has her usual set of aristocratic-looking
ladies. Clearly Miss Teague’s prayer is: “Give us
this day our daily breed.” Her study of Archibald
Strong is much more human. She has caught the
Professor with his pedantry suppressed and a
birthday-party smile on his rectangular visage. Allan
Wilson is represented by a self-made portrait that
looks as though it was satisfied with its creator.
Percy Reason furnishes a pathetic view of John
McDouall Stuart almost lost in the sunburnt scenery.
F. H. Bruford puts his name to a large expanse
of wind and water, and Harry McClelland tries to
dispose of several acres of unreal estate. Ilis gaudy
“Golden Autumn” would keep a policeman awake.
There is also a gigantic “Township,” in which
practically everything but a tree and a horse appears
to be buried in the composition. Hal Waugh’s talent
flickers momentarily and disappears in two or three
little things of no great artistic value. The
diligent and clever Louis McCubbin applauds our
lovely landscape with a palette swimming in pea-
green. Montague Brown’s pretentious gumscape,
“Arbores Juvenes Patriae,” is merely a pile of
prospective firewood. C. E. S. Tindall’s water-colors
are dexterous but uninspiring, and E. Fraser's Eng-
lish and Belgian sketches are done in a tasteful
though academic manner. A Mt. Dandenong after-
noon shows W. Nicholls Anderson nearly at his best,
and a glimpse of Adelaide from Glen Osmond brings
Archibald Collins’s craftsmanship into notice. C.
Bowman Fletcher holds- his palette up to Meldrum
in a portrait. The signature is very fine. Cobb’s
etchings are again in considerable force, while among
the pastellists Miss Farr and Miss Wilson don’t
demonstrate any super-excellence in the graceful
medium. Miss Ethel Jackson-Morris, who has a
vein of fantasy, is revealing a tendency to introduce
a kindergarten class to the Raekham school. But
the worst stuff in the show is partially hidden away
in an annexe off the second gallery. The landscapes
in this section would be too dreadful to live with,
while, respecting two horribly butchered nudes, this
writer can only say that he has never seen anything
in which the art of assassination is so cleverly com-
bined with the assassination of art.
At Melbourne Royal they are about to
close “The House of Glass” and stage a
whimsical sentimental comedy, with no
crooks in it. Miss Florence Rockwell, the
leading lady, will take the name-part in “The
Misleading Lady.”
Annette Kellerman, divinely fair and
divingly audacious, is performing on the
screen at Melbourne Auditorium these times.
It is an odd-shaped fairy-story that Annette
figures in, and she mostly figures in attrac-
tive undress. Starting as the soul of a dicky
bird in a gilded cage, she becomes “A Daugh-
ter of the. Gods” in search of her soul-mate,
who turns out to be an estimable but
strangely unsatisfactory young prince;
Annette,, in fact, has bad luck all through
the 2 A hours of beautiful moving picture.
The mermaids of the sea do their best to give
her a good wet time, and the gnomes, who
are a heavily-whiskered community, clap
their hands when she dives from high places
into a gentle stream ; but they can’t change
her luck. A wicked old Sultan, inspired by
an ancient lady who travels on a broomstick,
and directed by the star lady of his harem,
orders' Annette to be burned at the stake,
thrown into a preserve of hungry crocodiles,
and drowned in the whirling rapids. This
last atrocity is evidently the most trying
ordeal that A Daughter of the Gods goes
through, for the foaming torrent treats her
as though she were a cork. Finally, she
leads a heroic army of converted gnomes
against the Sultan’s forces, and is slain by
her own best boy. The long film is admit-
tedly designed to interest children, in which
respect it comes as a change from the social-
sermon pictures dedicated to grown-ups only.
The Passing Show is to appear at Mel-
bourne Tivoli on Saturday and endeavor to
remove the feeding.of ennui caused by the
now-departed Follies. In the meanwhile
two or three of the Follies and some other
' people are filling in time cheerfully in a
mixed vaudeville programme. Eminent
among these fleeting entertainers is De
Forrest, a card king who commences his
slick act fro™ a seat in the stalls. Ella
Caspers, Goossens the Belgian, and Short
and Little reappear; Miss Lingarde discloses
her unclassical figure in classical poses;
while Rose and Dell do an acrobatic and
biking act made noisy by the infrequent
silence of Dell. Also there are a couple of
painful Amurkans who pool their meagre
vocal resources to the immense applause of
the ushers at the back of the house.
The Dandies are still at the old address
on St. Kilda (Vic.) Esplanade. At present
they are reviving their musical revue, “The
Seaside Hotel,” wherein Joseph Brennan
scores as a humorous pub porter, and Ford
Waltham is conspicuous as a beach pierrot.
In the first part Linda Ockleshaw’s dancing
continues to be a mild novelty without
amounting to an innovation. Also Lalla
Ward is a strong vocal asset as before. Last
week she was caught trespassing in “Love’s
Garden of Roses,” but she did it so nicely
that she will be discharged with a caution.
William Farnum is appearing in “The
Man from Bitter Roots” at Hoyt’s principal
Melbourne house this week. There is a
pretty girl-reporter in the tangled story, and
she furnishes a soft resting-place for Wil-
liam’s weary head after he has dodged a
fraudulent charge of homicide. Lionel Barry-
more is playing the strenuous triple role of
broker,. stoker and highwayman in the long-
distance special, “Dorian’s Divorce.” Oppo-
site Lionel is a new star, Grace Valentine,
with more natural aptitude for her job than
the average screen novice owns.
After the rejection of bis rolled-gold peace
proposals, the Kaiser sent a whirling message
to his Prussians telling them that they were
boiling with wrath arid filled with new
courage. After two and a half years of it
they would much rather be boiling with soup
and filled with new cabbage.
Abdul is not all bad: There are pictures
in circulation of orderly Gallipoli graveyards
showing that he has scrupulously kept his
promise that the mound above good old Bill-
jim shall not be desecrated. Around one of
the plots is a line of the same barbed wire
that, having checked the living in their
glorious rush at the Landing, now protects
the uncaring dead. The pictures were taken
since the evacuation, and there is evidence
that the graves have been not only preserved
but kept in order.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Ancient Prompter”: I have not seen
Harry Bracy’s age mentioned in any obit-
uary notice. Probably no one in Australia
knew it. He retained his slim, dapper
appearance almost to the last —a rather
unusual happening with tenors, who run, as a
rule, to fat. Yet it is all 45 years since
Bracy was an accepted light-opera artist in
London, where he sang the tenor roles in the
first English productions of “Madame
Favart,” “Olivette,” etc., with Florence St.
John, taking the town by storm in the leads.
Settling in Australia, Bracy developed into
a splendid all-round man under ,T. C. Wil-
liamson—operatic artist, stage-director, pro-
ducer and business-manager in turn. Never
was there a cooler and more imperturbable
stage-boss. On one of the rare occasions
when his equanimity was disturbed he was
producing “Madame Butterfly,” and rather
upset the Italian conductor by breaking up
the old-fashioned seini-circle of the chorus
into picturesque groups. The maestro wanted
the choristers where they could all follow
his beat. “Mr. Bracy,” lie exposulated, “re-
member this is not a pantomime.” “No, and
it isn’t an oratorio,” said Bracy.
THE PANTO., AT HER MAJESTY’S,
MELBOURNE.
The Principal Boy
in her naval rig.
Constance Cayley
as the Prince
Regent.
“0.K.”: My personal recollection of
Henry Bracy dates back to a time when he
was the good-looking but inanimate young
tenor of a comic-opera co. at the little
Strand Theatre (Lon.), the prima-donna
being Florence St. John. That may have
been previous to his first visit to Australia
in the Julia Matthews days. Anyhow, he
was back on the London stage more than
35 years ago, and it was not until the end
of 1800 that he happened again in Australia
and got a billet with Williamson and Mus-
grove. Bracy’s first appearance under the
firm’s management was in the never-popular
opera “Marjorie.” Florence Savile, daughter
of the Simonsens, sang the name-part splen-
didly. Bracy and his wife (the Clara
Thompson of their former days in Australia)
were more or less associated with W. and
M. for five or six years. Then the tenor
started his own co., and with “The Beggar
Student',” “Nemesis” and other things he
kept going in opposition to the bigger show.
This battling resulted in Williamson giving
him a good, easy billet to keep him out of
management; and his last years of active
life were passed in the service and friend-
ship of J.C.W., who, by the way, remembered
him in his will. He was the trimmest and
most serenely dispositioned theatrical stager
that ever I met. As an actor he never lose
his woodenness. lie was always a mechani-
cal, sweet-voiced, unimpassioned tenor, nor
did his smooth. s\Veet voice ever have much
color in it. And it faded until Albert
Whelan’s imitation of it was screamingly
funny.
“Ami” : Henry Bracy and his wife came to
Australia over 40 years ago, and seemed to
have made a false step. In England they
were in good business, with influential con-
nections, while in the Melbourne of ’7O their
outlook was so-precarious that Bracy had to
join the stock, and even descended to such
little parts as Guildenstern, in “Hamlet.”
However, Lyster was then finding, out that
managers cannot live by grand opera alone.
He had to fall back more and more on light
opera, and thus the Bracys got their chance.
Mrs. Bracy (Clara Thompson) always had
a defective voice; but her splendid industry
and high intelligence took her to the top.
Bracy knew his own limitations, and would
wisely play second to Beaumont. Meanwhile
Lydia Thompson, Clara’s sister, was doing
well in England and America; and I fancy
she drew the Bracys back to London. Bracy
was there terribly unlucky in management,
and lost pretty well his all over an opera
called “The Lady of the Locket.” Back again
with us, all was plain sailing for as excellent
a couple as ever adorned the stage.
“Super”: Henry Bracy was one of the
few men able to boast that, in the old days
at the London Savoy,, he had, without
authority, changed a piece of business ir.
one of the Gilbert and Sullivan operas, and
lived to tell the tale. As the Hilarion
of the original production of “Prince-is
Ida,” Bracy took upon himself in one scene
to stand instead of kneel at the foot of a
column, as stipulated in the stage direc-
tions, for the reason that in kneeling he
disturbed the fit of his elegant garments.
The next morning he received a note from
the office informing him that the alteration
had been observed; but, luckily for him,
Gilbert had approved of the change, and the
message went on to tell him that he was
at liberty for the future to stand while
reciting the lines. Both Gilbert and Sulli-
van, as a rule, fiercely resented any altera-
tion of their directions. They supervised
the production of all their operas at the
Savoy, and dictated every move in the
stage business. It was at a rehearsal of
“Pinafore” that one of the principals, when
directed by Gilbert to “sit down pensively”
on the hatch of the ship, flung himself down
and fell through, since the carpenter had
only covered the hatch with make-shift can-
vas. “That’s not pensively,” exclaimed
Gilbert; “that’s expensively !”
“Harrison O.”: It was not alone rever-
ence for the old English comedies that moved
me to protest against the gagging which
“0.K.” (B. 1/2/T7) appears to find such a
gratifying feature of Allan Wilkie’s produc-
tions. Meeting similar puerilities in a
modern musical comedy, I would have felt
that to snort was a duty I owed to the
thousands —nay, millions —of readers who
look to me for guidance In theatrical
matters. But no musical comedy or panto-
mime of my acquaintance has contained any-
thing more banal than some of Wilkie’s
efforts. For “0.K.” to defend a gag on the
ground that it is “what Goldsmith doubt-
less would have put into the situation had
he thought of it” is pure humbug. Without
proclaiming Goldsmith and Sheridan literary
demigods, the things they thought of strike
me as somewhat superior to the thoughts of
any mummers who have come my way.
I compared an actor who messes with the
work of a first-class dramatist with a scene-
painter who would add a few touches to a
Corot landscape or a vaudeville musician
who would interfere with Puccini. “0.K.”
calls the comparison preposterous. But
where is the difference? Why should
the art of a great writer be tam-
pered with any more than that of a
painter, sculptor or musician? Gilbert used
to declare that he would shoot the scoundrel
who tried to work a gag into one of his
librettos, and had he carried out the threat
any jury of literary men would have re-
turned a verdict of justifiable homicide.
Also, knowing “0.K.,” methinks (why
shouldn’t I methink, too?) I can hear the
burning words of scorn he would .direct at
the base knave who, lacking metrical skill,
subjected “The Kiss of Dolly Day” to the
treatment Wilkie meted out to “She Stoops
to Conquer.”

THE BROKEN DOLL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Miss Billie Burke has the highest salary ever
paid to a motion-picture actress. Attentions a
queen would envy are showered on this popular
actress. She has three private cars. A magnificent
yacht is at her disposal and a luxurious bungalow.
She is cooled by breezes from the ocean, and the
sea for some distance is reserved for her use.”
“It is feared that Emmaline Sloss will never act
for the movies again, the recent motor accident
having left a serious disfigurement.”
Time was when women thought it great
To be a queen and move in state.
The diadem from off her brow
An Empress e’en would barter now
To wield her sorceries seraphic
In parlors cinematographic.
The picture artiste owns the earth,
And uses it for all it’s worth.
The sun shines to impart a streak
Of rose to her celestial cheek.
The winds blow, by a heavenly rule,
To keep the picture lady cool.
The twinkling stars they blink at night
Bedazzled by her finer light.
That she may bathe the gods provide
The ocean and the changing tide,
By which her agent, iron-nerved,
A notice posts, “This S'ea Reserved.”
But when her million-dollar car
She heads down where the boulders are,
The laws of gravitation fail
In homage, and she strikes the trail
And breaks her nose. Then, thud!
The wondrous creature’s name is Mud.

IF FOOD DISAGREES
DRINK HOT WATER.
When food lies like lead in the stomach
and you have that uncomfortable, distended
feeling, it is because of insufficient blood
supply to the stomach, combined with acid
and food fermentation. In such cases try
the plan now followed in many hospitals and
advised by eminent specialists of taking half
a teaspoonful of bisurated magnesia in half
a glass of water as .hot as you com-
fortably drink it. The hot water draws the
blood to the stomach, and the iisurated mag-
nesia, as any physician or chemist can tell
you, instantly neutralises the acid and stops
the food fermentation. Try this simple plan
and you will be astonished at the immediate
feeling of relief and comfort that always
follows the restoration of the normal process
of digestion. Soldiers at the front and trav-
ellers who are frequently obliged to take
hasty meals poorly prepared should always
take two or three five-grain tablets of bisu-
rated magnesia after meals to m-event fer-
mentation and neutralise the acid. — Advt.

“Briner”: The new Seamen’s Mission,
being built near the Melbourne Wharf, is
a considerable job. The shipowners and
merchants subscribed £15,000, but I don't
think there were any large cheques from
sailors. The old place will be knocked away
to make room for wharfage. The amiable
semi-salt known as “Chaplain-Admiral”
Goldsmith is in charge. He had much Hong-
kong experience before coming to Mel-
bourne, and does pretty well ; but there is
too much kindly reference to the sailor as
“Jack” and “Honest Tar,” and the fussily
benevolent Ladies' Harbor Lights’ -Guild tries
to be good to "poor Jack”—which is worse
than ever. This well-meaning institution,

by the way, is “Goldy’s” sheet anchor. He
has even got branches of it in Ballarat,

“Hamer”: Re the early Armstrong bac-
teries (B. 25/1/T7). Mercer’s, sent to
M.L., was not more successful than the
battery despatched to China, which "The
Old Soldier” met. It failed to make any
impression on the powerful entrenchments
of the Maoris at Rangiriri on the Waikato;
and its commander fell in leading a forlorn-
hope charge on the stronghold—an unusual
thing for an artillery commander to do. A
trench was dug out towards where he lay,
and a Dr. Temple and others went and
brought him in. A V.C. and other honors
were distributed; but the highest honor
should have gone to a Maori chief (Teoreore,
I think) who dashed out, although friends
and foes were both firing his way, and re-
moved the wounded man .to the compara-
tively sheltered position wdience he was •
recovered by Temple. Yet the gallant Maori
didn’t even get a beer.

“F.N.T.”: It was recently suggested bj
The Bulletin that what the gospel of the
defunct Chidley really needs to give it a
lift is a lady Chid. There used to be an
ensevnble sisterhood in the U.S.A. It mostly
lived and moved and had its peanuts in the
“woods” surrounding a neat and comfortable
establishment where teachings, largely relat-
ing to the folly of footwear and the crimin-
ality of corsets, were absorbed on rainy
days. The main plank in the association’s
platform was No Wardrobe Whatever, so
it was an embarrassing time for Officer
O’Reilly when the sisterhood fell foul of
the Law and had to be summoned to appear
at court. O’Reilly and his summons were
affably received by the Chief Altogetheress,
a stout lady clad iightly in a kind smile and
a good head of hair, and he was politely in-
vited to stay to lunch and see more of the
Plan. O’Reilly, as it happened, had plans
of his own, including one named Bridget:
and he remembered Bridget in time and
fled.

“F.C.”: Melbourne City Council’s
scheme of licensing a dozen or so fruit-
kiosks or barrows at certain street corners
and hunting off all those sinners who used
to supply the eager public with fruit at
half the price charged in city
shops has had the effect of
creating a close corporation.
To-day you cannot buy a
pound of decent peaches in or
around Melbourne for less
than 3d., and you will pay
4d. and 6d. a lb. for choicer
goods. As the grower can
hardly scare Bs. a case out of
his customers and there are
401 b. to 451 b. in a case, it is
evident there is a discrepancy
somewhere. The grower hav-
ing a heavy crop of peaches
is at present moving heaven
and earth to find fresh mar-
kets and persuade “the fac-
tories” to buy the fruit for
canning and jam making. “The
factories”—which, as far as'
Victoria and Tasmania are
concerned, means three com-
panies—are not taking much
fruit, having heavy stocks of
pulp from last year’s crop, and
the “fresh markets” are not
easily discoverable. If the
fruitgrower would but organise
himself and pay as much at-
tention lo distributing as lie
does to producing, he would
need to worry less about the
disposal of his stuff. But in
that matter he seems hope-
less ; if he weren’t his success
in getting rid of last year’s glut of apples
would have taught him a point.

After 41 years’ service the cable-station
at Wakapuaka, near Nelson (M.L.). is to
be abandoned. When the line was laid be-
tween Maoriland and Sydney it was believed
that the land station must be placed as close
to the sea-cable as possible; but that idea
has been blown out. The M.L. end of the
line will now be in Wellington G.P.O. The
Hibernia and Edinburgh laid the cable
from La Perouse to Farewell Spit, thence
to “Waka,” the final splice being made
at 1 p.m. on February 18, 1876. The charge
for messages was 7s. 6d. for 10 words, which
was cheap, considering that the Eastern Ex-
tension Co. then required £9 6s. 6d. to send
20 words from Adelaide to London. But
the Tasman service was subsidised and the
other wasn't.

“Three Stripes”: Up near Euroa, in the
Kelly country, drowses the little Church of
England wherein the residents of Longwood
(population 800) sleep on Sundays. Thirty
of the congregation have" volunteered, and
five of them, all baptised within the church’s
walls, have made their names famous. These
are Lieut.-Col. Leslie Maygar, who won the
V.C. in S. Africa, went through Gallipoli, and
now commands the Bth Light Horse in
Egypt; Major F. H. Tubb (V.C., Gallipoli) ;
his brother, Lieut. Frank R. Tubb (M.C.,
France) ; Major Aubrey R. L. Wiltshire
(M.C. and Croix de Guerre, France) ; and
Captain Gordon Maxfield (M.C., France),
who stood to attention on the torpedoed
Southland and to arms in the trenches of
Anzac—surely the most remarkable collec-
tion of warriors that ever came out of a
dreamy little country tabernacle in one
generation.
* * * *
“Sandhurst” : Foundation Day is a pub-
lic holiday in Vic. in. honor-of the landing
of Governor Phillip in Sydney Harbor on
January 26, 1788. A more appropriate date
for celebration would have been Captain
Cook’s landing at Botany Bay on April 27,
1770. The idea of making Foundation Day
a national festival was conceived by Malachi
Cahill, a president of the A.N.A. Bendigo
branch. I pinched the idea and got the
Melbourne A.N.A. to wait upon James
Service some time in 18S8 and ask him as
Premier to proclaim the 20th of January a
public holiday. Our spokesman was the
late J. L. Purves, then leader of the Vic-
torian Bar. Field Barrett, the author of
the epigram that Australians were a moder-
ately moral people, also spoke in support
of the request. Service was sympathetic,
and it was my part to thank the Minister
before the deputation withdrew.

“Ike”: Is there a shortage of fruit back
home? If not, why are we in Egypt get-
ting jam from Uncle Sham? Last week we
opened a consignment the tins of which were
quite three-parts full, and some Billjims
had to go short. Still, that wasn’t much of

was.
“Wyndoyle”: The champion Billjim bluff
was worked in the days when "Kitchener
cheques” were plentiful. A party of seven
boarded the Melbourne express at Sydney
with tickets for six and promptly held a
council of war to decide how to pass the
seventh. The leader (a Oft., corporal)
took charge of the orphan and at “Show
tickets, please!” he explained to the collec-
tor that the other cow was a prisoner he
was escorting to Melbourne, where a court-
martial was awaiting him for deserting from
the Victorian forces and enlisting in N.S.W.
under another name. During this critical
period the prisoner wore handcuffs for
dramatic effect. The bluffers were streaming
out of Spencer-street station when I last
saw them, so I guess they battled through
all right.
“Doggie” : When the first batch of volun-
teers from the Postmaster-General's depart-
ment was about to leave for the Front I
had the pleasure of hearing the boss in his
farewell speech tell them that on their re-
turn “they would find their jobs waiting for
them.” Well, I know four who have re-
turned, and this is what has happened to
them: —■
No. 1 was a permanent employee who was unfit
for further active military service but desired to
act as recruiting sergeant (at a lower remuneration
than his departmental pay). The necessary leave
was refused, and, determined to do his best for his
country, he resigned.
No. 2 was a "t< mporary” employee. lie had
received a life-1 ng irj ry. On reporting for duty
he was told that he world have to take his chance
in the same manner as a new applicant. He sought
work elsewhere.
No. 3 was a prrr.o'crt officer who was invalided
home owing to a breakdown of health. Immediately
on resuming .duty he was transferred to a district
with one of the most trying climates in the Common-
wealth. He had to resign.
No. i was a lad who had to get out of the
trenches owing to, heart trouble. He was given
such heavy work in the mail branch that he had to
quit it.
After this fine bit of recruiting, no wonder
the men in this department rush to enlist!

HER VOICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I hear her voice!
It breaks upon my drowsing plight.
The faint, soft, scented summer night,
And all the sleepy living- things
Adrift above on moveless wings
Rejoice.
What miracles of melody
Eloat down to me V
I hear her sing !
Each dreaming- leaf drops pearls of song,
And all the tranced sky along
In vapor caves on cloudy wreck
Of snow-capped hills the perfect ech-
’Oes ring.
Like fading perfume of a rose
The magic goes.
I hear her song!
I might have slept, but every note
From that full, alabaster throat
Breathes Wondrous love to me; and so
I drink in all its poisoned bow.
How long?
What tender light—Jehoshaphat!
It is the cat! -

“Brassard’s” yarn of duplicity :
It is recorded that an eminent military authority
once said, “Give the Australians £4O each, place
Berlin out of bounds and they will be there within
a fortnight.” Well, they’d have a good try for it.
Billeted within a few miles of a large city, our
crowd naturally desired to see the sights, but a
guard blocked the way. That set us thinking. When
we’d finished about 20 of the boys proceeded to
within 'a hundred yards of the barrier, where they
were hidden by a turn in the road. One of the
party had commandeered an officer’s cap and water-
proof cape into which he poked himself, and the
“company” fell in, formed fours and marched.
“Haiti” came the voice of the Tommy guard.
“Passes, please!” “Australian picket—no passes
required,” came the ready answer of the “0.C.”
“Quick march!” and the “picket” went on its way
to the fleshpots. After the noble band had dis-
appeared a solitary Anzac managed to get on the
right side of Atkins. “Bo careful,” was the parting-
advice of that warrior. “One of your pickets has
just gone through.”

“P.X.”: Mothers (and fathers, too) with
soldier boys at tlie Front have solid ground
for complaint against the Postal Depart-
ment. There may be some reason why Bill-
jim, whose whereabouts are more or less
uncertain, should not get letters till they
have ripened for 10 or 12 months ; but there
is no reason why the Expeditionary mail
should be the last to be delivered in Aus-
tralia. A mail arrived in Sydney week
before last. On Friday morning I received
a letter or two from London. On Saturday
some newspapers dropped in and a printed
appeal from Mrs. Lloyd George for help for
sailors’ wives. Monday morning’s mail
included a London publisher’s circular and a
service card from France. On Monday
afternoon an anxiously-awaited line or two
arrived from a soldier-son who, at previous
advices, was. at the point of death. That
was 31 days after the first letters by the
same mail were delivered, and three days
after printed matter had been made avail-
able. Similar things have happened again
and again. Repeatedly the vessel which has
brought the mail has crawled round to Syd-
ney and berthed at Circular Quay before
the Expeditionary letters landed at Adelaide
and sent overland have been delivered in the
city. These small things count.. I don’t
know whether, among the whole boiling of
Commonwealth Ministers, there is one with

When the anxiously awaited and avidly-read

From The Bulletin’s bird on the West-
ern Front: —
Winter is settling in now, and with it an
easier if more monotonous time for us. During the
summer, when it was light at 3 o’clock ancl not
dark until nine, we did five and sometimes six hours
a day in the air. It doesn’t look much on paper;
but at the end of it you feel as though you have
earned your 255.
Outside it is a typical late-November day, cold,
damp, raw, with a thick mist which puts flying
out of the programme. It has been like this for
THE BREED.
“Yes! I suppose it's the old Viking Hood in
me! But I couldn’t be out of it any
longer, so I went up yesterday and
signed on for the Army Bay Corps.”
nearly a week; so, the machines having been closely
looked over and made ready for flying, we have prac-
tically nothing to do but keep ourselves as fit as
we have got our mounts. That’s the compensation
of the Flying Corps. When you are on the job
you certainly are on the job; but when the job is
done you get what the poor beggars in the trenches
can’t have—absolute rest in comfortable quarters
away from the hellish shell-fire and everything.
The perfection to which flying has been brought
is one of the great marvels of the war. The whole
corps can spend a hundred hours in the air without
a single crash that can be attributed to a fault of
the machine. Of our actual work one can say little:
every month, to keep us reminded of our frailties,
we must accept an order committing ourselves to
all sorts of penalties for disclosing information of
military value. But most of us have rough diaries:
and there should be some talking afterwards. I
should not care to risk a bean, though, that my
diary will be any use to me for 18 months.
“Archie” is our chiefest worry. He is the Huns’
anti-aircraft gun. Their own machines are nowa-
days very rarely seen, even when the weather is
all that it should be. Nine times out of 10, when
they do come out and there is the possibility of a
scrap, they beetle off. But, to do the beggars
justice, “Archie” is magnificent; and so are
“Archie’s” gunners—as they ought to be, considering
the practice they get. Our own fellows have only
one or two shoots a week. I have had the planes
of my machine renewed twice, thanks to “Archie,”
but have had better luck myself. It isn’t one-
sided, however; for if the Hun airman won’t let
us fight him, we can direct our artillery fire and
have some play with bombs. One gets some price-
less sights in bomb-dropping. The other day, in
a raid on a railway station, I played on an engine;
and there was nothing left but steam and splinters.
Perhaps it isn’t quite the thing to talk of the
moral effect that your own crowd’s work has on
others. But chaps in the trenches have told me of
how it, has hearteqed them to see our machines
going over the Hun lines day after day in great
formations, spying out the land, noting every move-
ment, and giving them who merely walk on the
earth tho confident assurance that no great surprise
can be sprung upon them.

“Nulla”:—•
Hard luck? ’Struth! It was Crismus in Egypt,
and though Jim and me had fed well we could ha’
done with a lot more to drink if we’d had the
dough. Moochin’ about on the off-chance of striking
someone we knew, we lobbed at a camp just when
they was handing out Crismus billies. We got one.
’Struth! It had toothbrushes, ’n’ soap, cards, a
prayer-book, and bottles of pills an’ a plum pudding.
We couldn’t look at tucker, so we give the pudding
to a nigger—poor cow, he looked hungry—an’ at the
bottom of the billy we gets a letter. It was from
three good sports of girls in Bunbury, an’ after
wishing us everything of the best, they wind up:
And we hope you won’t break your teeth on the
half-sovereign in the pudding.”
“Billjack”: Dick was hit at the same
time as me. I wasn’t all out, and I. just
gave myself up to the comfort of lying still,
but it had made a mess of Dick. I watched
him for a time, thinking he had gone over,
but presently he began pulling himself
together. I'c was a hard job, but he got
as far up as his knees; then, all shaking,
he raised his hands, and I heard him say
his prayer: “Oh, God, it’s no use talkin’ —
You knoiv!”

“Tommy Kangaroo”: The order about
third-class carriages for the boys was bad
enough, but it’s the limit the way staff offi-
cers are treating our nursing sisters in Egypt.
They are hedged in with such ridiculous and
pin-pricking restrictions and regulations that
it’s a marvel there isn’t open mutiny. It
really seems that every-
thing is done to aggra-
vate and annoy the
nurses, while nothing is
done to make their duty
nleasant. If allowed out
in the evening they must
not leave before 8 p.m.,
and must be in by 10
p.m. excent when a late
pass is given as an extra-
special flavor. They are
not allowed to dance. Oh,
dear, no! Everybody else
may dance in Cairo, but
not the girls who are
slaving so unselfishly for
the soldiers. They must
not talk to the Billjim
hospital orderlies, or go
out with anyone less than
an officer. Recently an
order was issued forbid-
ding them to have dinner
in Cairo with Australian
officers, hut there was
such an outcry from the
officers that the order
was rescinded. There’s
not enough ink in this battalion to tell you
all the “thou slialt nots” that confront
Billjimiina here.

“Rio”: Something for the superstitious
told by Captain Lindsay Browne, M. 0., the
Sydney boy who fought with the Irish
Rifles in France. The evening before leaving
Ireland for the Front, Browne, while walk-
ing with the R.C. chaplain, picked up a
rosary. He offered it to the Padre, saying,
“Here, Father, you’d better take this; it
probably belongs to one of your flock.” But
his Rivirence refused, saying, “No, my son;
finding that is a good omen. Put it in your
pocket and no harm will come to you in
battle.” So Browne pouched it. He went
through the hottest of the fighting of 1914,
staying with his battalion while it dwindled
from a thousand to under a hundred and
all other officers but himself were hit. lie
had three bullets through his cap, eight
through his coat and others through his
breeches. The heel was blown off his boot
by a piece of shell and several times
weapons were blown out of his hand. On
other occasions he was bespattered by the
blood of men who were fighting alongside
him. Although he suffered from strain and
shock the Germans never even drew blood
on him. And Browne is “Church of Eng-
land !”

“The Oldest Inhabitant”: A property,
offered for sale by auction on the Blue Moun-
tains a few days ago, was advertised as an
“early selection made by the late Henry
Parkes,” and within four minutes’ walk of
the Wentworth Falls railway station.” Also,
on March 24 will be offered, likewise in the
Allen estate, “Faulconbridge, Henry Parkes’s
early selection,” etc. When Henry Parkes
assigned his estate on October 12, 1887, for
the benefit of his creditors, the following
were amongst the items: —
Trustees of the late Geo. Wiaram Allen, secured
upon the Faulconbridge estate, £9344.
The City Bank, secured upon property at Faulcon-
bridge, Coleridge, Ashfield and Wentworth Falls,
£6371 18s. 3d.
City Bank, arrears of interest on same, £5OOO.
The Colonial Mutual Life Association and nine
private individuals, including Francis Abigail,
£14,000, secured upon property on North Shore;
and as to the Colonial Mutual Life, 10 shares in
the Holt-Sutherland estate.
Bank of New South Wales, £4045 os. 9d., secured
on land at Jervis Bay.
E., S. and A. C. Bank, secured upon property at
Guildford, Moama and Canley Valo, £2464 12s. 2d.
Saul Samuel and John Frazer, secured upon pro-
perty at Coleridge and Jerilderie, £2214 4s. Id.
Commercial Banking Co., secured on property at
Springwood, £lO5O.
S. Fawkes £5OO, and estate of the late John
Cuthbert, £5OO, secured upon property at the
Valley and 50 acres at Springwood.
Not one of the Mountain properties was
worth anything approaching the amount that
the old man had succeeded in borrowing upon
it. Faulconbridge and Wentworth Falls,
were merely selections on the then usual
terms of fl per acre, with residence condi-
tions. When the wonderful financier assigned
his estate he was Colonial Secretary. He
vacated his seat, but-not his office, and was
re-elected a week later.

THE CONSTANT LOVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dearest Harold,—You have gone,
Like a vision from my sight,
And my soul may feast upon
Nothing but my grievous plight.
From the rose the scent has flown,
From the grass the song of Spring;
And about the world my own
Dullness doth her porridge fling....
Ah! When I kiss Reg to-night
Hay my lips to his transmute
Something of the dear delight
That from yours they culled —you brute!
But when you , with bliss divine,
Kiss the cat you now prefer,
How I hope each kiss of mine
Will return and poison her!
N.S.W.

COMING TOGETHER.


—STRENGTH —
AND HOW TO GAIN IT.
INSTEAD OF EXERCISES, PATENT
FOODS AND MEDICINES, TAKE
PHOSPHATE WITH YOUR MEALS.
The demand to-day is for men and women
who are strong in every sense of the word
—possessing the physical strength necessary
to endure hardships and fatigue; the mental
strength to grapple with difficult problems;
the nervous force which endows the body
with vigor and vitality ; the will power to
triumph over adversity and turn defeat into
victory. But such glorious strength is im-
possible' so long as your nerves are weak
and exhausted; and therefore if you would
be really strong you must first care for your
nerves. Weak, exhausted nerves need food, and
it has been proved in numberless cases that
the only food element they can or will absorb
readily and naturally is phosphate in the
well-known form of bitro-phosphate, which
most chemists stock in 5-gr. compressed tab-
lets, so that if you feel your strength is fail-
ing from any cause, you should get a supply
of these bitro-phospliate tablets and take one
with every meal. Practically all of the
minor ailments afflicting mankind, as well
as many of the more serious maladies, can be
traced to nervous exhaustion and lowered
vitality, and probably this explains why such
a remarkable improvement in the general
health is invariably noticeable when bitro-
phosphate is taken as directed, and the
nerves are thereby revitalised and made
strong.— Advt.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Captain O. V. Hoad, Chief of the Tas-
manian General Staff and Aide-de-Camp to
the Commandant, is
a son of the late
Major-General, and
is popularly known
in Tassy as “the
Crown Princ e.”
Denied thus far the
opportunity of win-
ning a V.C., Hoad
has decorated himself
with big-bowed shoes
and green socks. He
is admired by all the
pert maidens when
doing the Block ; but
with an expression
of Bracken’s “Not
Understood” he main-
tains his dignity as a
British soldier should.
CAPT. O. V. HOAD,
Chief of the Tas.
General Staff, and
aide-de-camp to the
Commandant.
Taking his second chance, Lieut.-colonel
Simpson, once a teacher at the Albury
Grammar School, was killed in France last
month. Early in the war Simpson, then 31,
left cricket and football to lesser men, said
good-bye to his wife and son, and went out
as captain in the 13th Battalion. His bri-
gade (the 4th) followed up the exploits of
the 3rd at the landing by advancing on Sari
Bair, so gallantly woft and lost at the time
the Suvla Bay effort failed. He won the
Military Cross at Gaba Tepe for directing
his battalion through unreconnoitred scrub
singing with Turkish bullets and alive with
snipers. Wounded, he was invalided home,
and the Albury people made him a presen-
tation. But he felt that his job was not yet
done, and early in May, 1916, he joined the
33rd as major. With fresh fighting came
further promotion, and he was temporary
lieut.-colonel of another N. S. Wales battal-
ion when the end came.
THE MENU.
HE ( with the breakfast liver): “Eggs, eggs,
nothing but eggs! And this one's
rotten! What have you got for dinner
to-night—eggs?"
SHE ( stinging with resentment): “Rats!”
HE : “ Then you had better cook only one. I
shan't be home!"
It was Archbishop Dunne, the gentle old
prelate of Brisbane who died the other day,
who first suggested that Cardinal Moran
(then Bishop Moran) should be appointed
to Sydney. They had been fellow-students
in Rome, and in the course of time Dr.
Dunne had come to an Australian bishopric
at Brisbane, while his friend had gone to
O.ssory, in Ireland. At the meeting of the
bishops when the Archdiocese of Sydney
became vacant on the death of Dr. Vaughan,
Bishop Dunne recommended that the name
of the Bishop of Ossory be sent to the Pope.
“You will never get him to come out here!”
he was told; but he insisted, the name was
sent and the choice was ratified by Leo XIII.
The N. S. Wales Government missed a
chance the other day. It wanted two men
for Arbitration Court Judges, and it might
have made good with Billjim by offering
one of the jobs to Dr. Brissenden, who is
now at the Front, for which he left as a
private. He was a good man when he left,
and he will be a still better man for the
very human Industrial Court when he comes
back. For a while “Bris,” the readiest wit
of the N. S. Wales Bar. was kept back at
the base, supnlying law for a corporal’s pay.
But he pushed and pushed till they let him
out and into the trenches.
Peace to William Ruka, of Rarotonga.
Trained at Auckland for war by the Maori-
land Government, the islander got as far
as Sydney with his smart little detachment,
and then had to be left behind at the Coast
Hospital. He died last week among kindly
strangers who could not speak his language,
and if any Australian wants to help put a
headstone above him, a small sub. sent to
the Maoriland Soldiers’ Reception Society,
5 Moore-street, Sydney, will be welcomed.
James Clarke, a grandfather, of Bendigo,
received a note from his son, Sergeant
Sydney Clarke, saying, “I have voted for
conscription, and I hope you and mother
did too. For God’s sake send along some
men—they’re wanted badly enough.”
Grandpa, who was a chauffeur for six years
to Captain G. Y. Lansell, now at the
Front, and recently wounded, determined to
set the boys an example. His other sons
are too young to join the colors, so he went
himself to the recruiting office, was accepted,
and was in camp when The Bulletin
heard the story.
“C.M.”: This is the youngest Billzac —■
Bertie Dunncliff, of Pirie-street, Adelaide,
No. 3735. He is not 15, yet he has been in
the trenches since November, 1915. He was
13 years 4§ months when he enlisted, had
his 14th birthday on the battlefield, and is
now returning to Australia suffering from a
nervous breakdown. His father was killed
at Gallipoli.
N.S.W. Bench has a row of very grey
wigs waiting for Time to call the last case-
Simpson (Chief Judge in Equity), <4;
Rogers, District Court, 76; Docker ("Little
Brown Jug”), 75 presently; Fitzhardmge,
71 • and Heydon, President of the Industrial
Court, three months less than that.
Pickburn and Rolin, the two newly-appointed
Industrial Judges, seem like intrusive small
boys alongside their ancient brothers-in-law.
Yet Rolin is 53. Brother Pickburn is 46.
Admiral 11. C. Kane, who died in London
lately, was the captain of the Calliope when
in 1889 a terrific hurricane tore into Apia
Harbor. Samoa, where seven warships lay.
Kane, full of courage and confidence in his
ship and his seamanship, cut his anchor,
steamed into the hell-sea, and rode it out in
safety. The U.S.A. navy lost three ships
and 104 men that day. and the German
shortage was two ships and 96 men. All
Kane got from the Lords of the Admiralty
was their “official thanks.” If a German
could have “bluffed the eternal sea” like
the English seaman did he’d have been given
a cargo of iron crosses and invited to wallow
in the Imperial sauerkraut trough any time
he was passing.
What an interesting list it would make
if somebody found out the former profes-
sions of the Bungs of Australia! Campion,
of the Swanston-street Queen’s Arms
(Melb.), who died the other day, was an
engineer before family interests called him
to the bar; and his friend Kerr, of the Port
Phillip Club, who died a few days later, was
an architect who took to butter-making,
established the first factory in the Western
District, and afterwards helped to build
scores of them throughout Australia. /
A salute of 19 guns should be arranged
for Mrs. A. A. Leane, of Prospect (S.A.)
in part recognition of what she has done for
her country. Until January 4, when one of
them, Alan W., was killed in action, she
was the mother of three lieut.-colonels- -
Edmund Thomas, on the 2nd Divisional
Administrative Staff, who was a captain in
Gallipoli with the 12th Battalion, of Lone-
some Pine fame; Raymond Lionel, who, also
as a captain in Gallipoli, took Leane’s
Trench with a bunch of the 11th Battalion,
won the Military Cross thereby, and later
gained the D.S.O. in France; and Alan W.
aforementioned. The last-named was officer
commanding the 28th Battalion, whirl he
joined as major and company-commandar in
April, 1915. Mrs. Leane’s two remaining
sons—B.B. and E.A. —are a major and a
sergeant respectively. The major jumped
from chevron to stars when he was pro-
moted from the ranks the day after the
Landing. In addition to the first generation
four grandsons and six nephews are helping
to make the Kaiser realise that he spoke out
of his turn. If this be not enough, Lieu-
tenant T. C. Fairley, of the 4Sth Battalion,
who won the Military Cross at Pozieres for
line-repairing under fire, is an adopted son
of Mrs. Leane’s daughter, Mrs. W. Taylor,
of Kalgoorlie.

Passed back into the crucible at 65, T. U.
Walton, chief chemist to the Colonial Sugar
Refining Co. He came out from Greenock
(Scotland) 36 years ago, and he and his
sweet job held each other firmly till Death
stepped in last week. Local scientists lis-
tened to him with deference, and the Sydney
branch of the Society of Chemical Industry
was pioud to call him its lion. sec. lie was
just the sort of man Australia can’t afford
to lose.

Thirty-six years ago 11. W. Cameron won
the champion-baby prize at Tamworth
(N.S.W.) : a week ago one of his children
won the “finest baby under 12 months” prize
on the same showground.
“F.L.J.”: As tlie Carmelite priests go
everywhere to preach missions. Father Ley-
bourne and Father Devlin will be remem-
bered all over the Australian continent.
They came from and went back to an inter-
esting old church in Whitefriar-street, Dub-
lin, which is bound up with the modern
history of the old city by the Liffey.
Father Leybourne died since his return
to Dublin, and he has been succeeded as
head of the “Children of Mary” by Father
Devlin, who, like him, used to flourish at
Port Melbourne. The “Children” have been
celebrating their silver jubilee, and Father
Devlin took occasion to talk of Australia.
The Dublin “Children” are more liberal
than the Australian ones, by the way. They
admit married women to membership.
“The Life of E. W. Cole,” the venerable
Book Arcadian, just, published, is by “One
Who Knows Him Well”; and the one is
supposed to be Pyke, his manager. It is
doubtful whether the column of type in the
“boneyard” of more than one Melbourne
newspaper, prepared some time ago when
the good old boy was ill, contains more in-
teresting details. The Book King, says his
biographer, landed here in the sixties, after
two years in S’Africa. With a frying-pan
as a signboard, he sold cordials to the Mary-
borough (Vic.) diggers, but in the ’seven-
ties drifted to Melbourne and at a bookstall
in Paddy’s Market laid the foundation of a
business which has spread over three cities
and runs to almost everything except trac-
tion-engines ' and giraffes.
Captain William Richard Benton, of the
Manchester Regiment, killed in action at
the Somme, crowded plenty into his 40
years.. Starting as clerk to his father, a
London stockbroker, he skipped from the
office and enlisted as private in the “blue
marines.” While on the Australian station
he deserted from the Orlando, then flagship
at Sydney, and got up country to Cowra,
where he worked as a farm-hand. He went
into an Australian contingent to the
S’African disturbance, and then joined the
Cape Police. In the force he “got religion.’
and going to England to be trained for the
Anglican priesthood, conscience made him
give himself up at Portsmouth as a deserter
from the R.M.A. He was court-martialled
and served a sentence, afterwards going
through liis course at an Anglican seminary
and becoming ordained. Returning to
S’Africa, he expounded the Word for some
time to the lepers on Robben Island. When
war broke out he got a military chaplaincy
in Flanders, but the Hun frightfulness made
him doff his dog-collar and look for a job
in which he could hit back. His _ former
training secured him a commission as
lieutenant, and he had got his three stars
when the Last Post sounded.
EVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
• Further exemption has been refused to a designer
of a firm of West End drapers, who was to have been
sent to Paris to select Spring fashions. The tribunal
said that fashions were now unnecessary. Changes
were not desired for three years.- —Cable item.
Since first a fig-leaf fluttered free
By rivers dim of Paradise,
Eve’s flashing eyes were wide to sec
The cut and shape of some device
Her shapely form to featly dress
And emphasise its loveliness.
Fresh fashions with the turning years
Made dazzling pageant as she went.
The work of needle, loom and shears
To subtle fantasy was lent.
In numerous garments, or in few,
Eve smiled because the style was new.
About six thousands years or so
Tlie dress conventions were her care,
Till war’s wild horns began to blow
A louder call to earth and air—
No previous red commands to slay
Had changed the tenor of her way.
But this war was of larger make.
It held the whole round earth in thrall.
The tottering thrones were seen to shake,
The steadfast mountains seemed to fall;
And word went forth “all fashions cease
Till fire and sword have won us peace.”
The newer modes were wrought in steel,
The latest styles were fringed with fire;
And Fate stood cloaked from throat to heel
In poison fumes and murder-wire.
The awful fashions of the hour
Were lightnings linked to death and power.
What mattered young Felicia’s dress,
Tube skirt or billowing petticoat,
The robe that sat like a caress,
The scarf on summer winds afloat?
The manj'-colored fashion-plate
Was absolutely out-of-date.
Eve, in her daughters, laughed aloud.
The fancy of the time went free.
None to a set convention bowed
While khaki held the world in fee;
But every damsel, happy faced,
Brought up her own reserves of taste.
All colors, every style and shape,
Were setting for her laughter clear ;
And soft alluring things to drape
Her sweetness filled the changing year—
Men saw, the storms of war o’erblown,
The best-dressed world that they had known!
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

The profit is better than any disclosed since
the end of 1914—foolish days when the
press was instructed in plain words to pull
the public’s leg, and the deluded hanks, in
common with others, believed that the war
would be all over in a few weeks. Last
year, on discovering how wickedly it had
been misled, the Victoria was feeling pretty
bad about it, and its figures suffered in conse-
quence. Now, the world seems a bit
brighter, so the profit looks up again.
From this it may be judged that
not much importance can be attached
to these figures in such times. Ordinarily
a bank can no more than indicate the trend
of its profits. But in disturbed times like
these there is no settled trend. One banker,
taking a gloomy view, may regard it as
necessary to make very large provisions
against probable future losses ; and he will
bite out of present apparent profits large
sums to cover these contingencies. Actu-
ally nobody knows what is going to happen.
But if banks can escape losses of principal,
they ought to show good profits for sofne
years to come, assuming, of course, that
there is no national catastrophe ahead of us.
As to losses of principal, what are the
prosnects V Such losses are generally due
-—in Australia particularly —to forced real-
isations. The creditor won’t wait for the
recovery. There is no reason to expect early
trouble from this source, because nowadays,
so far as the banks are concerned, we are
mostly our own creditor. But that won’t
necessarily save the banks from temporary
inconvenience. Because nobody knows what
our staple products are going to bring,
nobody knows what our land and
other securities are going to be worth.
Nobody knows either what their ex-
change business is going to be worth to
the banks; for that depends largely on
prices, too. The future is theretore un-
certain. But, assuming fair prices for
wool, wheat, meat and metals, bank profits
should be good. Interest is not going to
be low for years ; and, apart from the earn-
ings on their own funds, it is easier for a
bank to make profits when money is dear
than when it is cheap—especially when the
banks are a happy family as they are in
Australia.
The Victoria has a small, heavily-gilded
backing in the State to which it is confined ;
and its deposits at interest are much larger
in proportion to other deposits than is the
case with most banks. For that reason it
is justified in working on smaller liquid assets
than most banks. Still, even allowing for
that circumstance, 28 p.c. is a small holding
of liquid assets. The wheat harvest is,
however, coming in, and the wool-clip has
been sold ; so the advances will probably be
reduced, and the cash position strengthened.
That will bring the figures to something
more like what they were. Here is a com-
parison of the present with a year ago and
the December prior to the war: —
Liabilities.

Capital and reserves combined are
£1,918,125. Total liabilities (deposits, etc.)
are £6,049,825. So the Victoria owes less
than £3 10s. for every £1 of its own in the
business ; and that gives the creditor extra-
good security.
Apropos the Victoria and another, an old
banker writes: —
The recent amalgamation of two small Queensland
banks suggests the amalgamation of two nearly-pure
Victorian institutions —the Victoria and Colonial.
Both do business on the same lines in the same
limited sphere. The original charters of both were
opposed By the British banks and their operations
were limited. Each has< a London office and the
Colonial has one in Sydney. The Victoria cannot
open in the other States. If a fusion were effected,
there is no reason why a Federal Act should not be
passed giving the new concern the right to open in
the other States. The Victoria has 98 branches
and agencies; the Colonial 107. Of these 32 offices
are open in the same towns and would be closed,
leaving 173 branches and agencies, which seems
plenty for one State. The union of the staffs would
permit of some of the very old managers being
pensioned off. The savings effected by shutting up
the 32 offices would enable the Board to raise
salaries to a considerable extent all round. That
would be most desirable, because the two banks do
not pay their employees nearly scf liberally as some
of their rivals pay theirs. And the coalition should
earn increased profits, while the resources of the
new institution would be materially strengthened.
There would be a lot of the banks’ own real estate
available for sale to go to the credit of the joint
reserve fund. The united_.capi±al_Xif. nearly two
millions would be ample fox prudent ...extension of
the business. The adjustment of the share capital
interests ought not to be difficult on the basis of
fully-paid-up £1 shares. No new issue of capital
would be- necessary. With ten millions sterling of
deposits and advances and 3-1 millions of liquid assets,
the new concern would step into the front rank of
Australian banks and ruffle for new business with
the best of them. The Commonwealth Bank in the
near future, having neither dividends nor share-
holders to consider, will make the earning of banking
profits by little joint-stock banks not quite so easy
as it has been in the past; and increased taxation
will add to the difficulties. If they are wise they
will economise where they can.

In saying good-bye to general-manager Hen-
derson, who has just retired, it was recalled
“with pride” that the City was one of the
very few banks which did not shut IfieTr doors
in the crisis of the ’9o’s. Well, Mr. Hender-
son may be proud of it.; but he might have
hath a lot more sleep in subsequent years if
the bank had made a clean breast of its diffi-
culties then and used the axe and the broom
as others did. Instead, though a great deal
of capital was lost, the bank went on pre-
tending that it still had its £400,000 intact;
and for many years it has struggled to pay
a dividend on the full amount. If it only
had £300,000 (and there was a time when it
probably couldn’t show that much), then a
4 p.c. dividend was really a 5| p.c. dividend
on its actual capital. And even 5J p.c. looks
better than 4 p.c. The poorness of the divi-
dend and the public’s uncertainty as to the
bank’s position must have been a severe
handicap; and thus it has probably taken
twice as long to restore the lost capital as it
would have done if the assets had been
written down openly and capital reduced
accordingly. However, the City has always
had a little group of good friends; and. even
though the market value of the shares is
still much below par, the general manager
probably leaves his old chair knowing that,
on a fair valuation, there are now enough
assets in excess of liabilities to represent the
full capital. It will be for the new general
manager (J. S. Marks, lately secretary and
head-office manager) to persuade the public
that that is so. This done, an increase of
the visible profits and the dividend will
follow. ’

All things considered, it isn’t a bad 10 years’
record; and the improvement in the ad-
vances is no doubt better than the figuies
suggest, because there should be now less
dead-wood in the pile. Total liabilities
are £2.130.194. Deduct the liquid assets,
£572,499, and there remains £1,557,695 of
debt, represented by £1,820,095 of bills and
advances and £193,740 of premises. The
unknown quantity is the proportion Of doubt-
ful assets (if any) among the bills and
advances, But, as said, just nbw, it is
doubtful Whether there is much Over-valued
stuff left On the books. At any rate, the
disclosed profit nowadays is well over 7 p.c.
on the capital, and that, the chairman
assures the shareholders, is after placing a
very large sum” to contingency account.
Assuming that the £400,000 capital is now
intact the liabilities are equal to about £5 6s.
of the shareholders’ money in the business
(apart from reserves of all kinds) ; and that
isn’t by any means the limit of safe trading
when a bank has the confidence of its
depositors.

Here is another case in which the share-
holders might very properly be invited by
director Irvine and director Donald Mac-
kinnon to put up at least £50,000 of new
capital for investment in a War Loan.
Even including the undivided profits just
transferred to capital, the total is only
£50,000, which leaves £200,000 uncalled.
Apart from every other consideration, a 14
p.c. dividend does not look well; and for
prudential reasons alone the co. ought to
j lira at a chance of reducing it. If share-
holders put up £50,000 for a war loan, the
dividend could be reduced to a less con-
spicuous p.c., and still the shareholders
would get the full amount of the present
dividend plus the full interest on the loan.
Moreover, there is no doubt that a good
many of these cos. ought to put up more
capital. This co. doesn’t say the value of
the estates in its hands; but the total must
have increased very much in recent years,
and the capital should have increased in
proportion. If there was no need for it —If,
in other words, there are no risks to cover —
then there is no justification for the present
charges.
The co., while paying a dividend varying
from 10 to 30 p.c., has accumulated assets in
this fashion :—•

public securities ; so there is no doubt about
the genuineness of the lay-by. The “other
assets” consist of the co.’-s Queen-street
offices and sundry advances, etc., and neither
of the lots look over-valued.

Of course no co. which supplies a public
necessary, and especially an article upon
which hundreds of other industries more or
less depend, ought to pay a 13 p.c. dividend.
There can be no worse enemy not only of
private enterprise but of Australian enter-
prise than a light and power co. which uses
its monopoly to make profits of that sort.
How can the Australian industry which
needs these things compete decently with,
say, ftn English industry which can buy
municipally-supplied gas for less than two
shillings, largely becahse the capital in the
Undertaking is paying only 34 or 4 p.c.?
Cds: of this sort have been sheltering behind
the barrier erected against the socialistic
attacks on Private Enterprise. But we
have got to begin to take such matters more
seriously. National existence simply demands
that our industries shall not suffer
handicaps from which those of other coun-
tries are free. Such considerations intro-
duce an element of uncertainty to invest-
ments of this kind. Probably no injustice
will be done to them. Even though they be
sharks, none of the teeth which they now
have will be drawn. But it seems likely
that they will not be allowed to grow any
more teeth.
Still, the rate of dividend is really hot as
bad as it looks. For note the premium which
has been paid on shares. The share capital
is £957,2i0. Add to that the premium capi-
tal and the total is £1,515,540; and On that
lot the present dividend is a shade under 8i
p.c, But the co. has made very large sums
which it has not divided, and it is still
making them. Look at this comparison !
1906. 1916; .
Total Outlay charged to capital £2,171,745 £2,472,540
Total revenue . . . . 202,400 446,163
Revenue p.c. of capital outlay: 9.3 p.c. 18.0 p.fc.
Thus the revenue ih the 10 years has id-
creased by £243,731; and to produce that
result the shareholders have supplied only
£300,795 more capital. How much the cus-
tomers have supplied can’t be told for cer-
tain ; but look at the table, and you will see
that during the past five years £315,510 of
profit has been put into works —that is to
say, into “extension and improvement of
plant.” That is exclusive of the revenue
that has been employed in repairs and re-
newals. Last half-year, for instance, £07,141
was spent on repairs, maintenance and re-
newal of works and plant, including mains ;
and another £12,500 or more went in repair-
ing meters and stoves. The average profit
put into additions and improvements was
thus £63,000 a year, which is equal to 44
p.c. p.a. on the mean of the capital and pre-
mium capital combined during that period.
Thus, if the dividend is only 8* p.c. on the
capital actually put into the business by
shareholders, the transfer of profit to capital
account brings the total to 12J p.c.; and
there is, in addition, the Unappropriated pro-
fit put to reserve. Ohe of our national pro-
blems is how to obtain cheap light and power
for our industries; and cheaper lighting
also means a ioWer cost of living, which
makes lower wages possible without decreas-
ing the value of the wages. We must begin
to ioOk at these matters now from the
national point of view; and a change must
be made here.

That steady shrinkage of profits doesn’t iook
good, especially as the risks haven’t been
shrinking too —in volume anyway. So far
as the past year is concerned part of the
slump is due to extra land and income tax
payments; but not much, because all that
Maoriland is collecting towards the cost of
the war is still pretty harmless. Any real
struggle to pay the bill is being evaded in
the most cowardly fashion. Apparently the
idea is to let the men who are making the
disgustingly fat war profits have their fling,
and hand around the bill presently when the
fellows who are doing the fighting are back.
There is no more disgraceful feature of
Southern politics than the behavior of the
Maoriland politician, who, while pouring out
gallons of mushy patriotic “oratory” in Eng-
land, has been loafing on the harassed old
country for war-money and war-orders, and
has taken scarcely a step to appropriate
any of the war profits. It, of course, only
means deferring the trouble, for the bill will
have to be paid out of something else pre-
sently. This co. isn’t one of those that are
making war profits. But it is one of those
that will suffer by and bye because war pro-
fits haven’t been collared for the relief of the
community.

finance cos. which have made up their mind
that a special Providence is watching over
