The directors shouldn’t have done it: they
have rushed the .dividend ahead far too
quickly. They have lodged with the
Treasurer £11,390 (face value) of Govern-
ment debentures which cost them £10,004;
and Tiie Bulletin is pretty certain that if
this stock was written down to present value
the whole £569 of reserves and a lot more
would be wiped out. The Treasurer
recently agreed with trustee cos. to pay par
for stock lodged as a guarantee, provided
they bought it at par. This co. didn’t, and
apparently has no guarantee from the
Treasurer. Anyway, here is the position:
tlm co. made an investment six years ago
which has shrunk in value more than the
amount which has been put to reserves. In
the plainest terms this means, assuming that
the Treasury investment is not guaranteed,
that the directors have paid away in divi-
dends more than the total net profits of the
co. It is a bad start for a trustee co. to
make: the management, knowing how profit-
able a trustee co. is when it gets thor-
oughly established, might ivell have exercised
some self-restraint in the matter of divi-
dends.
From one of the best-known of Australian
working prospectors:—
Whst an enormous area of auriferous country the
early diggers had the run of! Go out from Barraba
(N.S.W.), where nowadays it is all wheat and sheep
and rabbits (a very successful freezing works treat-
ing the latter), and where mining is almost a thing
of the past, and all about among the serpentine
and diorite hills yon strike flats that have been
pot-holed for alluvial; and wonderful tales are
told of some of the finds away back in the ’so’s.
At Wood’s Reef, about 12 miles off, are two old
batteries, and the remains of another. Good stuff
was crushed there, one 100-ton lot returning 800 oz.,
and the mine was worked profitably for years. The
tailings, alas, as is usual in N. S. Wales, have gone
into the creek.
Heaven only knows how much the cyaniding
industry has lost through this practice in the Ma
State. Ancient and weird batteries out Campbell's
River way, near Rockley, that were worked by
water-wheels long before copper plates were intro-
duced, and so long ago that all records have
been forgotten, similarly ran their tailings into
the nearest creek, and none remain for the cya-
nider. In Victoria, ever the more far-seeing State
in matters of gold-mining, the practice was pro-
hibited in very early days, on account of the silting
up of the watercourses; and that wise prohibition
indirectly did more to stimulate the cyanide industry
when it came along than anything else. Nowadays
anyone who runs tailings into a Victorian water-
course is speedily called to account; but in N. S.
Vales, whatever the law says, nobody takes much
notice. You can easily, if you own a. tinpot battery
in the Backblocks, run the residues on to the bank,
so that the first flood sweeps them away. Thus a
heap of tailings large enough to warrant the erec-
tion of a cyanide plant, and the consequent pro-
duction of wealth, never gets a chance to accumu-
late.
In one way it doesn’t make much difference,
because the regulations relating to tailings are so
hopelessly inadequate and out of date that in them-
selves they are sufficient to prevent such an industry
as cyaniding being established on a commercial
basis. So long as mining laws are the outcome of
the ignorance of Sydney politicians, mostly lawyers,
what can one expect, though ? But at making
laws that involve hardworking and deserving people
in unprofitable lawsuits they leave little to be
desired. It is a significant fact that when anyone
makes inquiries about a hung-up mine in N. S.
Wales he mostly finds he must go to a Sydney law-
yer. If not, then it is in the hands of some other
Sydney shark. N. S. Wales mining, outside one or
two big fields, is largely a matter of providing
sharks with suitable tucker. Yet a few dead sharks
on Bondi beach are objected to.
The Port Adelaide Dock Co. has held its
last half-yearly meeting. It was founded in
1878 (Joseph Fisher, chairman), and the
dock was opened by Premier Morgan in
1880. Only one of the seven original direc-
tors survives. The S. Aus. Government is
acquiring all docks and harbors in the State,
and has bought this one “at a price satisfac-
tory to the Government and shareholders
alike”; so the co. goes into liquidation at
once.
* * * *
From Westonia:—
In the Edna May Deep Levels report to November
.30 the directors state that between 8000 and 12,000
tons of ore worth 60s. exist above 500 ft. Now,
what is meant by this? According to official reports
the show is down 480 ft., and what shareholders
would like to have is an estimate of ore reserves
above that level. The tonnage that exists between
480 ft. and 500 ft. cannot be raised by legitimate
mining operations, but only by underhand stoping or
pig-rooting. By the way, what has become of the
high values reported in Deeps bore at 443 ft. ?
According to the manager, the value of the ore at
the 480 ft. level is 605., but assays from the bore
referred to ran into ounces. The public, as a whole,
appears to have forgotten this bore, but many indi-
viduals should have good reason to remember it.
Manager Stuckey has been requested to attend the
meeting of shareholders in Melbourne in order to
explain the present position. Local-director Duff
accompanies Stuckey as a sort of escort; but neither
he nor Stuckey looks forward to this trip with
relish. It is pretty certain both will be asked ques-
tions in quite a different tone to that which greeted
them on the occasion of previous visits. No doubt
the debenture holders, who have the right to acquire
Deeps stock at 255. per share in liquidation of their
debentures, will be particularly inquisitive. Sydney
shareholders who entertained Stuckey in such good
style may also be expected to be heard from.
The demise of Edna May at the 385 ft. level is
approaching. The lode is shorter, narrower and gener-
ally not as good as at corresponding points in higher
levels. The basic dyke has settled the mine’s preten-
sions, and has not permitted Deeps to have any at all.
Edna May will continue to pay 3s. dividends for a little
while, but will shortly find itself in the position of
not having sufficient rich ore to mix with the poorer
stuff. This means reduction of monthly output, and
consequently reduction in dividends. The mine may
pay another 60s. ; beyond that, no'body should guess.
Mary Mac (W.A.) is in trouble. There
is a good treatment plant on the mine, but, as
with most of the Melbourne-run mines, devel-
opment is far behind the mill, and additional
cash is wanted to look for more ore. Local
opinion is that the show is all right, and
that capital is only needed to make it pay.
But in nine cases out of 10 local opinion
is mostly that of the pub and storekeeper.
The newly-installed battery at Mt. Bod-
dington (W.A.) has been given a trial run
and crushing is expected to be in full swing
in a week or two. Meantime efforts are
being made to work up the market.
♦ * * *
The Queensland Government Geologist
(L. C. Ball) in 1011 figured the reserves in
Mt. Oxide at 250,000 tons, but in his report
to the Public Works Commission the quan-
tity has dwindled to 30.000 tons, value 20
p.c., plus a large quantity of lower grade.
The question to be decided is whether Mt.
Oxide can provide a train-load of ore per
day for a railway estimated to cost £400,000.
Corbould of Mt. Elliott reckons the reserves
at 95,000 tons of profitable ore, and his pre-
vious estimates about Alt:. Elliott have been
remarkably correct. But estimation of- ore
in a patchy mine is tremendously difficult;
and no better proof is needed that Alt. Oxide
is patchy than the 3241 tons of 40 p.c. ore
packed away on camels to Cloncurry or to
Normanton, on the Gulf of Carpentaria.
Mt. Cuthbert (Q.) will have its smelter
completed very shortly, so there is a lively
interest in the development of the Mighty
Atom and Kalkadoon mines, which will assist
the parent property to supply ore. Nomi-
nally the ore reserves have not increased
more than a few thousand tons since 1912,
when they were estimated at 150,000 tons
containing 12,000 tons of copper; but recent
work has disclosed ore that has good fluxing
qualities. The Kalkadoon, for instance,
has a run of high-grade ore at the 250 ft.
level for a length of 240 ft.; but the deepest
level (350 ft.) is mainly a low-grade lime-
stone formation with occasional enrichments.
The Mighty Atom has levels at 150 ft.,
250 ft. and 350 ft., but a great amount of
development remains to be done at the lower
levels. Above the 150 ft. there is a fair
quantity of 12 p.c. ore. The most recent
estimate of reserves in the various leases
owned by Mt. Cuthbert is 158.000 tons, con-
taining 12,300 tons of copper. The works
are expected to produce 350 tons of blister
copper per month.
Mammoth Copper (Q.) has been sending
small quantities of picked ore to southern
smelters, but the Board has determined to
sink deeper and do more development work.
Mammoth’s trouble is the high cost of Coke
from N. S. AVales and freight on shipments
of matte to Pt. Kembla. The mines are
situated more than 20 miles from the rail-
head at Alungana.
Corella, Cloncurry, was handicapped last
half-year by increased costs, as indicated by
a ne t profit of £6878 from 9184 tons ore and
509 tons copper. More than £5 worth of
metal was recovered per ton of ore, so it is
difficult to excuse a net profit averaging less
than 14s. The profit with the balance
brought into account gave the directors
£7061, which they cut up bv writing off
£648 for depreciation, providing £3OOO for
taxes and placing £3BOO to an equalisation
reserve. On October 31 the surplus of liquid
assets was £8039, which compares with
£4700 at the end of the previous half-year.
Copper products on hand or in transit were
valued at £28,000. but against this the bank
overdraft was £22,000.
Sid Plain has been ringing joybells over
Main Reef, Maindample (Vic.).* lie has
mostly handled shows with little gold, and has
become expert in chasing .after cheap costs.
But he thinks he is now training for 4oz.
results at Main Reef. Two years plugging
away at 240 ft. has opened up a length 'of
47oft. on the lode, but the shoot narrows
from 2ft. to din. Now it has pinched itself
into a 2in. formation; but as there is the
chance of another shoot of stone ahead, a bit
more prospecting is to be done. Main-
dample has a patchy reputation, hut Plain
is satisfied that the block between 240 ft. and
140 ft. will pan out well. As a side-line he
has a crosscut out for Anderson’s reef, which
ought to have sparkled up long since. As
the country has been bucked about by heavy
faulting a blank has been drawn so far,
though work has gone out 430 ft.
Ministers Livingstone and McLeod are
awake to the fact that Victoria has an asset
in brown coal, for last week they chirruped
to the effect that nothing better than Mor-
well was standing idle under the Southern
Cross. McLeod, when at the Mines Depart-
ment, didn’t realise the fact; but what has
got him apd Livingstone on the go now is
that some unnamed crowd wants to put
£200.000 or £300.000 into handling the
deposits. It wouldn’t be a nice outlook for
gas and electric-light directors if the State
went into the business of making producer
gas and churning out electricity from brown
coal. So a few people are doing a lot of sly
kicking; in fact, there is talk of an open
light for possession of Morwell. Premier
Peacock will need to take a firm stand to
resist the pressure which is being applied to
stop the State from taking control of the
latent wealth of the Morwell deposits.
John Brandon has pulled off the Misima
deal for Broken Hill Block 10, so that
he and Tom Rollason again know the ex-
citement and joys of a mining float. It
wasn’t so rare once. Brandon ought to make
a tidy bit out of the acceptance of the St.
Aignan’s claims by Block 10, and so should
Gordon Lyon and T. Birkbeck out of their
Misima syndicate which held the rest of the
leases that go to make up the new float as
approved by A. B. Ward and Clayton. Block
10 sunk over £12,000 at Misima in doing
hard mining before it took over the option,
but what Gordon Lyon spent in reconciling
the 40-odd interests in the leases can- never
figure in any balance-sheet. The story gOes
that it was like drawing teeth to get Duncan
Mcßrydc to agree that Block 10 should put
up £30,000 to get this Misima spec, going.
Lucky for him that just then he could con-
sole himself with a seat on the fat Board of
the Broken Hill Associated Smelters.
“Larraba”: Excellency Gilruth continues
to provide evidence of the advantage of
employing a horse-doctor to administer an
allotment over four times the size of the
United Kingdom. The latest concerns the
battery at Hayes’s Creek tinfield. The field
warranted a battery. Dr. Jensen urged the
erection of one, and it was decided to lop off
five head of the old Zapopan battery which
has served as a lodging-house for the Wagge-
man tribe for the past 10 years. The next
thing was to get a man to erect the outfit.
A competent mining engineer, now doing his
bit at the Front, applied for the job. He
had put up batteries all over the West, but
he was turned down. Then Oliver, Inspec-
tor of Mines, was ordered to get the battery
up somehow; and as he could not do it him-
self he had to cast around. Finally, the
foreman of the diamond-drill had the job
thrust upon him. He objected that he was
a driller, not a mining engineer; but he was
over-ruled and had to carry on. He did his
best, but the battery won’t work. Efficiency
is fatal in the N.T. Strangman, Day, Holtze,
Boyes, Tensen. Francis. Askins and now
Winters —all efficient and all fired. Isn’t it
time somebody asked something about the
firer ?
Wallaroo and Moonta shares are strong
because the accounts for 1916 are certain
to show a bumper profit. Figuring on
declared dividends of 17s. 6d. per share,
this ancient concern paid a higher rate of
interest on its issued capital than any other
big copper co. in Australia. It is difficult
to say, however, whether dividends will be
increased again to ss. per quarter. War
taxes and high mining costs make a heap
of difference. Development is a matter of
great importance, too, for while the smelter
is asking for 70,000 to 80.000 tons of 9 p.c.
ore, from ISO.OOO to 200.000 tons have to
be raised out of the mines and concentrated
up to the required grade.

A note from George Laurie, brother of
Bill, prospector of Tanami and other Far
Nor’-West fields (Bill’s death, through an
accident on the track, was reported in
Bulletin of February I)
Bill had come in from Tanami one week before
the accident and wired the Mines Department that
he had sunk a shaft 185 ft. and struck heavy water.
He had cut a four-feet lode worth one ounce to the
ton at 80ft. He opened out at 160 ft. to crosscut
tor this lode. Dr. Jensen pieked the site of the
shaft and assured a pound-for-pound subsidy. The
Director of Mines has petrol placed along the road
to Tanapii and is going out with a. jnotor-car as
soon as the wet season permits to sec if the field
warrants n treatment plant. On my last trip I
reported on a route from Blunder Bay to Tana mi
which would traverse two belts of mineral country
and cut off about 200 of the present 500 miles track
to Tanami. This route would make it profitable
to treat the 50 thousand pounds' worth of ore that
has been carefully sampled and measured on poor
old Bill’s leases. Tom Laurie is administering the
estate and will arrive in Darwin on the 16th of
this month. As for me, I’m on my way to see an
eve expert at Melbourne. 1 lost the sight of one
eye through an accident while taking out an official
to confirm mv report on some copper lodes I had found
near the head of the Negri Diver.
What a family of pioneers! And yet they
get less consideration from Australia than if
they poked around doing odd jobs in the
suburbs!
Mt. Bischoff (Tas.) continues to handle
large, quantities of gossan, porphyry and
alluvial wash dirt. The recent 2s. (id. fol-
lows a ss. dividend in June, so there is some
cause for the slackness in shares. Costs
have been up to nearly Bs. per ton, and
that is altogether too high for Bischoff, whose
crude ore is often worth only 10s.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, February 5, 1917.
My Dear Abini.—
From the landing of Captain Cook, in
which the Kindergarten abo. with the spear
is fearsomely large, to delicately-painted
vases and exquisite embroideries is more
than a penny section; yet anyone who
visited the exhibition of Public School chil-
dren’s work held at the Education Office
can trace the journey step by step. -Not
even the persistent leak of Friday morning
could damp the enthusiasm of Mr. Branch
and his band of art teachers as they sur-
veyed the result of last year’s labors.
The visitors wandered from table to
table admiring book-plates and book-covers ;
here, a beautiful frieze, there some sten-
cilled at length they could see
the young hod-carrier of the future eating
the evening meal from hand-painted china,
while the garish electric light was softened
by the latest design in lamp shades. It is
an idea with great possibilities. In the
left-hand corner of the outer room there
Avas a touching little exhibit of three speci-
mens of brush-work on black ground. These
Avere the work of three very young deaf-
and-dumb children; and that makes us
very grateful to a system that opens up the
world beautiful to these little brothers and
sisters.
What about a Girls’ Anti-shirker League
whose members shall be pledged not to flirt
with the men Avho stay at home? Recruit-
ing agents simply waste their breath while
Doris and Lurline and Babs smile upon the
shirkers and sustain them Avith persiflage.
Youth can only be really hurt by youth, and
one frozen shoulder in an organdie blouse
is more effective than a hallful of male
eloquence. It’s about time the girl-shirker
heard a few home truths from her brother
at the Front.
Many waters could not quench the gaiety
at St. Vincent’s Hospital on Wednesday
evening when the nurses laid down their
thermometers and poultices and became
Pierrettes and other frivolous persons. There
Avas a feminine Charlie Chaplin, a lot of
colored lanterns, much supper and music
of various brands. Nurse Fennelly led the
dramatic forces, Nurse Hastie bjat all
comers at euchre, and Drs. Moran and Lam-
rock and Mobbs made efficient M’s.C. The
proceeds—nice plump proceeds, too, in spite
of the rain—are to go towards paying off
some liabilities on the new Nurses’ Home.
Miss IloAvsell, the tactful lady whose
remarkable gifts enabled her to live at
peace with members of the Women’s
Patriotic Club (recently defunct) for 10
years, has taken on the job of guardian
angel at the Soldiers’ Club, George-street.
She has, at last, lost count of the number
of soldiers’ socks that have clicked off her
needles.
Bertha Stock, a Maorilander with a good
voice and most flexible hands on the piano,
is among those who keep the Red Cross flag
flying in New York. The Stock lady is
more than holding her own in the musical
Avorld against all-comers under Old (and
now Very Gradual) Glory.
Last Saturday—a wild and Avatery day
—the N.S.W. teetotallers intended to ex-
cursh down Sydney Harbor and present
their champion, Archdeacon Boyce, Avith a
portrait of himself by Julian Ashton, presi-
dent of N.S.W. Society of Artists. Nobody
Avho knows the history of Julian’s duel
with the Royal Art Society will mistake
this for a weak-kneed attempt to throw
oils on troubled waters. Any oil that is
thrown from Julian’s end will be carefully
set fire to first.
Among the summer visitors who are bait-
ing their hooks with their troubles in the
Kosciusko trout-streams is Francis H. Snow,
the metal man whom Billy Hughes tried so
hard to convict of trading with the enemy.
Snow, an Adelaide man, had other anxieties,
too; for while Australia was trying to con-
vict him of helping the German, the Ger-
man was trying to “pot” his son, who is in
the Flying division. However, so far the
scoring is even, for both the Billies have
thus far missed.
Mrs. Tom Rolin, wife of the new Indus-
trial Court Judge, is a daughter of the late
Dr. Bowker. As a girl she was a raving
beauty, as the Yanks say: black hair, violet
eyes, perfect features and complexion and
uncommonly tall. She still retains many of
those enviable qualifications. The new
Judge himself had also a few looks of his
own in his day.
Mrs. Edwin Geach, senior, passed out last
week. The sudden death of her husband
through a tram accident at the Quay two
years ago was a shock from which the lov-
able old lady did not recover. The Geach
family were ever doers. Edwin, jun., is a
film magnate; Portia Geach has made a
name as an artist, and of late has been much
identified with the Child Study Association.
She and a few other girl food-reformers
have started the Natural Food Cafd in Eliza-
beth-street, where pure wheatmeal and
wholemeal in various forms will be the lead-
ing lines. Another sister (one of the early
victims of appendicitis) was a charming
verse-writer. Two other sisters featured the
gospel of .the Hat.
Taking in a dose of ozone at Manly is
Cecily, daughter of the Speck’s ex-Minister
Teddy Muleahy. An illuminating circlet
adorns the left hand, placed there by a re-
turned warrior, a son of Hobart’s Arch-
deacon Ritchie.
The late E. W. O’Sullivan’s youngest son.
Leo, is wearing the trimmings of the Field
Artillery. One of a> bolting gun-team gave'
him a scalp wound last week, but the injury
isn’t serious enough to keep him from taking
part Over There.
A grand-daughter of oldest Sydney, Mrs.
Annie Parkyns, died at Brooklyn, on the
Hawkesbury, recently, aged 86. Grand-
father was Captain Anthony Fenn Kemp, of
the old Rum Corps; and father welcomed
last century from his house and store_ on
what is now Waters’s Corner, where King-
street bumps into George-street. Being im-
plicated in the Bligh rebellion he went to
England as a witness in the Johnston court-
martial, and, returning, lost all his grants
and leases. Afterwards he went off to Tas-
mania, and stayed there till he was 96. I
don’t know where he went then.
There is sorrow among the workers at the
Red Cross Book Depot, for Mrs. O’Gorman
Hughes, whose recent sudden death caused
such a shock to her friends, was one of the
original workers, and the first of the. devoted
little band to pass over the Dark River. 80
far there have been mostly engagements to
chronicle at 100 Pitt-street, but, alas! these
days gloom comes close upon the heels of joy.
When the last mail left, Captain Cleave,
who in times of peace is magistrate at the
Children’s Court in Sydney, was having an
overhaul in what an officer-pal described as
the Epsom Dry Dock —a war hospital in
pleasant Surrey. “Nothing serious.” said
the officer-pal. “He has been at the Somme,
and is mostly suffering from being 46.” To
this County of London Hospital had just
come a big winter consignment of men suf-
fering from “trench feet,” among them an
Australian black brother who had got the .
trouble so badly that he seemed likely to
have to go about on stumps for the rest of
his days. I don’t know whether he could
get any consolation from knowing that
“Birdy,” most loved of commanders-in-chief,
has been laid up with the same trouble —a
not very usual one among generals. But,
then, ‘.‘Birdy” isn’t a usual general.
The Helmore family, of Christchurch
(M.L.), got a lot of sympathy recently on
the death in France of Ernest Ilelmoro.
who was a lieutenant in the Sherwood ,
Foresters. He went into Sandhurst last
year and passed out with honors. One
week in the trenches —and his bit was done.
Before the strife the young man did duty
as one of the Liverpool aides.
The sympathy of Red Cross circles, as
well as of Sydney artists, is with Dorothea
Barclay, who received news of her mother’s
death in Tasmania on Thursday. Miss Bar-
clay is director of the Red Cross Toy In-
dustry, and for months now has been
battling early and late at the Ash-
street workshop to make this very practical
repatriation scheme a success. Two of
the unselfish toiler’s brothers have died
within the last 18 months; and what
made this last blow particularly cruel
was the fact that Miss Barclay had
at last been persuaded to take an
urgently-needed holiday, and was to have
left on a visit to her mother on February 6,
in company with Miss Ethel Stephens, who
was herself bereft of her mother a few days
ago.
The Y.M.C.A. Snapshots from Home
League got this application the other morn-
ing from old Billjim at the Front: —
Please send me a photo, of my baby, to be born
about Feb. 27.
That’s lining up in the queue for the early-
doors all right, isn’t it?
An Australian singing woman who is
waiting for the clouds to lift writes me
that in dismantled and sand-bagged Venice,
where the canals smell high but it isn’t
etiquette to mention it, the cafe waiter now
demands payment before the traveller eats
his food. The Austrians are everlastingly
bombing the watery place from the air, and
crowds of cunning Venetians were eating
well and escaping payment in the periodical
stampedes until the c.o.d. rule came in.
If you wish to prove yourself one of the
“are nows” and not one of the “has beens”
you may no longer eat your breakfast egg
the way you used to. The egg of fashion
now lies sideways in an oval eggcup, and
your attack is on what used to be its flank.
Well, why not? And the silver sideway set
will come" in for a wedding present, too.
One woman’s way of giving a shove. She
stood near the busy lifts of Sydney’s most
popular tea dispensary the other afternoon
(and I suppose it wasn’t her first appear-
ance), and as each male thing happened
along a white cliook’s feather was with-
drawn from a capacious apron and deposited
in the “civvy’s” nearest pocket. The “civvy’s”
private thoughts of the lady would only bear
publication on a sheet of asbestos.
A Maoriland engagement concerns Nora
Brandon, of Wellington, youngest and pretti-
est of the three sisters of Flight-Captain de
Bathe Brandon, D. 5.0., M.C. Miss Bran-
don went to England to help the war along
and met Reginald Birch, Ist Dragoon
Guards. Now she hopes to help Reginald
along. Another sister married a Hawkes
Bay squatter called Nelson and a third,
Lucy, is usually amateur lady golf champion of
M.L. Another engagement of war-time note
is between Lieut.-Colonel Hugh Stewart, of
the Canterbury Regiment, and Alexandrina
Johnston, of Liverpool. This ’groom-elect
is Professor of Classics at Canterbury Col-
lege (M.L.) in normal times. MiSs John-
ston has made some sort of a name for her-
self at the Liverpool Repertory Theatre.
Rose, eldest daughter of the McCartney
Rookes, of Manly, and grand-dnugliter of
General Rooke, was married at St. James’s
last week to Robert Buchanan Donnell, son
of Major Donnell, of Adelaide. The tall,
fair bride was in a gown of white cachemire
de sole, richly trimmed with crystal beads,
the sleeves being formed of bauds of pearls.
The long Court train was of tulle finished
with thick ruchings of silk; the tulle veil
fell from a band of Limerick lace fastened
with orange blossoms; a hi a shower bouquet
completed a fascinating bridal costume. Four
bridesmaids were froeked alike in dawn-pink
chiffon taffeta with tunics of Limerick lace.
They carried pink i> and wore black
sequined Oriental caps v/ith hanging, jetted
beads. After the wedding -banquet at the
Australia the newly-married went off for a
walk-about before sailing for their home in
Singapore.
There was a novelty at the wedding of
Miss Ilingsch and R. S. Stobo at Newcastle
the other day. As the bride and bridegroom
left the church a number of Y.A.D.’s (the
bride had been one) formed an arch of
mops and brooms, and the first telegram to
arrive was handed the bride in a dustpan.
Miss Alice Bowman’s bungalow at Palm
Beach went up in smoke last week. It was
a charming seaside home, with big airy
living-room, sun- and shadow-trap veran-
dahs, heaps of artistic embroideries and
rugs brought from afar, and the most delici-
ous views of bush and ocean, with Barran-
joey winking his other eye in the distance.
There were thrilling episodes about a cas-
ket of ancestral jewels and an insurance
policy; but I’ve told you quite enough.
Palm Beach is not up to the Florida insti-
tution, but it has its Charms and is gradu-
ally attracting a brainy community—Gar-
rans, Thrings, Curnows, Breretons, Bul-
mores and so on. It was from Miss Bow-
man’s cottage that President Lister-Lister
caught many of the charming seascapes he
showed at the Royal Art Society’s last
exhibition.
The drawing-room of the Bartletts’ New
York apartment in the new comedy, “Fair
and Warmer,” at the Criterion is a medley
of crude colors that sneer bitterly at each
other. The mustard shade, appropriately
enough, gets the last word. The play is
written for, by, with and to husbands, and
all the husbands in the audience greeted it
with exceeding great joy. Dress isn’t the
long suit of the two imported ladies, Misses
Atherton and Royton. A correct English
accent never seems to go amicably with
n6glig6s, anyway. Mr. Marmont, a finished
elderly knut, is the most debonair being
that ever floated into Sydney. But how
we’re going to win a war with things like
“Fair and Warmer’* in our midst I don’t
know.
The go-slow virus seems to be invading all
sorts of places. The telephone girl has it.
The stately pace of the duchesses in the
millinery department has become even a
little more stately; and they look long and
lovingly at their pencils before they begin
to make out your bill- February is a go-
slow kind of month, anyway. Perhaps we’ll
all get a move on in March.
There is an interesting American woman
in Sydney this week —Mrs. Graham, who
has a home in N’York. She is one of the
heads of the American Needlework Guild,
which, having affiliated with the Queen Mary
Guild since the war, has collected and
despatched hundreds of thousands of gar-
ments to suffering refugees from Belgium,
France and Servia. Mrs. Graham was one
of the movers in establishing “work centres”
in Paris and Lyons when the war broke out;
and to this forethought of energetic and
practical American women many a little
seamstress in those cities owed the where-
withal to keep body and soul together
through the first awful months of chaos and
misery.
Major N. C. Lockhart, who in pre-war
days used to devote his grey matter to the
service of the Crown Law Office, aud who
went to England on the Government House
case, has recently been appointed to the
Headquarters Staff of his brigade in France.
Brig.-General Lee and his wife, who used
to be the popular Memie Town, will arrive
in Sydney in a week’s time. The Brig.-
General is to take up his duties as State
Commandant on the 17th.
Gaiety and beauty and money, the indis-
soluble trinity, have been Hocking to Manly
to nibble tea-cake. On a recent Sunday the
crowd included Mrs. Jimmie Osborne, who
used to be the beautiful Mercia to Superbus
Knight’s Marcus. She had a lovely young- .
ster in tow—a future Billzac. The Fred
McMasters and the Philip Mortons and
“Baron” Brown from Newcastle were also
playing around. The twin Macarthurs were
with the Frank and Henry Osborne dames.
By the way, one wonders if it is a sudden
religious scruple, or merely the weariness
caused by the informal dances on Saturday
evenings, that is responsible for the absence
of knitting among Sunday four o’clockers.
In a recent packed lounge only two votaries
of the purl and plain were visible, and one
of them sported a swanking set of tortoise-
shell needles.
After three years in England, Miss Nell
McQuade, of Tarana, is making for Aus-
tralia again. When the llohenzollern got
busy the lady rolled up her sleeves at St.
George’s Hospital, where she has been work-
ing and smiling ever since. However, she
finally tore herself away, and when last
heard of was how-de-doing friends at Singa-
pore.
There is in Hunter’s Hill a man of middle
age who has never been in Sydney nor
travelled on a ferry boat. The nuts of that
sanctimonious little burg are always trying
to rob the poor man of his title to
fame, and one night, by means of strategy,
they lured him on board a boat that was
tied up to the wharf. The moss-grown
celebrity thought it was some new kind of
side-walk. But before the rope could be
untied he realised the dreadful truth, scat-
tered the boys like chaff on a threshing-floor
and made one wild leap for the shore. lie
hit it hard at several different points. But
he saved his reputation.
News from the Fog tells of the engage-
ment for a special high-kick and low-neck
revue of Gwen Williams, only chick of stout
knight Surgeon-General Williams. Miss
Williams, who was launched in the profes-
sion by actor and Mrs. Matheson Lang, and
later made good with the Laurence Irving
Co., is a niece of the Monty Severns.
During the trip up the Northern coast to
the Clarence River in that fast and com-
modious steamer, The Daily Emetic, as soon
as the passengers have completed their share
of the programme the boat herself heaves to
and allows all who are capable of it to drop
their lines and fish for the deep-sea sc-hnap-
per and the ruddy rock-cod. This
Arcadian custom is on a par with that of
the South Coast trains, which about this
time of year pull up for half an hour or so
while engine-driver, firemen, guards and pas-
sengers leap out into the bush and catch
blackberries.
Pitt-street at a quarter to nine is a pro-
cession of workers, women predominating
these days: flappers, alert young business-
women, and, alas! the middle-aged woman
who, in so many cases, has given to the
Empire’s cause the sons who were her sole
support. I wonder how they manage the
lunch question! Many of those women
board; some have tiny one-room-and-share-
the-kitchen flats. In most cases they live
too far from the city to return for the mid-
day meal. That means at least 6d. a day
for the unsatisfying sandwich and tea; and
6d. a day amounts to £6 in the year—quite
a large sum when the average weekly earn-
ings are in the neighborhood of £2 2s. per
week.. I wonder if more large firms
couldn’t adopt the fashion set by the Bank
of N. S. Wales, which provides a luncheon
free of charge to its women workers (the
male members of the staff pay something
nominal), and the menu includes soup, hot
joints, entries, sweets and tea or coffee.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

Do You Get Your Share of the
World's Great Store of Pleasure?
NOT one of us but has his share of the worries and troubles
—they are the heritage of mankind and come unsought.
But the pleasures —we have them only as we make them.
All about is the world’s great store *of the materials of happi-
ness. From it we may choose and build the beauties and the
joys of our lives as we will.
The wonderful art of music!—what material is here for
tinting with rose hues the shadows of life! Here is happi-
ness such as all the world s treasure of gold cannot buy. And
as much of it is yours as you will take!
The Pianola, oldest —and newest —of player-pianos, makes
easy the path to the heights of this beautiful art.
. A ith the Pianola you may hear music and you may play it
—enjoy passively or experience the supreme pleasure of
creating.
The Pianola provides the great musical scope of the piano
and a.technique—a playing ability—that is sure and efficient;
comprehensive of every pianistic effect.
Such an instrument as this will add so much to your own
life, to the lives of your family and friends, its value may not
be estimated in-terms of pounds. But in reality its cost is
very moderate.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, February 5, 1917.
My Dear Yakiue, —
The compact A.N.A. show is clattering
along in the centre of the huge Exhibition
Building, surrounded by space, draught and
echoes. It is not a large show, but
it is at least intense. Some girls
occupy a hollow square and make
lovely undies just as fast as the whirring
machines can embroider and stitch. Young
men who look lit enough for the firing-line
are attracted by the glass-case shows of
beautiful wax ladies dressed lightly in rib-
bons and lace, and hang over the polished
counters of the enclosure. One of these days
I will beat one of them over the head with
my worst umbrella and make a recruiting
speech.
For those who want ordinary amusement,
there are open-air concerts at which singers
get relaxed throats and much applause. The
prizes for drawing, painting and carving
attracted hundreds of competitors and thou-
sands of admiring (and cursing) relations.
The paintings are skied for safety in the
balcony corridors, and all the studies in the
“nood” are concealed at the very far tran-
sept behind big pillars. If there had been
a dark cellar down a rotten stair they would
have been there. I applaud the wisdom of
those responsible. A lady in a Chloe atti-
tude stands at a window against a blue
velvet curtain; she has a face like the last
of the barmaids. If viewers recover from
that, they may gaze at a siren higher up or
a depraved cherub lying asleep beneath a
purple coverlet. The cherub has three
writhing limbs showing, so he may be a
dyed octopus for all I know.
.A huge exhibit is made up of military
things. There are clothing outfits for any-
body from a private to a field-marshal or
an admiral. Rows of Australian-made wag-
gons are there; also a tiny hand-cart sort
of kitchen, a huge grey-green pontoon and
complete Red Cross ambulances. A boot
section has returned soldiers learning
“clicking” (a sort of leather cutting) and
other detail work. The faded battalion
colors show on the sleeves of their piled
tunics. The Broken Hill mining people have
a huge steel exhibit hemmed in by heaps of
ore and fenced with huge posts of “blooms.”
That our Australian trees have a wonder-
ful future if we can stop people from burning
them is shown by the spread of Australian-
made furniture. Buckley and Nunn make
an artistic display with a heap of lovely
timber in the shape of a dining-room suite.
The set is a fraction of the appointments
lor a five-roomed bungalow, and any girl
can acquire the lot by owning a young man
with £l5O. Of course, the boy is not abso-
lutely essential. Still the canoodly appear-
ance of the armchairs and lounge suggest
that he would not be superfluous either.
Mrs.. Holden, wife of the Harbor Trust
chief, is a busy woman these days. She
takes command at Government House of the
Overseas Club’s special brand of Red
Crossing and works like the busy head of a
sewing-room, patrolling from table to table.
As well as this she runs a smaller Red Cross
of her own at the Harbor Trust offices.
The Mayoress’s Patriotic League is get-
ting busy again in the Town Hall. A mon-
ster meeting was held on Thursday, when
the situation was reviewed and the most
recent shipment of comforts was placarded
for the encouragement of all. Some items:
20,000 socks; 11,000 leather vests; 7000
flannel shirts; 6000 handkerchiefs: 2000
towels; 11 tons of oatmeal; 444 tins of
milk; 100 cases of foods; 50 cases of
smokes; and honey by the ton. Fruit,
“Tommy” cookers and post-cards were
thrown in as padding. There is no more
packing of kits for local soldiers.
In answer to a cable from the Australian
Comforts League in London, the Ilennessy
dame’s League cabled £5OOO to be used
there. A Button Day to refill the treasury
will strike Melbourne on February 23.
Charlie Mason, son of the late Speaker,
who was Associate to 'Justice Hodges before
the war, sends a cooee from France saying
lie has been given the crown and star of a
lieut.-colonel. He landed at Gallipoli with
the first Anzacs as a major. A flying frag-
ment of Turkish lead stopped further fight-
ing or promotion for a while. But after con-
valescing in England and later in Australia
he felt ready for more. Mrs. Charlie takes
breakfast calmly in “blockaded” England—
has been doing so for two years now.
Widower Edward Trenchard crossed to
Maoriland last week to meet his elder daugh-
ter (the Greene Kelly dame) and her chil-
dren. She is coming back from England to
shelter in the paternal home at Toorak until
the submarine plague is beaten. Knight
Greene Kelly is doing his bit somewhere.
You probably remember that he first met his
Australian bride at sea.
Budden, the tiieless commissioner who
has been looking after Billjim’s comforts,
is returning to Melbourne very soon. The
Patriotic Teagues are getting excited about
it in advance, and the Mayor has pro-
mised a civic welcome.
Adelaide’s parcel:—
At last there is some visible result of the agita-
tion against German schools in S. Aus. Four of
them decided not to open after the Christmas holidays
last year. Under the Act, the rest should have
gone out of business last May; and in no circum-
stances may they remain open after the end of this
year. The pupils are being absorbed by the State
schools, and, in some instances, where the teachers
are highly qualified and have made a special decla-
ration of allegiance, the Education Department is
finding occupation for them in non-German centres.
Meanwhile the German J.P. continues to flourish, and
two of him recently called tenders for inquiry by
the Justice Department. A Eudunda carrier named
Frederich Wilhelm Gustav Marchall (no, he’s not
an Italian) was up before them. This brute was
so revoltingly cruel to a sick pony that the liga-
ments of its hind legs were completely severed, and
by police orders the animal was destroyed. The
S.P.C.A. prosecuted, and the defendant was fined£2!
The Museum’s Director, K. R. Waite, is in the
unhappy position of being able to say “I told you
so.” Last year a few specimens of the black rat,
apparently imported from Egypt, made their appear-
ance here and were promptly denounced by him
as likely to very soon become a menace to fruit-
growers. At the time no one took much notice,
but now the visitors have so increased and multiplied
that in some districts they are in thousands, working
havoc amongst the fruit crop and eating marrows,
beans, peas and other vegetables wholesale.
Brig.-Gen. Gordon, at one time our military com-
mandant and familiarly known as “The Army,”
who has held a munitions job in Scotland for some
time, has got a step up. Though he is apparently
still munitioning he is on a higher grade. Tie
begged hard for a job under our Defence Depart-
ment, but couldn’t get one.
The Show is to be a feature of September. The
old grounds, which have been in the hands of the
military for some time, will be available, and as
the hiatus since the last Show is so great it is
suggested that this year it should be a five days’
affair.
T. R. Scarfe’s will has been lodged for probate
and sworn at just under half a million. With the
exception of some bequests to charities and employees
the estate goes to relatives.
One Jack Union, charged with assault at Port
Pirie, pleaded as provocation that the complainant
had addressed him as “President Wilson.” That
was enough. Case dismissed.
The Zoo has just received a consignment of animals
from the United States, the first cargo of the kind
to come here since the outbreak of war. Elks,
skunks, a couple of Alaskan bears and a good many
parrots are amongst the Yanks. Some Huns appear
to have arrived too. and are doing what they can
to destroy the city park lands and gardens. Hoses
have been twisted and damaged and fittings stolen,
young wattle trees uprooted and destroyed, and grass
plots damaged.
The Government’s scheme for the erection of a
new hospital is in temporary abeyance, owing to
the stinginess of the loan market. One of the first
things that is to get badly-needed attention is addi-
tional accommodation in connection with the mental
hospital. Parkside is overcrowded, and there is no
proper provision for the grading of patients, so that
people who are only slightly affected have to lie
sent to Victoria for treatment. The suggestion is
that a receiving ward should be erected in the
Adelaide Hospital grounds, and run as a branch of
the institution at Parkside.
An Adelaide pastor who took his large flock for
a picnic on S. Australia’s most recent holiday says
the festive scene looked like a “miniature battle-
field” about half an hour after lunch, and the
wounded took six hours to tend and cart away.
Stretchers, ambulances and a doctor or two with
morphia all put in strenuous work. Ptomaine poison-
ing was the trouble, but the cause is in dispute.
It must have been the salmon; it can’t have been
the wine.
The Yuille girl who has just become
engaged is the daughter of Albert Yuille—
not Archie. She is the niece of Archie. There
are three Albert Yuille daughters. One is
married to a Chadwick hereabouts; the
second is the wife of architect Hudson, npw
in the trenches; the third is the one who
has just become engaged in England to
Hume Turnbull.
Big Lieut. Edgar Marsden, of the sil-
very tenor, fell ill in France, was sent to
hospital, and fell captive to his white-
capped nurse—an attractive English girl
whom he rashly' describes as “the one
woman worth talking about.” She is
yearning to see Australia. Edgar (his late
father was Government Architect here)
was a Brighton youth who dropped his
Permanent Artillery sword to try his luck
on the English stage, where he figured in
“The Chocolate Soldier.” The war started
while he was holidaying here, and he
promptly cleared out to settle it.
Australians are keeping the parsons busy
in London. . Sydney Fairbairn. of the
Grenadier Guards, son of Stephen Fairbairn,
has married Nancy Cunard, only daughter of
the great shipping family. Young Fair-
bairn’s pretty cousin .Tess has said “Yes” to
Alec Phillip-Russell, whose people have just
returned to Australia. An Anzac is good
enough for her. Jess is daughter of the h reel
Fail-bairns, whose fighting son Beau is also
dashing into matrimony with Miss Hastings,
a kinswoman of the Marquis of Hastings.
The' orange-blossoming of Miss “Gipsy”
Burns, daughter of -Sydneyside Col. Burns,
and Sub-Lieut. Cyril \\ illiams, of the Brit-
ish Flying Corps, and in his spare time third
olficer of a I*, and O. liner, wasn t by any
means as sudden as it seemed. Two voyages
ago Miss Burns came to Melbourne on her
bridegroom’s ship, and it is suspected that
some preliminaries were settled then. As
a matter of fact, there should have been
another wreath at a I’, and O. masthead
quite recently; but there wasn t. 1 liey
did it more quietly still. Do you know the
boat?
Charles Gavan Duffy, who has found
another snug refuge in the Clerkship of the
Senate, has listened to 40 years’ gabble at
the big Parliament House in Spring-street,
lie was one of the Vic. Parliamentary offi-
cials who commenced to receive a Federal
salary when the State gas-tank became the
gasometer for the Commonwealth. life has
seen the longest service of any official in
the institution, though his record is closely
approached by that of Robert Mitchell, the
housekeeper of the Reps., who has been
there for 37 years practising the art of
being affable to everybody.

Lawyer Tom Hogan is in Mount St.
Evin’s hospital recovering from a sudden
serious ’itis. He is a buoyantly-healthy
young man, and is making rapid recovery.
Droopy visitors have to be warned off, as lie
refuses to have sympathisers moaning around
his bed.
The Mackays are back from the West,
where big-hearted. Sam dropped £.">oo into
the Westralian fund for the widows of
fallen heroes. Here in Melbourne he is
shouting aloud for the country to send
Bigjohn Forrest to the Imperial Conference
to represent Australia. Mackay offers to
make one of 20 rich men to contribute
£lOOO to cover the expenses of the jaunt.
The widow and daughter of old-time
cricketer Murdoch are reported to have been
killed by the explosion of the munitions fac-
tory near London. Mrs. Murdoch was one
of the Bendigo Watsons, and had lived in
England for years.
Excellency Liverpool, of Maoriland, plays
tennis with violence ; and at a recent tourna-
ment given by the vice-regals while inhabit-
ing Heaton Rhodes’s place at Christchurch
he was runner up. Next day, at the Wild-
ings’ lawn party, the little Countess took
the palm and the prize along with her part-
ner. Of course, the partner must have
helped.
A great circle of friends was shocked
by the inexplicable suicide of young Levin-
son, son of the Levinsons who are of the
merchant tribe of Benjamin. A lad not
quite 17, and looking only about 14. adored
by his parents, he left his mother brightly
that morning, whistling as he went. In
the afternoon he shot himself.'
The Berry Bank Concert Party, formed
of girls from the Uni. Conservatorium, has
under the management of Miss M. C. Mack
been touring parts of the hospitable Western
District to raise money for the Y.M.C.A..
Red Cross, Mayoress’s Patriotic Fund and
Comforts for the Light Horsemen in Egypt.
The girls met at Berry Bank homestead on
January 9, and afterwards gave concerts at
Lismore, Cressy and Colac. Then they were
motored to Mortlake, Willaura, Terang,
Warrnambool, Camperdown and Peter-
borough, and everywhere had crowded
houses. The tour ended up with a concert
at Peterborough, where the girls spent an
enjoyable weekend as guests of Mrs. A.
Affleck. The Berry Bankers were Misses
Thirkwall. Rous. Stirling. Mack and Jordan,
while Miss Pennicott,. though not a Conser-
vatorium student, threw in frolicsome songs
which thougn quite unknown to the Con.
scored encores always. Roughly speaking
this was the haul: Lismore £l7, Cressy £l3.
Colac £l7, Martin ke £3O. Willaura £2O.
Terang £24, Warrnambool £7O, Camperdown
£27 and Peterborough £20 —altogether a nice
little catch made by girls who reckoned they
could be happier helping brother Rilljiin
than having their usual midsummer loaf.
The fatherly Defence Department
gathered together its clerks and workers of
every kind and gave them a picnic on Foun-
dation Day. About 1000 toilers, etc., got
aboard a chartered bay boat and went to
have a look at the forts and other exhibits
and sideshows. The new State Commandant,
Brigadier-General Sellheim, postponed the
war for the day and took his wife. . The
party lunched on board and dined on land,
near Mornington, where the younger mem-
bers found a big bandstand with a lovely
floor deserted; so they set to and danced
confusion to the Germans. The picnickers
got back in time to read the evening news
about the war ; it was still on.
Billy Hughes is beginning to wear his hair
like Lloyd George, lie is letting it wave in
graceful ringlets over his coat-collar, and
even has the characteristic straight side-
piece of grey. I know all about George’s
hair, as I have watched it blow in the wind
through numerous dumb speeches on the pic-
ture screen.
Genial J. INI. McNally, who is both butter
magnate and water-colorist, has rediscovered
the beauties of the upper reaches of the Salt
Water River. He Ims done some excellent
sketches of its more rustic parts, and tells
entrancing stories about the good fishing to
be had there. True, true —McNally can toll
an entrancing story about anything.
Florence Rockwell, now starring in “The
House of Glass” at the Royal, is a replica
of Edna Keeley, Allen Doone’s leading lady.
Florence has Edna’s curls, Edna has
Florence’s Irish-Ameriean twang-brogue, and
both rush about the stage in the. same way.
I really don’t know how they do it—or why.
The late Henry Brac.v captured one
feminine heart by his strict attention to
dress. I met the mourning chorister last
week and listened to her with interest. She
remembered a fire at Her Majesty’s in Syd-
ney—no great damage done, but water and
ashes everywhere. Bracy’s expression of dis-
may when he came to rehearsal and viewed
the ruins was comic. “What a beastly
mess!” he shivered and looked down in a
horrified way at his brilliantly-polished
shoes now flecked with ashes. The stage
girls regarded him with adoration. He had
an air partly paternal, partly, lover-like, and
altogether charming. This led one day to a
fight on the rehearsal stage between P.racy
and a jealous, lovesick theatre employee.
After the duel, Bracy smoothed out his
lovely clothes and said frowningly: “I did
not so much mind him calling me a cad:
but I could not bear to be called a dirty
cad !”
Chicago papers are fussing over our Elsie
Mackay, as her Melbourne friends can see
for themselves by clippings sent to father
Sam and pretty stepmother Fan Dango.
Elsie is playing Shakspearian heroines under
the management of Ziegfeld. One paper calls
her “a new stage beauty of rare type,” and
declares that she makes “an enchanting
Anne Bullen.” Another declares that she
“promises to become an Australian Miss
Ferguson—or better.”
Melba’s protege, the young Honolulu
lad Correthers (I believe that’s really how
you' spell it), has fallen on his feet. He
had a knack of designing clothes, dress
materials and stage decorations, and has
now received an offer from «a well-known
Londiin firm at a good salary. The young
man helped, Fritz Hart with his opera pro-
ductions and gave lessons in gesture and
stage grouping at the Albert-street Conser-
vatorium.
The Broadmeadows Mending and Soldiers’
Aid Society, which foregathers and works in
Prell’s Buildings, Queen-street, is modestly
proud of letters of praise received from Sena-
tor Pearce and the State Commandant.
Pearce remarks: “It is a work that one does
not hear much about; but though it is not
as obvious as some of the others, it is just
as important and necessary.” Mrs. Ridgwa.v
is secretary of the committee of large-hearted
women who go along to patch the old breeks,
mend the tattered shirt and cobble the holes
in the socks. The soldiers bless them for
their homely help.
I am tipping that Margaret Anglin will
soon make another Australian visit. She is
with Ivolker in America, and Ivolker has
been tuning up his Melbourne friends with
letters lately. They don’t mention a pro-
bable trip—that would be too crude alto-
gether. But a sudden revival of correspond-
ence is always a sure sign that a mummer is
packing up. Well, we are ready for Mar-
garet Anglin, anyway. She left very pleasant
memories behind her.
Yours, sincerely.,
JOHANNA.

“Slybacon”: Re “Rab Cjjbiip’s” question
(B. 11/1/’l7), “When a stockman cracks a whip,
what makes the crack?” The crack is produced in
precisely the same way as the report from a dis-
charged firearm. In the case of a firearm the rapidly
ejected and expanding gases from the explosion
create a partial vacuum in the air, and the rapid
return of the air to fill this vacuum produces the
uproar. So it is with the stockwhip. The tail or
cracker is instantaneously jerked from a curved to
a straight position, thus creating a small partial
vacuum, which produces the crack. A whip cracks
better with a whipcord lash because this lash is
usually lighter and somewhat thicker than the whip
tail, thus creating a greater displacement of air
and consequently producing a louder crack.
A DIGNIFIED REBUKE.
“Wot did you do when 'e called you a dirty German?”
“Just treated ’im with silent contempt on the ’ead with a bottle.”
“Bilbie” : Put these amongst the records
of horses electrocuted by one flash of lightning. In
1910 ten draughts were turned into a paddock at the
rear of the Union Hotel, Trundle (N.S.W.). A
storm coming on the horses made for shelter in
timber -standing at a wire fence. A ‘‘ball of
fire” was seen to pass along the wire, and
afterwards seven horses were found dead and two
paralysed. These latter partly recovered, but they
were useless. In 1914 we were harvesting at A.
Morgan’s farm, five miles from Trundle, when a
storm with vivid lightning broke. Four horses
were unhitched from the stripper, and the driver
made tracks with them for the stable. On the way
a flash of lightning electrocuted the team. The driver
escaped, cleared out to Gallipoli, was invalided home,
but did another bunk and is now safe in the trenches.
“Adelaide”: Enter this for the easy-deatli
competition. Vv T as out in the scrub for a picnic tea.
Went down to the creek with a girl to fill the
billy. Saw a rabbit in a kind of open-work bush.
Slung the billy at him, quite playfully, saying “Get
out of that!” as one might to a dog. Result, one
instantly-dead rabbit, caught on the back of the
neck by the rim of the can. (And the girl thought
me a rather heartless slaughterer, of uncanny skill
with weapons.)
“F.N.T.”: Somewhat scornfully “Pick”
remarks (B. 18/1/’l7) that silo-experts “build upon
the theory that green fodder, even if damp, can be
safely filled into a silo,” and asks the cause of
the trouble with certain horses that passed away
“after eating a musty portion that had been forked
aside.” Well, it’s a simple matter. The unfortunate
brutes suffered from the same sort of frightfulness
that would be likely to overtake “Pick” if he ate
a meal of mouldy fruit. If air is excluded from cut
green-feed fermentation is arrested, and thereby the
Sugar and other digestible items, which during the
normal process of putrefaction are the first to leave
for parts unknown, are retained in the mass. The
feed is chaffed as finely as possible, to assist its
tight packing in the silo ; and if it is a trifle ripe
it is improved by being moistened, while a wet
crop may be used as it is with perfect safety. For
perhaps 12in. from the top of the silage, however, a
crust of mould forms. The farmer must discard
this when starting to use the feed; and such a crust
will of course form again and again if a regular
daily layer is not scraped off for the animals. Just
like any other chaffed green-stuff silage is only good
for stock when fresh. “Pick” fed his on rotting
offal.

“T. Messmate”: As an entry in the re-
sourceful Australian competition I run in Jim, stable-
man in one of the pubs of a North-West town. He
was ordered by the missus one day to take in some
clothes, which the laundress had leff to dry in a
far corner of the hotel yard, about two chains away.
Now it had rained a good drop a few days before
and that portion of the yard was under about
a foot of water. Jim had a look, and presently the
missus coming out saw him saddle the horse in the
stable, mount and gather in the duds, then take the
saddle off and put the horse back. She exclaimed:
“AVhat did you get the horse for, Jim?” ‘-Well,
missus,” he replied, “I wasn't going to be beaten
by a drop of water like that.”
“Toby C.”: For many years black foxes
have been fairly numerous about Monaro (N.S.W.),
but not until recently have albinos appeared, and
now comes the white fox. Albinos occur also in the
magpie household. I have never seen a live one,
but in Itolfe’s pub at Cooma there is a good stuffed
specimen to admire between drinks.

“Eryander”: Found the other day, on
Maroubra beach (N.S.W.), the corpse of a very rare
visitor to these waters —the sea-snake Pelamis bicolor.
Deep black above and bright yellow below, its ap-
pearance accords well with its extremely venomous
capabilities. There are about 50 species of sea-
snake, all very poisonous, but they are practically
confined to the torrid zone, and it is unusual to find
one so far south as Sydney. The body of these
snakes is laterally compressed to almost blade-like
form, the lungs occupy nearly the whole length of
the body, and the nostrils are on top of the snout,
so that the snake can breathe w'hile concealed under
the surface. A valve closes the nostrils when the
reptile dives, and its big supply of lung-air enables
it to stay long below. There is an imitation Pela-
mis, non-venomous, but shaped and colored precisely
like the other. One of these bit me when I was
swimming off Labuan, and record-time was made to
the beach, where I found bv the tooth-pattern that
there was no venom to be dreaded.
* * *• *
“H.V.E.”: The brown snake, which repro-
duces by means of soft-shelled eggs—like those of
a gohanna or other lizard —deposits them under
leaves, brushwood or other d6bris. The diamond
snake, which breeds in the same way, goes one
better. This reptile always makes a nest for its
eggs and coils itself affectionately around them.
Strange to say, the temperature of its body rises
during the breeding season, so that the task of
incubation is not left entirely to the heat of the
sun. One nest fashioned by a diamond snake which
lived under a large (and of course empty) beer
case near a sleeper-cutter’s camp, was made up of
stringy-bark, leaves, dry grass, bracken and scraps-
of newspaper. Into this residence, spherical in
form, the snake (about 10ft. in length) packed
itself wonderfully, with its black coffin-shaped head
resting on top of the great coils and the beady
eyes regarding intruders with a cold, malevolent
stare.

“Toby C.”: The sassafras tree possesses
virtues overlooked by “Condamine” (B. 11/1/’l7).
The bark makes an excellent preparation for
tanning furred skins, imparting a finished appear-
ance seldom obtained by the use of alum, saltpetre
and similar tanning materials. A strip of the dry
bark thrown into the pickling jar or cask com-
municates a peculiarly agreeable flavor to corned
beef, mutton or pig. Finally, the experienced bush-
man swears by “Sassie bark” in cases of dysentery
or diarrhoea. He scrapes off the soft, moist coating
inside the bark, takes about a teaspoonful for a
dose, and recommends the remedy to the first white
man he meets.
“Tap”: Let me tell “Lou” 'and “Bex” that
they both miss the point in dealing with the curse
of mallee shoots. “Bex” is certainly right when he
says that a running fire on the stubble is the best
method of killing them; but he does not mention
the easy and effective way that is- much used in the
newer Mallee of getting a running fire. If he will
attach a light pine roller well back under the comb
of his stripper, suspended by a wire at each end,
and, say, from six inches to a foot from the ground,
according to the height of his snags, he will find
his stubble lying close enough to the earth to burn
without the aid of a fire-rake. This also saves his
team a lot of unnecessary friction in walking through
standing stubble, and keeps his stripper-comb free
of chokes, if the roller is properly adjusted.
* * * *
“Mendoza”: Horses, cows, poddies, goats
and. giraffes are dead easy, but if you want to stop
that hog from violating the neutrality of the dog-leg
or brush feneff and absorbing the product of the corn
and melon patch you will proceed like this. Take a
piece of tough hardwood about three inches wide
and an inch thick, as long as the width of the Un-
clean One’s jowl, and bore a hole about one inch in
diameter near each end. Then take two pieces of
bamboo, hazel, or any tough sucker about four feet
long apd butt one into each hole and wedge up.
After that take a half-nelson on the hog, and after
passing the hardwood base under his jowl lift on
the sprits until the hog begins to settle down by the
stern. Then cross them over the back of his neck
immediately behind the ears and lash them together
at their intersection with a piece of wire or raw
hide. Fixed'in this way the Rejected of Judah is
at perfect liberty to graze or drink or climb tele-
graph poles, but the flimsiest kind of barricade puts
the high and permanent kybosh on him in one try.
Moreover, no hog, if regularly constituted, can back
out of this yoke without leaving his head behind.
* * * *
“E.S.S.” : Feople are always cursing flies
and mosquitoes, and yet they drive away or kill the
friends —birds, lizards and bats—that would protect
them from such pests. Invaluable are the little
geckos which enter houses and run about the walls
and ceilings, catching the flics. I have watched
these little lizards moving about on my body and
legs as I lay quietly on a bunk. They are wonderful
fly-catchers, superior to the police fly. In places fre-
quented by bats there is no necessity for such
measures as burning cowdung, for the bats quickly
clear the rooms of mosquitoes, and if unmolested
and allowed to use the house as a shelter in daylight
hours they will keep them clear.

DAD'S BATH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dad knocks upon the weather-glass
That hangs behind the kitchen door,
Then on the cloud above the pass
He wastes one solemn minute more.
He gazes on the windward sky, with speculation in
his eye, then calls to mother from the path:
“Ole girl, I think I’ll have a bath!”
“Where’s Bill ? Hi, Billy, come you here!”
And Mum commences waltzin’ round.
“Where is that Bill? Oh, dear! Oh, clear!
The villain never can be found
When wanted most. Hi, Bill, come quick. Prepare
the b’iler. Here’s a brick. Chop up some wood
and split a lath. Your father’s goin’ to have a
bath ! ”
Bub’s calling Nell. Hear Nellie rouse:
“I on’y got one pair iv ’ands.
Who’ll drop the panels for the cows?”
“You come right here,” the Bub commands.
“My word, you will cop out for this. You gotter
fill the b’iler, Miss, and do it slippy, too, be
gosh, for father’s goin’ to ’ave a wash!”
It’s Mum again, abusing Bub:
“You lazy good-for-nothing, try
To move for once. Bring up the tub.
Dan left it there behind the sty.
Dan, get a towel from the box, and cut some soap,
you stupid ox. Say, did yous hear your father
say he’s goin’ to ’ave a bath to-day?”
Bub burns her hands, much water’s spilt,
Mum slithers on the soap, and then
Dad comes out, gathered in a quilt,
And peeks up at the sky again.
“A change,” he says. “It ain’t so ’ot. I dunno,
p’raps I’d better not. I’ll wait, Mum.” (It is
father’s joke. The old man never has that
soak!)
VICTORIA.

“Karee” (from the Front) : Re “TiakiV
par. (B. 12/10/T6) anent the gentle Maori tunga
wood-grub, is it any relation of the Queensland
“booyum,” which lives in young gums and looks
like a lollystick painted in pink and-white shades?
Binghi quaffs them alive, but I’ve only indulged in
them roasted on coals. When the skin cracks and
turns a rich brown is the time to banquet. The
taste savors of prawns. A memorable fight took
place on Stewart Island (Q.) with nulla nullas and
language, between my young brother Jean and Garry
Mop’rah’s youngest olive branch, for the possession
of a billyful of the luscious worms, gathered by
the noble black man's progeny. Just as Jean was
forcibly proving to Garry junior the superiority of
the white race, Eileen, sister of Garry, collared the
loot, and climbing a convenient “apple” negotiated
the lot, in spite of the fiery speeches and nullas
hurled at her by the now allied warriors.
* * * *
“Simon”: Re sassafras timber (B.
11/1/’ 17). The quality of this wood was discovered
30 years ago by two Englishmen who, as a result
of the Victorian tariff, came here to manufacture
saddle-trees. Tasmanian sassafras ( Atherospenna
woschata) was hit upon as the .most nearly approxi-
mating English beech, and consequently the most
suitable for the work. Time has vindicated their
judgment. However, they needn’t have gone to
Tassy for it, as this and another sassafras (fancy
name Doryphora sassafras) are plentiful in both
Victoria and N. S. Wales.
* * * *
“Sarto”: Whilst splitting a red-gum log
the other day a lizard-like apparition about Si
inches long dropped out of a cavity in the-wood.
The hole was about nine inches long and three-
quarters of an inch in diameter, and his nibs had
evidently lain there for some time, judging by the
markings from his skin on the vegetable. He was
prettily tattooed on the back —black, with a wide
zigzag band of dirty yellow from the head to begin-
ning of tail, with yellow spots thrown in. The
underneath part was all white, as also were the
four tiny hands (or feet), whose appearance, and
the owmer’s general behavior on being released, de-
noted that it was his first introduction to the out-
side world. In- shape he was about the ugliest beast
I've met. His head was like a snake’s, and he had
a broad, flat tongue, the front half of which was
black. The tail narrowed where it joined the body
and looked as if it had been stuck on as an after-
thought. The only part that took my fancy was
the eye, which was a beautiful, shining bronze orb.
When I pointed a bone at him up went head and
tail simultaneously, and with mouth wide open the
little devil presented a vicious appearance. ’ What
was it ? And did it go into that hole in the log or
was it born there ?

“Wandi”: In Central parts the flood-
bird (Australia’s largest cuckoo) jumps the nest of
ihe crow, throws out the latter’s eggs and deposits
its own in their place. The crow hatches them, and
feeds the young flood-birds until they are pretty
well fledged, by which time Mrs. Crow begins to
have suspicions. Finally she consults Mr. Crow.
“Look here, Nick,”
she says, “do you
call them crows?”
Nick sidles along
the edge of the nest,
eyeing them criti-
cally. “Well, I’m
blowed,” he says at
last, and he looks
hard at Mrs. Crow.
After a heated dis-
cussion the little
aliens are roughly
and wrathfullv fired
out to battle for
thems elves. The
pink-eared duck also
appropriates t h e
crow’s nest, consti-
tuting thereby a re-
markable anomaly in
bird life, for the
crow’s sharp eyes
are always on the
lookout for eggs
secreted in such
nests as the average
duck makes in rank
grass. Unlike the
flood-bird the pink-
eared duck turns the crow out and hatches its
own eggs. A duck laying and sitting in a twig
nest built on the branch of a tree is in itself a
curious departure from the general habit of ducks.
* . * * *
“Corio”: The harness-cask was empty,
so, with the object of providing fresh beef, a
kangaroo-hunt was promoted. A deep ravine flanked
the camp, and it was resolved to drive the animals
thither, where they could be easily brought to a
standstill and despatched by the dogs. We mounted,
a party of three, and when the first ’roo was roused
we headed him after a gruelling chase into the
valley. Escape was impossible save over the
ravine; but with a leap for freedom the ’roo
cleared the trap and alighted safely on the opposite
side. Upon spanning his jump with a ball of twine
it was discovered to measure 35ft.
* * * *
“Hay”: “Two Seas” wonders why so
many ’roos are found hanging dead with their hind
legs through the top wires of fences. The ’roo
never jumps to any height when scared or hunted,
but travels with a long low swing which looks more
like running than jumping. When he strikes a fence
his leg goes between the wires, and his frantic efforts
to get free only tightens the twitch. When the coun-
try was first fenced I have seen dozens of them
caught that way. Likewise emus.
“P.A.Q.”; Blow into Shorty Buckland’s
pub last week, and met my old friend Timi Tapu.
He was gazing wistfully into an empty pintpot.
Tlmi’s son is at the war. “Hallo, Timi,” I said ;
“how’s the boy getting on?” “Oh, he’s kapai,”
said Timi. “I just get te retter from him ; but, py
gorry, he te tarn riar, my son, I tink.” “How’s
that?” I asked. “Werra, rook here. How te way
this for te tarn rie? Ho say in te retter when te
airship he come arong te British lie’s got te gun
what fire te five mires in te air. ■ He can’t do it,
isn’t it?” “That’s quite right, Timi,” I said. “They
can fire five miles up all right.” “By gorry,” said
Timi, “I didn’t know he could do that.” “But
risten to this one —he te .proper rie, I sure. He say
te man he go up in te barroon for to make it te
rook out. By and bye he send te message that te
prooming Teherman he’s twenty mires away. Then
bang goes te gun and te Teherman, 20 miles away,
is provven to herra quick.” “Biit that’s not a lie,”
I assured. “Werra I don’t know,” he rejoined. “That
te funny ting anyway. But rook here at this. Now
ain’t it te tarn rimit? He say tay got te big boat
what he can swim under te water and fire te bressed
gun under te' water too. I pet that one te rie,
wasn’t it?” “No,” I said, that’s not a lie —all
these things are true. And they can do more won-
derful things than that.” “Werra, if tem tings
true,”* mused Timi, “that te funny way to fight.
You know I thought Henare he pu’ my reg. But,
py gorry, if that te proper war to have these thing—
werra, you know what I think: te Maori war he
must ha’ been onry te pranky pig-hunt.”

(Tas.), on January 2 gave premature birth to four
bull calves. She had an argument with another lady
the day before and the calves were born dead.
Anyone beat it?

“E.S.S.”: A general favorite is the Aus-
tralian reed-warbler ( Acrocephalvs australis), a
plain brown little fellow who is persistent with his
pleasing tune, singing in the night as well as through
the day. Excepting the blue wren, who sometimes
delivers a nocturne from the roost, only one other
Australian bird, the bush-lark, sings at night. His
song has been declared by ornithologists to be louder
ahd more melodious than that of any . of his European
relations, Gregory Matthews, referring to the grey,
shrike thrush, the reed-warbler, the golden-breasted
whistler and the butcher-bird, says: “I prefer any
of these native songsters to the English nightingale
or thrush, while as for the reed-warbler I consider
he stands in a class by himself.” He is a familiar
habitant of river-sides, reedy swamps and sedgy
lagoons and lakes, often sociably sharing the same
spots with the grass-bird, or marsh-warbler, a little
brown chap whose weird and plaintive voice is
principally heard at night-time. The reed-warbler
is a friendly fellow, venturing into gardens after
insects; and while rendering excellent service in that
way he charms all hearers with his rich whistling
notes. To the Southerners he is a joyous harbinger
of spring; to the Northerners a cheery herald' of
winter.
“J.R.K.”: When mosquitoes are thick
around your camp, and no cattle manure is handy,
take two grass-trees, place them parallel with one
another and light a fire between. It will only
smoulder, but the smoke is the most deadly thing
on insect life that 1 have seen.

“Amateur Biologist”: It is a curious fact
that when a Dutch yellow rabbit is crossed with the
Himalayan variety—white body, with black legs,
black ears and black snout—the offspring are all
agoutis, the color of the ordinary wild rabbit.* This
exemplifies a reversal to ancestral type. If the
individuals of this progeny are mated, the propor-
tional composition of the next generation is: Agoutis
27, yellows nine, blacks nine, tortoiseshell three, and
Himalayans 16. Thus several characters result from
the two different grandparents. As a result of the
mating of the white variety of mouse with the
chocolate variety, the chocolate variety alone is pro-
duced ; on breeding together the individuals of this
generation the offspring are chocolate and white in
the proportion of three to one. If the whites of
this generation are bred together, whites only are
produced. But on breeding together the chocolates
of the same generation, chocolates and whites in
the proportion of three to *one are again produced,
and so on.
“Merrigal” Amongst the best-known Cen-
tral Australian forage plants the clustering saffron
green-fingered Parakeelia is easily favorite. Stock
fall over themselves in pursuit of it and camels fed
on alternate nights with it need but few other
rations on the voyage. The vegetable choice of
homes is in the vicinity of Warriwell, and it is
found nowhere on the surface of the continent out-
side Centralia.

THE BACK LOG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The grates that they have in the houses in town,
And their quaint imitations of wood,
Make a fellow feel cold when he’s asked to sit down
For “a warm” with the resident brood.
There’s room round the hearth for a pair and a half,
And no chance for the cat and the dog—
In the places / know you could roast a big calf,
And a dozen sit round the back log.
The fireplace was wide as a skillion, and deep ;
’Twas plain and old-fashioned, maybe;
But cosy and snug, and no trouble to sweep,
And ’twas always inviting to me.
With the fire in the centre there seldom was need
With a maul or with axes to slog;
lou could drop in long sticks as you’d drop in a
reed,
And behind them a solid back log.
The v?alls were whitewashed with wood-ash from the
bed
Where we roasted potatoes and yams;
Salt meat was hung round on stout nails overhead,
W T ith bacon, bananas and hams ;
The chains and the pothooks hung down from the
beam—•
At its elegance none stood agog;
But there we could 101 l in sweet comfort and dream
In the light of a blazing back log.
We lined the wide front and we squatted each side,
There was nobody left in the cold ;
And many a laugh then went echoing wide,
And many a story was told.
When winter winds wailed round the cornel’s at night
Or the landscape was shrouded with fog,
Or when icicles formed on the leaves in the bight—
Oh, then we enjoyed the back log.
N.S.W.
