“Jean S.”: The musical evenings pro-
vided by the orchestra at Melbourne Royal
are worth the money. The other night,
when I looked in, a very fair house was
hugging its seats in evident enjoyment of
the melodious fare. The menu included
Suppe’s “Morning, Noon and Night’' as
hors d’oeuvre, followed by a bit of
“lolanthe,” Flath’s “Summer Dream,”
Ivan’s valse “La Russe,” Kenyon’s “Flower
Suite” and two helpings of “Gorsave.” For
me, however, the musical performance was
marred by unseemly interruptions. Several
people, who looked and occasionally talked
like actors, had an altercation about a glass
house or something of the kind. I am
rather hazy about the details. The discus-
sion was not sufficiently protracted to ex-
cite much attention, but it was very annoy-
ing while it lasted. I wish the J. C. Wil-
liamson management would reprimand the
intruders. If they are not spoken to imme-
diately, they might abuse their small privi-
leges and get the delusion that they are the
entertainment.
“II.O.” : With most of its young men at
the war and its young women sewing—er—
seeds of kindness on their behalf, Melbourne
Repertory Theatre has not been flourishing
of late. A performance of “The Amazons,”
which was to have taken place last month,
did not come to pass, and nothing is certain
regarding future programmes. Meanwhile
Repertorians are agreed on one point—that
it is up to them to show their appreciation
of the work director Gregan McMahon has
done, and arrangements are now in hand to
give him a benefit. Also, while talk of estab-
lishing a Shakspeare Memorial Theatre is
loud in the land, and press barrackers are
shouting the name of imported Allan Wilkie
as the man for the job of director, many less
noisy persons are agreed that if there is one
man more than another entitled to such a
position it is the Repertory Theatre direc-
tor. Other considerations apart, Wilkie’s
incurable habit of hanging cheap necklaces
of gags around the English classics should
put him out of court.
“Jwosp” : Said David Belasco in his fore-
word to “Training for the Stage” : “Intelli-
gence is desirable, but not necessary. The
merely brainy is never a great actor on the
stage. The heart is greater than the brain.’
Not a word about the hide!
In a London revue, “Vanity Fair,” Gwen-
doline Brogden is singing a ditty, “Anzacs,
supported by a chorus in a “costume typical
of the Antipodes.” Said costume is in grey
and orange, and in cut resembles that worn
by Belle Diamond, the Pride of the Prairie,
or some such person, in 9435 wildwesto-
dramas. Its most prominent feature is a
hat that makes Kink O’Malley’s headgear
look like a mere candle-extinguisher.
“Stargazer”: Rarely—never lately have
I left the theatre after witnessing ,an Ameri-
can play without feeling something of the
moral abasement that comes upon a simple-
ton when he realises that the plausible
gentleman to whom he-has just trusted his
five-pound note is a spieler. Take “Ihc
House of Glass,” for instance. Up to the
last act I liked the play for what it was
worth—a clever bit of mechanical designing.
Then it was made apparent to me that 1
was the victim of a shameful take-down,
and that the author was an ethical con-
fidence man who had sold me a gold brick.
When his Ex. the Governor chipped in at
the tail-end of the adventure, and put every-
thing right for Mrs. Flarvey Lake, I per-
ceived that he might have been persuaded to
intervene in the interests of so influential
a dame at any stage, and the dramatist had
kept this fact dark to delude us into the
belief that a tragedy existed where there
was really no tragedy at all. In any case,
one always feels there is something rotten
in the state of a drama that calls in a pre-
viously-unused character to mop up the
mess in the final five minutes.
Patterson is a distinct anti-climax, and Ihe
House of Glass” is less worthy of respect
than a trick matchbox.
“S.S.” : Florence Rockwell, now appearing
at Melbourne Royal, has much to commend
her as an actress, but I don’t give her a
10-to-one chance of making a hit m this
city. She can do numerous things pretty well,
but she can’t dress, and it is many yeais
since an actress who was not also a cute
frocker has taken a fair grip on Melbourne s
ardent young affections. A pink dress Miss
Rockwell wears in “The House of Glass
is 'sufficient to destroy all feminine respect
for the wearer, and no actress can expect
to score in Melbourne nowadays who hasn t
the girls on her side in a body. Another
offence is Miss Rockwell’s manifest inability
to wear her own hair in a becoming way.
One must be a very good actress to be
contemptuous of the hairdresser. One must
be magnificent to be contemptuous of tne
dressmaker. But not even a genius can
afford to be contemptuous of both.

The Inky Way.
Got out his last issue, Walter Cummyi
Macdougall, proprietor of Balmain (Sydney)
Courier. Over 30 years ago lie founded it
and kept the press running with a single eye
to the well-being of his noisy water-suburb.
He stood for Parliament once, but the elec-
tors reckoned he was more valuable at his
inky job and refused to excuse his absence.
* * * *
Brig.-Genl. Ernest Williams, C.M.G. in
the last list, is another newspaper man who
reckoned the sword mightier than the pen.
In much younger days he attached himself
to the Ballarat Courier, and became its
editor. After leaving his impression on the
cushion on which the late E. -T. Bateman
sat so long, Williams sighted a better place
in the town clerkship of Ballarat. For a
time he has been acting-Commandant of the
Vic. military district.

“Ilassan’s” call to the craft: —
The new American manager of a picture-films co.
gave a number of newspaper men a dinner on a recent
Saturday night. The general tenor of the speeches
was to the effect that the host had fed the pressmen
handsomely and deserved well of the country. One
speaker declared that, the Government •should subsi-
dise an American film co. because it purposed pro-
ducing Australian stories with lankee actois. He
did not explain why. Another speaker remarked
that of course, while all criticisms of films weie
perfectly honest, the space allotted to each company
was in direct ratio to the amount of advertising.
I believe that the second statement was perfectly
true. The host replied that the rule concerning
advertising was that a certain amount of the profits
of a firm was allocated to advertising. Perhaps the
profits would not be very high to begin with; but
he hoped and believed that the press would help
the film firms to make huge profits, because the
huger the profits tire larger the advertising. ...
Now this was an exhibition of just that sort of
thing which gives newspaper men a had name. It
lias been generally noted that if you want am thing
from a pressman you must feed him or give him
cigars or whisky. This salesman got the pressmen
together to put them under an obligation to him,
and they, to use one of his Americanisms, “fell for
it.” The queer part of it is that if an advertiser,
to gain any advantage, had attempted a money bribe
to most of these same men, he would have earned
only enmity.

“J.F.H.” : We now number a noble duke
his Grace of Manchester —among us!
True, we might not have known it had he not
been obliged to stand his trial in London for
not having disclosed the fact that he was an
uncertificated bankrupt when making certain
purchases. Then it came out —his Grace was
“expecting £lOOO from his editor m
America,” subsequently amplified by the
information that he is the “London Society
Correspondent” to the Hearst papers. The
previous Duke of Manchester was a Presi-
dent of the Royal Colonial Institute and,
visiting Australia at the time, was roped in
(for a consideration, it was said) to formally
open the Silverton Tramway Co.’s railway to
Broken Hill. It was a glorious drunk, I
know.

From a winged Billjim :
Beatrice Harraden, novelist, is doing her bit as
librarian in the Endell-street Hospital, London,
where some hundreds of us are convalescing. It’s
unique in its way—staffed entirely by women, and
none the worse for it. “What do they read?”
someone asks her. “Books on England,” she says,
“bushranging stories, and their own poets.” Some
“will read nothing but Nat Gould.” Garvice,
Oppenlieim, Boothby, Haggard, Corelli, Dickens and
Clonan Doyle are among the also rans. Kipling
is the most popular of the poets, and many of the
fellows get off his gingles by heart while they are
being patched up. “Society” novels are never asked
for ; while Shakspeare, Beatrice is delighted to find,
“holds his own surprisingly well.”

“Quarter Single”: The petty jealousy of
daily journalism discovered by “Fx” (B.
25/l/’l7) is a matter of routine so far as
the Melbourne morning dailies are con-
cerned. The practice of each is to ignore
the other utterly. “Fx” is disgusted with
the Age because, in referring to Florence
Sandes’s work in Servia, it omits to men-
tion her brother Johnny Sandes, _ who was
for years on the Argus. Rut the Argus
soon' got a bit of its own back A few days
later it reviewed “Pip” Schuler s excellent
but ejaculatory history of Gallipoli, Aus-
tralia in Arms,” and, while admitting it
to be the most complete account yet pub-
lished of the Australian army’s doings in
the war, scrupulously refrained from men-
tinning that Schuler is the son of G. r. H.
Schuler, editor of the Age, and that young
Schuler had been on the staff of that paper
and is now in khaki.

“Bad Australian”: Recently Kultur
struck a nasty snag in this sinful city
(Buenos Aires) over the publication of a
number of plainly-expressed articles by
French and Belgian writers on the destruc-
tion of Louvain University, Rheims Cathe-
dral and several other victims of Hunnish
criminal lunacy. A hen these articles
appeared in the Nacion the German Min-
ister rushed a demand on the Argentine
Government that a stopper be put on the
chief organ of public opinion in this country.
While he foamed on the doormat he was in-
formed in studiously polite terms that the
only steps the Government felt at liberty to
take was to oblige the paper to publish the
German official denial of the charges. Then
the foamer foamed worse than ever and
called upon every squarehead in Buenos
Aires to cut loose from the Nacion, where-
upon they immediately withdrew their ad-
vertisements and subscriptions like one Hun.
The Minister then took a long lager and
a second helping of sauerkraut and sat back
and smiled. But when the next issue of
the Nacion came out with a notice on the
most conspicuous part of the front page that
it would not accept cheques drawn on the
Banco Tranatlantico Aleman in any circum-
stances, that Minister was the maddest and
most astonished diplomat in South America.
He made a new and immediate appeal to
the Government, only to be told that it had
no legal right to oblige any person to accept
cheques on any bank whatever. Then the
great German Transatlantic Bank was
rushed by its depositors and forced to close
its doors' for the day. That same evening
a whipped Hun crawled along to the good
Argentine citizen who runs the Nacion and
prayed for grace, which was duly accorded
him —along with a quantity of good advice.
* * * *
“B.B.” : It is not often that a Governor
has an opportunity of makings full-blown
thistle-eater of himself, so perhaps Liver-
pool, of Maoriland, is to be excused for
indulging himself the other day. He was
“reviewing” a hospital-ship staff when he
observed a newspaper man close handy.
Promptly breaking loose, he wanted to
know what the and why the, etc. Then Ink-
fingers (who, as a matter of fact, wasn t
bothering about the vital information which
his Ex. said was “confidential”) had to
accompany an escort. The pressmans
notes (they were about a meeting of a road
board) were taken from him, and he was
given a sailor’s farewell. Of course it was
a good “story” for his paper (,Bun,_ Christ-
church) and he made the most of it. The
next act in the comedy was his Ex. rush-
ing to explain. But nobody wanted him to
explain. The laugh was over.
Another Bulletin contributor, Walter
Kilroy Harris, has got the Military Cross.
This hustling young Australian came from
Newcastle, from which town, canvassing for
ads., he once overlanded to Adelaide in a
sulky. He wrote a book about that journey,
and, characteristically, sold out the issue.
Harris ranks as lieutenant at the Front,
and is a young man who will take a lot of
stopping.
* * * *
Jack Hilton, who has held the wheel of the
Westralian Labor paper since the wobbly ship
was launched, stands down after something
over three years’ toil. He put in a lot of
good work, 'but the man at the wheel on a
Labor journal has little chance of getting a
sound foothold. Hilton is one of Australia s
champion chessmen —can play 10 simultane-
ous games with his hands on a typewriter,
while dictating three games over the wire
for an international contest, and filling in a
cable message showing the market price of
jute at the time of going to presss.
“Rye Dry” : The country editor who won
fame by bringing out a paper without the
assistance of reporter, comp, or machinist
has had his feat challenged by a mere city
tenderskin. Martin Brennan, editor of
Variety (Sydney), has just produced 84
pages of mostly theatrical stuff. He wrote
practically the lot, “set” nine-tenths of it
and secured and wrote the ads., and is now
his own collector of accounts.

“Sardonyx”: Without defending the pre-
valent literary use of “o’-” for “of,” I may
point out to “S.T.S.” (B. 25/l/’l7) that
this form not only foes reflect a colloquial
way of speaking—the Scottish —but that, it
was in the past quite customary in familiar
English, in which “a” was also frequently
used for “of,” as in “Ivate-a-Whimsies” and
“John-a-Dreams.” Anyhow, we need not go
abroad. The average Sydneyite speaks of
“a couple of days” as “a-kuppla-dies.
“S.T.S.” says that “where the ‘f’ really
wants dropping one has to write ‘kinder
and ‘sorter’ to give the effect.”. Hardly an
improvement upon “kind o’ ” and sort o .
The latter form involves only legitimate
elision; the former interpolates the vulgar
Cockney “r.”
. * » *
“Yinsonlee”: Progress is taking great
strides in the Chinese Republic since the
death of President Yuan Shih Kai, and
every month brings forth numerous radical
changes. Yuan fettered the press with a
strict censorship, but the more popular Pre-
sident Li Yuan Hung, laying much stress
on the educative influence of newspapers,
has removed all the former restrictions. To
illustrate what progress journalism has made
in China, I may mention that the doings ot
the Parliament are now almost immediately
public property in the remote interior.
Whether the press will retain its new free-
dom I don’t know. Neither does anybody
else that I’ve spoken to on the subject.
TO A FRIEND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A faded brain, seeking to rouge its thought,
Here finds its toilet-table neatly spread ;
And here a thousand freaks and fancies caught
Rest each within a narrow, inky bed.
All that philosophy and science tanght
Is here to some plain words and phrases wed.
I love you for your deep humility,
And for your large, dogmatic, fearless pride;
Your information boundless as the sea,
Your swift suggestion eager as its tide;
And mostly you have told the truth to me
When others frequently and wildly lied.
I come to you because I do not know,
I come to you because 1 cannot spell;
I run my finger down from row to row
Seeking the secrets you alone can tell.
The half-remembered fancy seems to glow
When you accord it life and taste and smell.
All precious gems are yours, all golden spoil.
All treasures of all ages round you stand.
All wealth of harvest, corn and wine and oil,
• You give to him who seeks with open hand.
I come to you for herb and fruit and soil,
And all that ever was on sea and land.
Men print a thousand books for men to read,
Hut you forever keep a place apart.
The world may ponder Shakspeare’s magic screed,
Or pluck the thousand blooms that freshly start
From storied records rich with faith and deed ;
But you, O Dictionary, hold my heart!
N.SAV.
GEORGE STREET.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Eight years ago Australia had a sub-
stantial surplus of exports over imports and
paid £9.341,108 during the 12 months in
(Customs revenue. To-day the excess is on
the other side, and in the last seven months
£9,493,330 has been paid in duty on the
foreign goods. Production is falling, imports
are increasing, debt is going up with a bang,
and then distracted people wonder why men
are out of work.
Professor Woodrow Wilson has been
quoted lately as giving tlie best definition of
the German Empire :
It is a Federal State composed of four kingdoms,
seven grand-duchies, four duchies, seven principali-
ties, three free cities and the Imperial domain of
Alsace-Lorraine, these lands being united in a great
corporation of public law under the hereditary
Presidency of the King of Prussia. Its Emperor is
its President, not its Monarch.
A profound knowledge of law is a terrible
handicap to a national leader in these days
of anarchy and sudden death. While he is
looking up the exact status of his murder-
ous neighbor, there is another shriek and a
thud outside,, and some more blood trickles
in under the library door.

The Victorian railways having gone from
bad to worse, and the party politician not
being game to say what is the matter, the
Government has decided to import Tom
Johnson to make a report. The problem is
a commercial one; and Tom Johnson, being
an engineer, naturally knows all about com-
merce, just as Alec Peacock, being a secre-
tary of mining companies, knows all about
astronomy. All that is the matter with
Yic. railways is that, owing to early land-
grabbing and later-day centralisation, nearly
everything is carried twice as far as it ought
to be, while, owing to the ‘cursed slowing-
down, expenses have increased enormously.
Here is a nice little table showing how work-
ing costs have raced ahead: —
Per Mile Worked. Per Train Mile Run.
1902 .. £666 46.07 d.
1912 . . 971 59.70<1.
1913 . . 986 60.52 d.
1914 . . 1032 61.73 d.
1915 .. : 1101 66.47 d.
A State has no need to import a Tom John-
son to tell it what happens in such circum-
stances. Either it must increase its railway
charges enormously, too, or it must lose
money. And if it increases its railway
charges it reduces the chances of successfully
settling the country, cuts down the revenue
and prospective revenue, and thus only gets
deeper into the bog.
Of the three or four countries in the world
actually making money out of the war
Maoriland is one. Yet she is wading deeper
and deeper into debt. Out of her immense
war profits she could easily have kept from
borrowing. Instead, she lias gone on the
insane doctrine that “posterity should bear
its just share of the burden” —and sent Joe
Ward to England to, among other things,
“address war-loan meetings.” Naturally
lou is in his element telling the British
public to dip into its pocket. But he can
be relied on not to explain that, though
owing millions to a mother that badly
wants them now, Maoriland is making mil-
lions out of the old lady and not repaying a
penny.
Joe Cook, talking last week of the Lloyd
George Conference to be held in London,
said that “the Prime luinister should repre-
sent Australia.” Asked if he meant by “the
Prime Minister” Mr. Hughes or a possible
successor, Joseph replied: “I mean the
Prime Minister of Australia.” But why?
The Prime Minister is not necessarily the
best man for every special job. Suppose N.
S. Wales had to be represented at the con-
ference and Jim McGowen were still
Premier, could it be decently argued that
McGowen should go if Holman was avail-
able?

supply made from Tasmanian shale, the

Speck’s House of

and borrow money for

dubious the House of

reckoned it good

chances are that the
inspector of mines is
not a specialist in

must surely be in
Tasmania for the
benefit of his health.
The Commonwealth
Government had bet-
ter now consider the
business on its own
account. At any rate,
it had better ask
George Georgeson, sec-
retary of the Metal
Exchange in Sydney,
whether the business
is worth considering.
Georgeson spent about
five years trying to
save Newnes’s Com-
monwealth Oil Cor-
poration ; and that
experience shouldn’t be wasted.
From an old political hand: —
The British Government has not asked for Hughes
or Willie Kelly or anyone else to attend the Im-
perial Conference or take a seat on the War Cabinet
or whatever it is. A representative of Australia is
requested to attend, and in the circumstances of
the moment the Commonwealth could with more
propriety and dignity be represented by the High
Commissioner than by a politician. Fisher, three
times Prime Minister, is on the spot drawing a high
salary for just such work as this, and he ought to
be earning his pay. Every week makes it plainer
that the Federal Parliament is moribund; and if
the Legislature has any respect left, the odious
intrigues of the hour will be at least partially ended
by passing a resolution directing the Government
to make use of Fisher and- hold a general election at
the earliest possible date.
Hughes formed the first branch of his
“National” Federation at Geelong and has
lately been bestowing marked attention on
the district. Locally it is believed that the
Dominating Personality will make a bid for
the Corio seat at the next elections. At
.present Corio belongs to A. T. Ozanne, a
Laborite who enlisted. When Ozanne put on
khaki A. C. Morley, a Melbourne barrister,
who was the selected Liberal candidate,
reckoned it a fair tiling to stand aside, and
he has been standing aside accordingly.
“THE LAST BRIDGE HELD, BUT”—
In Melbourne anxious inquiries are being
made as to whether baby bonuses are to be
subject to the Amusement Tax.
While Jensen, Minister for the Navy, is
proclaiming his intention to “deal firmly”
with demarcation disputes at the Cockatoo
Dockyard (Sydney), the demarcation dis-
pute between Hughes and Cook was raging-
under his nose, and Jensen’s method of deal-
ing with that consisted mostly of wondering
what was going to happen next.
William Smith, mainstay of the Yic.
Fainters’ Union, is giving Joe Hannan,
M.H.R., a friendly go for the Federal Labor
selection at Fawkner. That electorate
chiefly consists of the two State divisions,
Albert Park and Prahran. While the Park
League has long chosen ex-Premier George
Elmslie as its M.L.A., the Prahran seat has
never yet been taken by Labor, and each
election sees a different candidate put up
against sitting member Donald Mackinnon.
It thus seems possible that Smith may find
enough supporters in the uneasy east to
balance the Hannan poll in the conservative
west; so a Bill Smith may yet push a
Priminister out of Parliament. For there
still are rumors that Hughes thinks he has
as good chances in Fawkner as anywhere.
Jack Cain, who has won the Labor selec-
tion ballot for Jika Jika (Yic.), comes
from the _ Yarra Bank with the Socialist
party behind him. He did about five years’
soap-boxing for the red-rags, and then became
sedate and sat with the Northcote Council
for a term. He is now organiser and Trades
Hall delegate for the Clothing Trades Fede-
ration. Membrey, a Liberal, holds the seat
that Cain is after.
After taking the Government’s advice,
planting the specified varieties of fruits
suitable for canning, and waiting patiently
for over five years for a return, settlers in
the Vic. irrigation areas find that the can-
nery isn’t there. A few settlers got up
early and placed their peach crops with a
Melbourne jam firm at £lO a ton. The
others can merely see their crops rot. To
get a cannery going would cost from £20,000
to £BO,OOO, with the necessary advances on
stocks till the wares are placed. The
growers can’t do it, so the Water Commis-
sion should pour itself into the breach.
Dead, Robert McNab, the ihan who blazed
the track which led Maoriland to adopt com-
pulsory military service. Six or seven years
ago McNab, after losing his seat in the
House of Reps, (he was Ward’s first Min-
ister for Lands), went to England and saw
what was coming. Returning to the Wintry
Isles, and finding Parliament fumbling in
the dreary old way with Defence problems,
he started out on a crusade up and down the
country, wrote a letter once a week to The
Bulletin, converted the public, and put
backbone into the Ministry. Within a year
compulsory service was a fact. McNab'had
a long and honorable record in political life.
But it will be through his work as a student
of Maoriland’s earliest history that his name
will live longest. In this field he took first
place, sparing neither time nor money at un-
ravelling the story of a buried past. He
was the eldest son of an old-time Australian
pioneer who settled in Otago away back in
the ’so’s. A lawyer by training, he was
politician, historian and bachelor by choice,
very well provided with cash, solemn, sandy
and 54.

We who have dreamed and dreamed we dreamed
alone,
Within the zone where Thought's grey river winds
Through groves of passion-vines and tamarinds,
Have heard some fist within the magic zone
Bang a wild gong, clanging a brazen tone;
And lo! Each drowsy dreamer wakes and finds
This idiot with a hundred million minds
And not a single mind to call his own !
Hands to mazed head the quavering madman runs;
Mumbling strange words of parliaments and kings :
Or salves with Mirth his gaping, cancerous woes ;
Or tosses wrath from out the mouths of guns,
Or wiiii arched eyes he speaks tremendous things,
Yet grins when some comedian thumbs a nose!
maoriland. BOYCE BOWDEN.

Hush, my country ! All is well!
Things assume a roseate look ;
Cheery news the papers tell:
“Mister Hughes sees Mister Cook.”
Week by week, and day by day,
Read the satisfying news,
Chasing all our doubts away-
“Mister Cook sees Mister Hughes” !

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The prompt scratching-of Patrobas • and
Shepherd King for the Australian Cup was a protest
against the handicapper’s rating of them as being
a pound or two better than Lavendo., But it seemed
reasonable to reckon them more likely than Lavendo
to carry 9st. into first place at the end of a 2-j-mile
race. Shepherd King and Lavendo each won a Caul-
field Cup, carrying ,7st. 71b and 7st. 31b. respect-
ively, and each ran in a Melbourne Cup, carrying
Bst. 31b. and 7st. 131 b. respectively. The King
came second to the 3-year-old Sasanoff this year,
whereas Lavendo had run nowhere behind 3-year-old
Patrobas last year. Patrobas and Shepherd King
are proved stayers and look capable of being wound
up tor a long journey. The brilliant Lavendo is
not a proved stayer and he doesn’t look capable of
standing a severe preparation. To this writer there
seems nothing in the handicapping to complain of
except that Wolaroi (Bst. 41b.), taking him on His
very best form, was kindly treated.
Sticking out from the Newmarket Handi-
cap chances is Ettefred, sister to Traquette on the
maternal side.' Ettefred at 7st. 81b. is thrown in.
She won the Maribyrnong Plate 15 months ago
down the Flemington straight. She won the Caul-
field Guineas brilliantly in October last, beating
Wolaroi and Meld King. On her day Ettefred is
very fast, and the first week in March is a good
time for mares. The thing to be said against her
is that she has seemed quite untrustworthy thus far.
She is by George Frederick. Half-sister Traquette
was by Traquail-, which made all the difference in
the matter of streaking away when the barrier rose.
Still it must be admitted that a mare of Ettefred’s
occasional excellence is well in the Newmarket with
■ only 7st. 81b. to carry. Of the English horses that
are still of an improving age and are apparently
coming along nhelv, Highfield (Bst. 111 b.) and
Whitefield look likely ones. Especially Whitefield.
lie won three races out of four as a 3-year-old in
England.
■:+***
The much-boomed Torch.v arrived first in
the Welter at Warwick Farm (Sydney) on Saturday
with McLachhrn aboard. Torchy is trained by the
veteran Harry Rayner. lie had been galloping well
for a long time and was solidly backed lately to
win a couple of big sprints at Randwick. He got
punctured in both. The mokes he downed on
Saturday were anything but champions and he had
only five pounds from the minimum, Bst. Still he
won well enough to justify the belief that he may
land in front again before long.
liandwick trainer Fred Williams has a
good one in the two-year-old Greenstead. The young-
ster’s first victory was at Wyong on Tuesday and the
.second at Warwick Farm on Saturday, where Green-
stead had to carry 51b. penalty. The nag is by The
'Welkin out of Tuning Fork, and last year cost 100
guineas. Greenstead went out at even-monev, and
won by fully eight lengths from Frank McGrath’s
filly Malt Maid, which was expected to do well at
the recent Randwick rdbeting, but did no better than
run prominently. In third place in this race was
Amera’s Daughter, owned and trained by W. Booth,
of Rosehill. Booth has turned out more two-year-
old winners and place-getters in the last three seasons
than most of the other trainers combined.
* * *- *
Karanaud King is a strange mixture. At
Randwick recently when expected to run well for-
ward he Was almost last. Later, he ran so well in the
Anniversary Handicap that he was one of the first to
Hit the home stretch. Right up to the distance he
looked to have a chance of at least getting a place,
but joined in with the also-rans. In the Warwick
Farm llaiidicap on Saturday the brute led practically
all the way, and lasted long enough to beat Narivo,
another disappointment, by a short head, with Willow
Green, yvho started favorite, a couple of lengths
behind. Karanaud King is the property of Jimmy
McMahon, Sydney’s biggest carrier and one-time
representative Rugby Union full-back. The writer
would sooner own the carrying outfit than Jimmy’s
moke.
At the “picnic” Lake Albert meeting
(S.A.) an event with only two starters was a cer-
tainty for one, and it was a nice point whether the
investments on the other would be enough to pay'
the machine’s commission on the lot. They were —
just; and backers of the winner - got" their money
back, without loss! The equitable machine needs
room to turn round in. Fortunately events with
two starters are not common.
This Lake Albert meeting saw some record hard
luck on the part of Allan Bowman, its principal sup-
porter. There were seven events, and he had a cut
at every one <*f them. In three of them there were
onlv two starters. The Bowman colors got six sec-
onds and a third! And Mr. Bowman himself was
taken ill and removed to hospital a day or two before.
Jockey 11. Jenkins won all five flat races
on the programme at Broken Hill last week, and
thus did a fair day’s work for several days’ pay. But
the books saw nothing to cheer about. Each nag
was a favorite.
The Australian-bred colt Biplane and a
colt named Hvmestra,. owned by J. D. Ormond, are
the star performers among the young horses racing
this season in Maoriland, Up to the present it
looks as if Hvmestra is the smartest when the
journey is short, and Biplane the better over a bit
of distance. - Biplane won the Great Northern Foal
Stakes at Auckland, six furlongs, but was beaten by
Hvmestra in the Kilburn Plate at Wellington, four
furlongs. Last week the colt won the Middle Park
Plate at Chchurch, running the distance in Imin.
13sec. Biplane is owned by J. R. Greenwood and
trained bv Richard' Mason, the most successful con-
ductor of two-vear-olds in Australasia. The argu-
ment over the pair may be settled at Easter in the
Champagne Stakes.
“Spreader”: The objection of the racing
clubs to a doubles tote is that it keens too much
money locked up while the two legs and intervening
races (if any) are being run. With only a straight-
out machine operating the club deducts a percentage
each race from practically the same monev ; but if
the thick end of the capital available is diverted to
a “double,” covering, say, four consecutive races,
then the loss , of revenue is a Very serious matter to
everyone except the people who provide the cash.
“JJI.M.”: About betting shops (B.
11/1/’l7). What a harvest they used to make out
of the ante-post betting with their “all in sys-
tem! When Fairy Gold’ (1 think it was) dropped
dead a couple of days before the Melbourne Cup a
big, husky wharf-laborer came into a shop where he
had backed the horse and demanded his money back.
The penciller tried to explain that the bet was
made “all in,” but the punter was not satisfied, and
threatened to knock the shop to splinters and pick
his teeth with them. So the clerk tried to “square
off” to him by saying how stiff his luck was, too—
that he had a fiver on a horse and it was scratched.
“Oh, scratched ?” said the aggrieved wharfie. “I
don’t mind a horse being scratched, but I’ll have no
drop dead on me.”
* * + *
In the final race-meeting of the English
season, held at Newmarket, Bob Sievier (late Sutton)
had the luck to pull off two events. The second was
a selling race on the concluding day, and he won it
at “any price” with the somewhat oddly-named
Lady Randy, by Simon Square out of Marlborough
Duchess.
It was Fred Kay and Harry Stone again
on Saturday—this time at Sydney Stadium. Henry
jumped much less than usual, mainly because leap-
ing around Kay is about as useful as bombarding
him with a mouth-organ. The trouble is to get
at him, the principal obstacles being an inconvenient
right and a left concealed in the second entrench-
ment, for when the first line has been carried the
next has a habit of springing over to where the
other was. At times Stone scored well, but his
custom of hitting with the inside of the glove took
a lot off the count. The accumulation at the end
was with the lanky riddle from the West, who still
can fool ’em all. Kay is not so much a pug as a
conundrum.
Vince Blackburn (N.S.W.) and Jack Jan-
ncse (Vic.) had another argument about the
bantam championship on Saturday. The controversy
raged at Melbourne. This time it was not Blackburn
who won, and John retired from the scene decorated
once more with the label the other youth had torn
from him at the temple in Rushcutter’s Bay. It
was an earnestly-conducted exchange of views, with
Blackburn mostly on the aggressive and the other
fellow’s left interposing. The Melbourne lad is a
fine defensive boxer, and, though he was once or
twice rattled by the onslaught, the referee took the
view that skill in this direction was entitled to more
points than was Blackburn’s system of assault.
Point scoring in boxing is a mysterious, business and
unless one understands it —which nobody does —it
was hard to see enough between these two on Satur-
day to justify a decision either way. Naturally,
therefore, victory has not brought peace in the
bantam department.
TOM WARNE. Victoria.
The lightweight championship of the
Speck was alleged to be at stake when “Snowy”
Corbett, the holder, ran foul of a gent, labelled
Rud-Kee, described as a Chow champion. Rud may
have devastated China all right, but he failed to
make much impression on Corbett, and after 20
rounds of unpleasantness, the white hope got the
verdict.
* * * *
Les Darcy, glove-fighter (only), writing-
like a schoolboy from New York on January 3:
“Just a line to let you know my whereabouts. I
was sorry to have to run away like that; but you
know why I done it. I am all right on this side,
if I am all wrong on that side. I am in, bad, I
know; but why should I worry ? I reckon I am
another Houdini, don’t you? We had a lovely trip,
a bit lonely. Sixty-two days! How did -—- get
on? Did he go into camp? Poor old r!” But
all the time it’s “poor old Darcy,” the runaway.
-If Uncle Sam comes into the scrap, where
will Darcy bolt to for a fight or two in pleace and
comfort ? It almost looks as if he will' have to
pussyfoot back to Australia, as the only country on
earth where the war doesn’t matter. Anyhow, if
the U.S. does get into holds with the Kaiser, the
public thereabouts will have mighty little time for
anyone who deliberately bolted. Even as things were
when he arrived, most of the newspapers made re-
marks that were a long way from complimentary
and warned Darcy that he wasn’t the draw he would
have been had he appeared under different conditions.
The question whether a scrap could be arranged
between him and Carpeptier appeared to hinge less
on whether the Frenchman could get leave to box
in America in aid of the Red Cross funds than
whether he would consent to have anything to do
with Darcy. As events are shaping, it looks 100
to 1 that he would not, and that the Yank promoter
would hesitate about offering the Austrajian any
heavy wads.
Letters from U.S.A. say that Griffo, the
old-time Sydney lightweight boxing wonder, has been
rescued from the slums of New York and set on his
feet. A few years ago there seemed no hope for
him, but good friends stuck to him and “to-day he
looks a business man with grey hair.” He may
come this way as a music-hall turn.
“Eryander” (B. 18/1/T7) speaks as if
Sandow, with his supple muscles, was a freak. The
old idea of training was for athletes to get hard.
Men stood up with their muscles tensed while
trainers smacked ’em with hand and towels. This
“getting hard” was the accepted idea. Even cyclists
were treated so that all the waste tissue was well
rubbed and rolled out of their limbs. To-day the
system in every branch of athletics is to make all
muscles soft and pliable. When an athlete loses his
flexibility he loses his chief asset. It is the begin-
ning of the end with him.
* * * *
Heavy rain, broken bridges, torn-up roads
and fallen trees were mere incidents to Alex. Jewell
and J. Walsh, who the other day drove a 45-li.p.
Studebaker Six between Sydney and Brisbane in 324
hours. The previous “best” (36 hours 50min.) was
made by Dodwell 4 and Lang in 1912. Three sets of
non-skid chains were-worn out on the journey.
The motor cyclist’s idea of carrying a dead-
weight of relative or soft, round girl on a cushion
behind his saddle keeps money in the family; but
care .should be taken that the passenger’s garments
don’t obscure the stern-light of the machine. A
Melbourne man took his father-in-law for a ride last
week, and when he was pulled up for showing no
red disc, he discovered that the danger-signal had
been hidden bv the old man’s hanging coat-tails.
Then the startled old man investigated behind him-
self and found that his tails had been burned to a
cinder.
Fanny Duraek added another champion-
ship to her costume last week by winning the N. S.
Wales 300yds. title, with 50yds. to spare from M.
Winn and M. Lovelace, in 4min. llsec. Earlier in
the week Fanny cut the 300vds. record back to 4min.
2 1-s°'’'’ b- "imping 9 3-ssec. off some figures she
created in 1913.

The long arm of coincidence elbowed its
wav into Bananaland’s 100 yards Swimming Cham-
pionship for 1917. The first two home last .year in
this race were Springfield and Pierce. Both are
now arguing the point with a bomb. This vear’s
result also reads Springfield 1, Pierce 2—young
brothers of last year’s place-getters who are keeping
the families supplied with trophies.

Tom Sullivan, oarsman, who with his son
fpught in France with the Maorilanders and was
(again with his son) made prisoner by the ITuns,
gets a quittance from Germany under an agreement
of exchange with Britain. Young Sullivan' isn’t of
the prescribed age, so he remains at Ruhleben. One
of Tom’s performances was to cut out three miles
330yds. on the Parramatta in 18min. 414 sec. That
was on .Tune 28, 1891.
“Haka”: Answered “Here!” at the roll-
call of the 24th Reinforcements, G. Smith, of
Feilding (M.L.), well-known horseman, athlete, shot
and shearer. His best performance with the tongs
is 260, and at the hop, step and jump 44ft. 9in. ;
highest leap, sft. 6.fin. Which reminds me that in
1901 another Maorilander, C. C. Laurie, won the
Australasian Championship with sft. sin. Smith was
always in great demand as a rider, and at the
last Palmerston Show steered! eight out of 14 jumpers
in one competition. His tally for the day was 296
fences without a spill.
* * * *
“S.T.S.”; Have you got hold of the new
trick of. walking under water? I thought I knew
most things that can be done there, but this seems
a new principle. The man I saw simply proceeded to
the botttom of six feet of sea, and, leaning far forward,
without apparently using the hands, kicked the sand
in gigantic strides. He had no weights, and why his
head did not bob up to the surface—well, that is the
. new principle. The important point is that he
travelled faster than any swimmer, lie says he can
walk 50 yards in 17secs., and I believe it. This
thing has to be barred, or it will, where the condi-
tions in shallow water are favorable, plav up with
swimming races. And in long-distance diving how
can it be barred ?
“Yarran”: Well, put up your money.
Recently old Billy Campbell left his selection on tile
Dawson to catch the 6 p.m. train at Wallumbilla
(Q-).i distance, 40 miles ; start, 6 a.m. He arrived
in time to give a lengthy discourse, damning King,
Country and Billy Hughes for rejecting him. Age,
70-odd. There is a purse here to say that he will
walk anything of his age off the face of the earth.
* * * *
Chas. Dunell Rudd, whose death is
announced, was one of the athletes of his time in
England, though he is better remembered for his
association with Cecil Rhodes in the purchase of two
quarter-shares in the De Beers diamond mines. With
this interest to work on they succeeded in bringing
about the formation of De Beers Consolidated. At
Harrow in 1862. when he was 18, Rudd won the
quarter, the mile, the hurdles and throwing the
cricket ball, At Cambridge he won the inter-Univer-
sity racquet match, and later he was one of the.
famous big-game hunters of S’Africa. He was in
the confidence of Lobengula, from whom he obtained
the land and mineral concessions for the British
South Africa Co., and among his most valued posses-
sions were the original charters, which he kept, a
memento of one of the biggest deals in which a
simple savage was ever the partv of the other part.
“Pick”: In the stage-conch days, Jack
Hurley, a noted Monaro (N.S.W.) go-as-you-please
Tied., • undertook to race the mail-coach from
Cooma to Nimitvbelle, 23 miles. He took what-
ever advantage the winding road gave for short
cuts, but the gain thus afforded was not more than
a couple of miles. When the mail rolled into
Nimitybelle Hurley was digesting his second long
beer. Bill Sang, a half-breed Chinese, reckoned that
he could eclipse Hurley bv beating the coach from
Nimitybelle to Bombala, 33 miles. He led the
way to Bibbenluke, 23 miles along the track, but
there dropped more or less dead.
“F.H.E.”: G arae is becoming short nowa-
days. I know, because me old man, who is the
best long-distance shot in the district, has to
lather his cartridges with salt to keep the game
he hits fresh.

“Jobber” : For the old-age stakes in horses
I’d like to nominate old Bob, who was bred and
owned by the late Major E. H. Weston, of The
Meadows, Albion Park (N.S.W.), one of the finest
horsemen the State has produced. The old prad was
32 when he passed the post.
* * * *
“P.F.” : What they call the black bream
in Tasmania is a good sporting fish running up to
41b. or 51b. and .fine eating. It goes to sea in the
winter, but in the summer it comes into the brackish
water of creeks and estuaries. Many of the creeks
on the coast have their mouths closed by sand-bars,
and unless there is a flood in the soring or early
summer to clear the channel the bream cannot get
in. But it’s all right this year, because the heavy
floods about Christmas broke all the sand-bars, and
there is better bream-fishing to be got than there
has been for many years.
* * * *
Adelaide’s athletes have taken the war
very seriously. Last summer there was a little
unofficial cricket; this year there is none scheduled,
and an occasional match between two corps or two
camps has all the charm oi novelty. A recent game,
Dental Corps v. Exhibition Camp, brought out two
well-known names. Arthur, for the camp, took nine
wickets for 22, but his side did not win. He had
been put on a bit too late, and Desmazures, for the
dentists, had had a big innings. He did not remain
for the drawing of stumps, but his 94 (c. and b.
Arthur) was enough to win on.
Country rowers had things nearly all'their
own way at the 79tli Hobart Regatta. The Frank-
lin Club, from the Huon, scored first in five races.
The five Hobart and suburban clubs had to be satis-
fied with three firsts. There was one competitor in
the Alexandra Sculls whose age puts him out of the
running for enlistment. This was A. F. .Sharland, a
youth of 60, who was the amateur sculling champion
of Australia 30 years ago and put up a record that
still stands. by winning the Alexandra Sculls five
years running in 1882-188 G. He finished fourth this
time, off the 30sec. mark.
Police raided the steamers following last
Saturday’s sailing race on Sydney Harbor and
arrested five men for betting on the results. Simul-
taneously on land two horse-race meetings were being
held in the metropolitan area, and the Law was
giving the glad hand to backers and layers and
having a flutter on its own. Just why it should be
moral to bet on a horse with a kid on his back and
immoral to bet on a > sailing-boat with a man at the
tiller the Law forgets to explain.
“Old Flick” : This is the original, blown-
in-the-giass way to remove obstructions in rifle
barrels. Place the barrel in a charcoal fire, turning
slowly to avoid unequal expansion. A very moderate
heat is sufficient to char the obstruction at the edges,
especially if it is an oily rag, as it is in 99 per cent,
of cases. A gentle prod with the rod will then
eject it. Cool the barrel by dabbing it with a rag
dipped in melted tallow, which will also restore the
temper. Seeing how badly Australia needs popula-
tion, the practice of blowing out obstructions can’t
be too severely condemned. I tried it once. The
breech of the rifle went skywards, and though it
happened 15 years ago it hasn’t come down yet. My
eyebrows and whiskers likewise disappeared, but I
got them back.
“Xela” : Sharks seem to be extra-hungry
this season. While the Manuka was recently doing
15 knots across the Tasman Sea a big fellow made
a snap at the distance-log trailing astern. The
lawyer succeeded in biting off about 21b. of brass
and left one of his teeth imbedded in the remaining
portion of the instrument.
“Pick”: This is the way to remove a
jambed pull-through. Fill the barrel above or
below the obstruction with kerosene and let it
soak for the night. In the morning you may push
out the lubricated rag with a ramrod or length of
thick wire, as easily as you could pass a needle
through the thirst of a camel.
The best score by any unit of an over-sea
reinforcement draft during test shooting in England
was registered the other day by Sergt. Hugo Freeth,
a Maorilander. Over seven ranges he accumulated
156 out of a possible 170, including 41 out of 45
in the frenzied effort known as “the mad minute.’’
For this performance the sergeant was grabbed front
behind and rushed off to instruct at a school of
musketry. He is quite a youngster and contracted
the shooting habit as a kid.
. Edmund Fisher, crispest of versifiers,
should re-write his famous rhymed narrative, “The
Billiard-Marker’s Yarn.” Girls have now taken
up the smoky job in London. But they won’t be
able to learn the tricks of the business in less than
two generations.
* * * *
Australian novelist and explorer Mary
Gaunt has been putting in a good word for pet dogs
threatened with annihilatiofr by English food econo-
mists and other strenuous war reformers. In the
Daily Mail she deplores the “unkind fate that made
me a cheerless widow,” and rejoices that she has
found some compensation in a small Japanese pug,
“my companion and friend.” She would consider
herself “worse than Judas” if she were to pilot the
pet into the lethal chamber. On the other hand,
novelist George Moore, not being a cheerless widow,
reckons there are a million and a half of dogs in
London alone, and “some of them eat as much as
two men.” He found 37 dogs living luxuriously in
one fashionable hotel, their owners paying 3s. 6d.
per day for their board and lodging. “The dog
question goes to the very heart of things,” and
Moore would raise the tax on the brutes to £5 at
the very least.
The English girl gets a new sort of chance
these war-times. London Express (20/11/’l6) adver-
tises for “six gii'ls who can ride well, weight not
to exceed Bst. 71b., for general work in large racing
stable. Furnished cottages close to work.”
“Tvke” : My mate had had a long run of
luck with the dice, so he gave me a six and three
throws with the other two. I threw a six and five in
the first throw, and stood on that. He had one
throw and got three sixes—lB. I reckoned his luck
was good enough for a ticket in Tatt’s, so we sent
for one. The ticket turned up No. 25542 =lB
in The Metropolitan No. 2. It arrived
on the 18th, and all the visible letters on
the rubber stamp on the ticket totalled 18. I
received a bill the next day for £6 6s. 6d. (18),
and reckoned we were on a cert. Got the result
yesterday, and we are just about as near the rhino
as the Kaiser is to London. Don’t talk to me
about straight Griffins. Have a banana.

Bay Bum loz.
Oi'lex Compound a small box

Kodak (Australasia), Ltd., formerly
Australian Kodak, Ltd., which began as from
June, 1908, with a small deficit representing
preliminary expenses:—

The directors explain it this way:—
During' the year just passed the co. has been in
the unique position of having full stocks of raw
material, and consequently has been able to supply
the unprecedented increased requirements of the trade.
As at September, 1914, “stocks of merchan-
dise, materials and supplies, at cost or
under,” were valued at £94,448. Twelve
months later they were down to £89,000;
now the total is £133,074. Probably, though
it is worth £40,000 more, the stock is no
larger to-day than it was when the war
started. And that suggests a danger; for if
the war sent up prices so suddenly and seri-
ously that a large profit was made out of the
stock bought at pre-war prices, peace may
be followed by a slump so sudden and severe
that a large loss may be made out of the
stock bought at war prices. Recognising
this, many a prudent manager has a con-
tingency fund built up out of war profits to
make good what may be called peace losses.
Perhaps this co. has some such stocking
somewhere. If there isn’t a hidden stock-
ing, at any rate the one on view is swelling
visibly. Last year £33,594 was put to for-
mal reserves —net profit in excess of the 20
p.c. dividend. There is a nice little bit there
to come and go on even if it should be found
presently that some of the stock bought
during the war must be sold at below cost.
But it is a remarkable document alto-
gether. Here are half a dozen points: (1
The assets side of the balance-sheet start i
this way: “Goodwill, trade marks, t. ....e
names and processes—no value has been
placed on these or anything other than actual
tangible assets.” (2) The co. risks no
money but its own. Its total liabilities-
trade accounts—are £49,080. (3) Of the
capital of £lBO,OOO more than half is in free-
hold. and one doesn’t usually expect to have
that security and a2O p.c. dividend. (4)
Profits are so ample that the co. generously
writes 2 p.c. p.a. off buildings, as well as
from. 5 p.c. to 40 p.c. off the original cost of
plant. (5) Shares in other cos. have on sale
hitherto yielded handsome profits; never-
theless the balance of .the shares held are in-
cluded at cost. (6) In addition to the in-
vestment of half the capital in freeholds, the
co. has approximately £45,000, or one-fourtli
the capital, in cash, shares and public securi-
ties (including £15,000 war loan). It isn’t
a good thing to linger long over these
accounts without smoked glasses. Let us
get on.

Five years ago the co. had £2109 of liquid

co.’s property isn’t all leasehold, though;
there is an adjacent freehold, with buildings,
valued altogether at about £12.000. Thus
the co. has, apart from the leasehold, about
£22,000 of assets towards the capital of
£48,000. The amounts shown as written off
go mostly towards the fund for replacing the
capital now represented by leasehold, and
are adequate. The profit is at'present cut
into pretty badly by taxes ; but there is still
a good margin over the present dividend
after providing for the depreciation of the
lease.
“Edo”: The war is made an excuse for
some extraordinary requests. Westralia’s
mining law wisely provides that mineowners
must annually supply the Minister with an
up-to-date plan of underground workings.
Every eo. of any size employs a surveyor,
who keeps adding to his plans from week to
week, and as all the Department requires is
a blue-print, it is a simple matter to provide
a copy of the plan. For small shows a
freehand dimensioned sketch is accepted.
In face of all this, the Westralian Chamber
of Mines solemnly approached the Minister
with a request to be excused for this year,
on account of the war, and the Minister
quite as solemnly granted the request. Now.
what can the war have to do with the
question? Not more than two regularly-
employed surveyors and draftsmen on
Westralian mines have enlisted. The only
conclusion to be drawn is that the Chamber
has something up its sleeve. There must be
some fairly good point to be gained by hold-
ing back the prints, and it will be interesting
to watch events. It will also be interesting
to hear what the Miners’ Union will say
on the subject, since in ease of a cave-in
causing many deaths a plan of workings is
a handy thing at the Coroner’s inquest^

Why Have Grey Hair?
When a simple, old-fashioned and harmless
recipe will correct it.
Few people know that grey hair is not a
necessary feature of age—that it can be
avoided without resorting to hair dyes A
very old. home-made remedy will turn the
hair back to a natural color in a few days.
It is only necessary to get from the chemist
two ounces of concentrate of tammalite and
mix it with three ounces of hay rum. Apply
this simple lotion to the hair for a few
nights with a small sponge and you will
soon have the pleasure of seeing the greyness
disappear. This recipe is perfectly harmless,
is neither sticky nor greasy, and has given
perfect satisfaction for many generations to
those in possession of the secret. —Advt. [4

From a tripper to Pago Pago, which is
on the way to Honolulu :
I didn’t know what a help the picture-show could
be to Civilisation till I came here. Civilisation
mostly consists of getting somebody to do things
for you, and the trouble here was to get the native
to do anything. So Civilisation sagged till the
picture-show was invented, and now the native will
work like a beaver to get enough to buy a ticket.
The show is run by the American Service Club,
which represents Civilisation ; Civilisation gets in
free, but the natives have to pay—which is to say
work. A little, while ago a variety troop visited
the island with some stirring turns and good old-
fashioned posters. The tariff, on the advice of Civili-
sation, was pushed up to a dollar and a half; and
as soon as the posters went up there was a stampede
to earn the 6s. 3d. Every native from three to a
hundred-and-ten, male or female, took to the bush
to gather copra and anything that could be cashed.
Then Civilisation knew it had made one of its mis-
takes. It ought to have recommended 10s. tickets.
It was just this way in the Garden of Eden. Civili-
sation would never have got a start if Eve hadn't
wanted a change of clothes.

IN BANANA LAND
“De Facto”: The Bulletin (18/1/’l7)
had a good word for the trucking of 5750
jumbucks from Barcaldine to Goondiwindi.
a distance of 1048 miles, with the loss of
only one wether. Well, what d’you say
about this? Recently there were trucked on
behalf of the Winchcombe-Carson Co., 9989
sheep at Aramac. They arrived at Dirran-
bandi, distant 1216 miles, three days later,
with the loss of only one wether. Upwards
of two inches <?f rain fell while this lot was
being handled. Another lot of 8855 trucked
from Aramac to Newmarket arrived at the
destination with no losses at all.
Daylight saving is rapidly assuming a
party aspect in Bananaland. The Courier
strenuously approves; the Daily Mail as
strenuously condemns; and the Labor press
is busy making capital out of it as one of
Hughes’s blackest iniquities. The sectarian
crowd will probably yet discover the trail of
the Scarlet Woman over the whole business.
Bernard Canniffe, who dragged two boys
out of the Brisbane the other day in the nick
of time, simply did it because he couldn’t
help it. He inherited the trick from his
father, Tom Canniffe, well known in the
Rivers and Harbors Department. Canniffe,
senior, has saved half a dozen persons from
drowning. About 30 years ago he did more.
At Bundaberg he went to the rescue of a
man who had been overcome by foul gas at
the bottom of a well, tied the unconscious
man to himself and brought him up to
safety.
From the Under-Sec. of Justice: —
With reference to a contributed paragraph appear-
ing in Tiie Bulletin of Ist "inst. relative to the
staff of the State Insurance Department, i am
directed to furnish you Svith the facts in regard to
the matter. The permanent staff of the Insurance
Department consists of 6.2 officers, and not 19, as
stated by your correspondent. _ Mr. Fihelly desires
that I enclose a list giving ..the names of employees,
which in itself will show how grossly inaccurate is
the statement of your correspondent.
The yarn of the former correspondent was
that “in the new Insurance Office 17 out of
a staff of 19 belong to Fihelly’s religion."
The list forwarded contains 62 names but
whether there are seven or 17 or 57 of
Fihelly’s religion among them only the Lord
knows, and He isn’t any more in-
terested than The Buixetin.
“ltan”: The timber industry is an endur-
ing argument in favor of Unification. When
Bananaland found that she had not as
much pine within easy reach as she thought,
she stopped any more going to N. S. Wales.
Annually, since then, she has been collaring,
at a royalty of 6d. per 100 ft., millions of feet
from the N. S. Wales northern rivers, though
she has far more hardwood forests locked
up than Ma has. If the forests are to be

properly managed they should go under a
Federal staff, who would not be. moved by
these petty State considerations, but act in

vital asset.
Billjim, junior, is living right up to the
standard set by his big brother. There is
a stretch of 23 miles between Maryborough
and Pialba, and the road is mostly gutter.
The other day two youngsters,- aged 11 and
12, set out from the first place on bikes to
cover the distance. About eight miles from
Pialba one of the back tyres blew out, so
the youngsters decided to walk. They got
the damaged tyre fixed at had a swim
and a ride round the township and started
on the return about 4 o’clock. Soon after
leaving, the same tyre began to hang out
signals of distress and the youngsters tied ti-
tree bark round the rim. About 10 miles from
home the ancient tyre decided to throw in
the sponge, and again the boys faced the
long trail, arriving home about 8 o’clock.
They had got over about 50 miles during
the day and were up in the morning in time
to milk the cows.
“Ilopbooze”: The stirring days of the
Gympie gold escort seem more recent
by reason of the death of __ Tommy
King on Frazer Island at Go. His
father was an American Civil War
veteran before he married in Ireland and
brought a family of four sons to Queensland,
where he entered the force. The boys slid
naturally into the mounted police and all
made good troopers, but Tommy’s peerless
skill has become a proverb. We were mates
in the late ’seventies when he brought off his
best job by running down Johnny Campbell
—_ a black devil who broke loose on the
Upper Burnett and acquired a German repu-
tation for murder, outrage and robbery, from
Gayndah to Gympie and Ipswich. King
picked up the nigger’s tracks near Gympie,
and after three weeks of doubling in the
coastal scrub ran him to Noosa Heads, where
his gin delivered him into the hands of a
local tribe, who handed him over to the
police. I formed one of Campbell’s escort
to Brisbane, where he was hanged, and he
told us that he had travelled in water and
walked backwards in a vain effort to throw
King off the trail. Another fine performance
was the capture of the Dora Dora murderers,
whom King and a mate tracked from New
England (N.S.W.) to Mackay. Tommy
was also a fine oarsman and had paddled in
Bananaland s champion fours.
“Shay”: “Ilopbooze,” re McCawley’s an-
pointment as judge (B. 1/2/Y7), is mentally
asquint. In the three cases successfully con-
tested before the Privy Council by McCawley,
he saved the people of Banarialand just on
£250,000. Had those cases been in ordinary
lawyers’ hands they would have made a
punch. That McCawley was receiving only
£BOO per annum makes “Hopbooze’s” case
all the weaker.
“D.J.C.” : No accounting for tastes. Re-
cently a Northern sanitary-man dashed past
the local doctor, standing admiring his
garden during the daylight-saving hour. Feel-
ing amiable that night the doctor called to
him : “Wait a moment and I’ll cut a bunch
of roses for you.” “No good to me, thanks,”
shouted the busy municipal official over his
shoulder. “I don’t like the smell of ’em.”
The Dark Bride.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No dawning’s daisies would I grow for you
Shaking the dew from their white pinafores,
With little pink lips ready for a kiss
Tiptoeing at the Morning’s open doors;
No fit bouquet for my dark bride were this!
But I would pluck you one swart bud of night,
A tulip, deep as velvet, muffled well
With heavy petals where the perfumes fold,
And the whole flower swings like a mourning bell
Above a hidden heart of molten gold. .
No tendrilled jasmines would I twine for you,
No bridal wreaths, fit for the bare brown arms
Of dancing girls; but, hidden from the glare
Of halls of pleasure, there are subtler charms
That offer ripe rewards to those who dare;
Which are betrayed by movements in the dusk,
By the hot touch of lips unseen; by eyes
Piercing warm blackness, where a limb gleams white,
A touch to dearer nakedness grown wise
Than that which postures for a caliph’s sight.
And I would fire for you a brazier
Set behind lacquered screens. No flame to pry—
Only the smoke that, rising thick and sweet,
Makes other screens; and then, for comfort, 1
Would clasp through incense your small trembling-
feet.
Or in a garden where the bulbul hides
Between knit shrubs, a hand like ivory
Parts the close leaves as breaks the sky a star.
Swift in my palms the coldness of a key
Spelling the easiest way to lift a bar!
And then the leaves will join again, as night
Joins the flat shore-line to the flatter sea ;
No rustle in the grove, no jewel’s gleam,
But in my fingers I shall hold the key
To prove, for me, it is not all a dream!
Wait till the slave has quenched the cresset’s flare,
Wait till the yawning dancing-girls go home,
Wait with short breath and loose, unlaced shoon,
Glad if the fireflies have forgot to roam,
Glad that to-night there is not any moon.
y*h.
What shall I bring you ? Bracelets made of jet ?
A storm of cloudy pearls from unlit seas?
Black diamonds priceless—steal from midnight’s heart
The ebon circlet? What were fee for these?
A linked joy the dawn must rive apart?
There are no lilies in our field of love,
Nor any daylight bloom. Yet we enclose
Within four walls a garden exquisite,
The touch apd taste of passion’s perfect rose
That only grows in darkness and in night!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, February 3, 1917.
My Dear Tha rg om i n baii, —
This afternoon his Ex. laid the foundation
stone of the new Town Hall. In recompense
he was given a trowel. Another was handed
to the Mayor (Alderman lletherington). but
neither proposes to go into so useful a busi-
ness as bricklaying. Incidentally, the
heavens wept.
The Mayoress wore a summery silk coat
and skirt, and cast envious glances at her
husband's overalls. Mrs. Ryan, in mole-
colored taffetas, wished she had arrayed her-
self in seaboots and oilskins. Mrs. Iluxham.
in Assam silk, and Mrs. Bill Hamilton, in
a checked tweed, were the only other Minis-
terial ladies present.
H. D. Brennan ceased to be deputy Fed-
eral taxation commissioner this week and
entered on his duties as Bananaland’s Cura-
tor. On Wednesday night the staff of the
Commonwealth’s Taxation branch met at
the Cafe Majestic to farewell him.
t About £lO,OOO of tiie £21,000 which the
Y.M.C.A. needs in order to carry on its
work among our soldiers has been raised.
The Y.M.C.A. rest-huts in France and Egypt
have been an unforgettable source of com-
fort to our boys, but —well, comfort costs
money.
Two well-known squatters, W. Glasson, ot
Warkoa station, Yeulba, and Harry Winter,
of Roma, left Australia this week for Lon-
don, where they will join a motor-transport
brigade. Both are over military age. The
Queensland Club farewelled them at the
usual dinner.
Byron Watkins, one of the first science
graduates at the local Uni., is in an English
munitions factory. R. G. Quin, another of
our Uni. boys, is likewise engaged.
Killed in France, on January 1(5, Lieut.
Nugent Le Poer Trench, only son of an old
Queensland bank manager. He was on the
cable staff in Canada when he felt it was
up to him, and went away with the 29th
Vancouver battalion.
To help the Comforts Funds of the sth
Light Horse, the 9th and 49th Battalions
and the 9th F.A., a cafd is to be established
in the basement under the Brisbane Club.
Feb. 6 will find it open.
Lome MeDougall, eldest daughter of %■
McDougalls of Cooyar station, is pledged
to Henry A. R. Veness, only son of Mr.
A. A. Veness. of Glen Innes. But don’t say
a word to a soul, my dear.
Lieut.-General R. Hoskins, who has been
placed in charge of the East African cam-
paign, in succession to Gen. Smuts, who goes
to London, has had a remarkable career of
soldiering. lie began under Kitchener in
Egypt, and at the end of the S’African cam-
paign was said to be the youngest brevet-
major in the army. Between that and the
big conflagration he had a few'little border
scraps in India and Africa. In August.
1914, he went out with the first 100,000, and
fought his way up to Mons and hack to the
Marne. Then he was given an important
African command, and now has the job of
taking Germany’s last little bit of colonial
empire. He’s a cousin of “Mario,” who used
to write you this weekly letter.
Is the Railway Department in conspiracy
with the Brisbane newspapers for the pur-
pose of increasing their circulation? It is
quite common to see a passenger on a subur-
ban line haul a copy of a daily from his
pocket and spread it on the seat to protect
liis clothes from the grime. Woman, having
no convenient pocket, just has to put up
with it. As Minister Coyne proposes to
abolish dirt-money for the employees,
wouldn’t it be a fair thing to pass it on to
the passengers?
Yours affectionately.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
If the Presidency of the Legislative Coun'
cil goes to Bill Hamilton, it will be strictly
in accordance with tradition. Hugh Nelson
was a 15-stoner; Arthur Morgan, 14 stone;
and Hamilton is about the same, if not a
stone morq. In the chair, at any rate, it is
tiie House of Fat.

. No outside haulage from any position, however difficult —
Thirty-two miles an hour to within eight miles of Armidale, in spite of heavy
rain from Murrurundi —
A broken bridge over a swollen creek meant an hour and a half’s delay, then
a plunge straight through and a wetting for the drivers—
The nearly impassable thirty miles to the top of Spicer’s Gap took five hours in
the driving rain—
A fallen tree across the track meant another hour and a half’s delay,
and yet this Studebaker Car lowered the previous record by four and a half hours —
More proof of Studebaker Quality —that’s all.

Miss Gladys Cooper Explains
Some Beauty Secrets.
The Popular London Star Favors Simple IVlethods.
I have been asked to give a few simple recipes that I know, either through personal
use or by observation, to be valuable to the toilet, and which are within the reach of the
average woman. In these days of £lOO facial treatments and elaborate and expensive
beautifying processes my suggestions may read like lessons in economy, but they are
not especially so intended. They are merely practical suggestions, in which the keynote
is “effectiveness.” All the materials or ingredients which 1 mention are either already
at hand in the home or may be readily procured from the chemist. Fortunately Ido not
suffer from the ailments or troubles enumerated below, but some people who are not so
fortunate have told me their experiences, and with your permission I will set forth some
remedies which they have found to be efficacious.
Home-made Hair Tonic.
My acquaintances say tliat a good stimulant
to the natural growth of the hair is boranium,
A very simple, effective and safe stimulating
lotion made up by mixing one ounce of bora-
nium with i pint of bay rum. This may be
applied to the hair roots occasionally with the
finger-tips with good results.
' Complexion Renewals.
Complexion experts advise me that a normal,
healthy complexion is constantly renewing it-
self by dropping off tiny flakes of worn-out
tissue, thus revealing the fresh young skin
underneath. They say that when this process
cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of water, or
witch-hazel, and use it as a face lotion,
smoothing the skin with the fingers until it
is dry. This method is perfectly harmless.
I am told that a really beautiful, natural, vel-
vety bloom results and remains for many hours
quite unaffected by the most trying conditions
out of doors or in the ballroom or theatre,
and that it gives a much more natural appear-
ance to some skins than does ordinary powder.
Grey Hair.
I have observed many attempts of many
people to conceal grey hair. Some of these
experiments were musing, some disastrous.
is checked by age, expo-
sure or some of many
causes, the complexion
becomes dull and ugly.
The rational treatment
recommended is to help
the skin perform the
natural f-u-n c t i o n s of
“shedding” worn-out tis-
sue. For this purpose, I
am informed, there is
nothing so good as pure
mercolized wax, used for
a few nights, just as you
would use a face cream.
It is claimed that it pos-
sesses a special affinity
for the effete scarf-skin,
which it quickly removes
by absorbing it. The face.
I am assured, will soon
look much younger and
prettier under this treat-
ment.
The Curling Iron.
Don’t use a hot iron to
curl your hair. Some of
my friends make the cun-
ningest sort of curls
Wherever they want
them simply by damp-
ing the hair with liquid
silmerine before retiring
MISS GLADYS COOPER.
Photo Wrather & Buy.
and some were success-
ful. Personally I believe
I shall let my hair turn
when the appointed time
comes, but if I were
going to try to evade it.
f would give a trial to a
real old “grandmother”
formula that would prob-
ably do the work. This
formula. I am informed,
has been used with de-
crees of success for many
generations, and consists
merely of two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite
mixed with three ounces
of bay rum. It is ap-
plied to the grey hair
a few times with a small
sponge, and ladies tell
me it appears to darken
the hair to a natural
shade, not like a dye. but
gradually and naturally.
Miscellaneous Hints.
A few useful hints from
my friends on aids to
beauty, are as follows:
* The eyebrows and laslies
may be made to grow
stronger and darker by
applying mennaline. a
at night. When the hair is dry in the morn-
ing it will be softly curly just where you
want it to be. This method is perfectly harm-
less, even beneficial to the hair, and the curls
last a long time. The liquid is quite pleasant
and neither sticky nor greasy.
Hoav to Shampoo.
Most women, I am informed, do not know
how to use stallax properly when shampooing
with it. Unless the hair is naturally very
oily, a stallax shampoo may sometimes leave
it rather dry. But I am told if you will ap_ply
olive oil freely to your hair and scalp just
before shampooing with stallax, the result is
most delightful. The hair will be left clean,
soft, bright and wavy, the olive oil having
properly balanced the action of this wonderful
hair cleanser.
Is Powder Necessary?
A great many women object to using pow-
der, for various reasons. The following
formula is a good one: Dissolve an ounce of
specially delicate pomade, with the finger-tips
at night.
Rouge is often obvious, and many women
therefore prefer to use powdered colliandum,
which gives a most natural tint.
The lips may be prevented from drying ana
chapping by touching them with a stick
of prolactum, which also gives them a good
color. , . ,
Freckles are most effectively combated witn
ammoniated mercury creams, of which jetta-
line is the best.

At St. Kilda the other day Aiiss Webb
married Air. Foote. They do not intend to
hyphenate their names.
It’s just one thing after another with the
Vic. Women’s Political Association. Re-
cently its weekly Government grant of £45
went; and now, “owing to a disagreement as
to policy,” Adela Pankhurst has left too.
A soldier’s suggestion f
A fitting- tribute could be paid to the memory of
our gallant lads who are buried in unmarked graves
all along the path of the recent battles on the
Somme, by the erection, after the war, of some
simple column setting forth that Australians are
buried here. There are hundreds of these graves
containing the remains of from six to 20 of our .lads
who have fallen and been buried hurriedly in a
common grave. Many indeed have been buried by
shell-fire, and it is of course impossible to identify
these spots. Permission would be readily granted
by the French nation, and I'm sure the Australian
nation would as readily find the small amount of
money that would he required.
“M. 8.” : All local records of families serv-
ing in the war are beaten by that of the
Cardews of the West Country (Eng.). The
family comes from Dr. Cornelius Cardew
(1747-1831), Chaplain-in-ordinary to the'
Prince of Wales of that bad old period. Of
direct descendants of the first Cardew 116
went on service. Ten were killed, 26
wounded, three are missing and two are
prisoners. The list of honors shows one
K.C.8., one C. 8., three C.M.G., six D. 5.0.,
Two 'MX. and' 23~mentioned in despatches.
Corp. C. L. Cardew,. A.A.S.C., was one of
the first seven who enlisted for service from
N. S. Wales.
“Karee” : Just before I got what brought
me into a Middlesex hospital, one of our
“birds” came back after recovering from a
“Blighty touch” and reported that a piece of
“whiz-bang” had smashed his arm. While
he was having it dressed, another whizzer
blew him and the dresser to—wherever they
were booked for. We picked up what re-
mained of them, but as we couldn’t tell
which was which we sorted the pieces in two
equal parts, put them in two sandbags, and
buried them under their respective names,
leaving them to sort themselves out on the
Day. of Judgment. By the way, it’s a nasty
feeling to have some of your best mate
under your nails.
“Kodak” : A lad in a skiff had dared to
snap a camera at a soldier mate standing at
the rail of a transport anchored in Sydney
Harbor, and the patrol crew swooped down
and took the machine from him. The sol-
diers hooted this performance and a few of
them tossed potatoes and rubbish. The
patrolmen didn’t like it, and one of them,
taking a parcel at random from a pile in the
boat, shouted : “If you don’t stop, I’ll throw
this overboard!” The childish threat only
amused the crowd and the hoots continued.
Splash went the parcel, and the little party-
in the skiff recovered from the water some
sodden c-ake and ruined comforts and a letter
and Bible (slightly damaged). The litera-
ture was sent aboard to the owner on a
string, and ap far as I know the patrol
which throws parcels overboard when it is
annoyed is still patrolling.
Another good Australian got a bad knock
when Serg.-Major H. R. Sparks, of North
Sydney, was blown up in the trenches. He
was one of the first to enlist in N. S. Wales,
and like his friend “Teddy” Larkin, M.L.A.,
lost a brother on the memorable 25th
April, when the A.I.F. was blooded. Larkin
fell a' few paces to the left of Sparks, whose
lirother was killed on his immediate right.
For his part in Lonesome Pine he was men-
tioned in despatches. After the evacuation
Sparks went on to the Western Front, where
a “coal-box” paralysed his legs. However,
there’s a chance that he’ll recover. In the
years before the war he was a solid worker
for Labor as hon. sec. of the Willoughby
(Sydney) P.L.L.

“The Old Soldier” writes
“Croce di Malta” (B. 11/1/’l7) is not far wrong
in his estimate of the Maltese. As for their ethno-
logy, there is very little known about it. They
call themselves “Smite’.r,” and the island dialect
is a very curious mix re of all the languages
spoken round the Mediterranean. The suggested
Phoenician origin of the isl. ’ri-s is nonsense. Mostly
the language used is Italian, with a strong infusion
of the Sicilian dialect, which contains many Greek
and Arabic derivatives. Malta has had a curious
history for the last 400 years or so. Early in the
16th century Charles V., Emperor of Germany, gave
it and the other islands of the group to the knights
of St. John whom the Turks had just expelled from
Rhodes. The Order at once set about the work
it had carried on at its old home of hunting down
the Turkish and Arab pirates who infested the
eastern Mediterranean, and continued it till the
growth of national navies did the knights out of
their job. Having nothing useful to do they then
became just like other knights. It was on June 11,
1798, that Napoleon, on his way to Egypt, sailed
into Yaletta Harbor, and persuaded the representa-
tives of the Order to let him leave a garrison behind
him. But he had no sooner sailed away than the
inhabitants began to find the soldiers a nuisariec,
and got into communication with the British General
commanding in Sicily, who sent over a supply of
■ arms and a young engineer subaltern. He was
exactly 20 when he started to besiege the garrison,
and he wasn’t much older when the flag came down
! in September, 1800. That subaltern died a field-
' marshal in 1870. He was Sir John Burgoyne, the
son of that General Burgoyne who was forced to
surrender to the Yankees ait Saratoga in 1777. John
Bull was then given the islands—there are three of
[ them —and the.-' people ieejSf -never to have . regretted
it.

When the quantity of food taken is too
large, or the quality too rich, sour stomach

eating, take Chamberlain’s Tablets and
the sour stomach may be avoided.

Constipation brings many ailments in

termed “starvation of the body,” for food
that is not digested is not assimilated by

nourished. Chamberlain’s Tablets will
relieve the most obstinate case of indi-
gestion. Many a long sufferer owes his
present good health to a course of Cham-
berlain’s Tablets.
Constipation resembles nothing so
much as the stopping of the sewerage

break of disease that would result if
this should happen. Then try to realise
the danger to yourself if you allow
your bowels to become clogged. The
poisonous matter which should pass off is
absorbed into the blood, which is the

system of all foul accumulations and re-
store it to a normal condition.
