g
Business, Bobbery, etc.
Jabez Wright, Labor M.L.A. for Broken
Hill, and ex-undertaker, talks shop:—
“V know, I never make a speech from notes —
only give it to 'em straight off me chest. Notes
jumble me up. Same way up at Broken Hill. No
matter how many measurements I took, I kepi ’em
all in me head. If I figured it down I always got
the width and the depth mixed somehow. Once I
got the length for the coffin mixed up with the time
for the post-mortem, and the coffin was delivered a
foot short. AVhat f did I do? Had two slits cut out
of the end with a key saw and anchored a few
, wreaths to his feet.
“Plenty variety about undertaking—-different case
every time.
“Once 1 put on a man who was terribly nervous.
He seemed afraid that while he was handlin’ the
remains Gabriel might blow his horn and the
remains turn on him. A man like that would never
make a tradesman. While he was with me a man
was found dead with his chin resting on his hands,
and o’ course, when we turned him over, there he
was in a fighting attitude and not lookin’ peaceful
as he should. It would never do to torn out the
finished article that way, so I told him to put a
strain on and force the arm down straight. Well,
just as the arm was gettin’ into the right position
his hand slipped and the dead man’s fist came back
like a misfired cranking-handle and dropped him.
He had a week’s wages coinin’, but I've never seen
him since.
“Embalming? Look, if I’d lived in the days of
the Egyptian mummifiers my name would be iii the
Bible instead of Hansard. They couldn’t embalm.
They only cured and smoked ’em.
“It’s good business to always keep a- big out-size
coffin in stock. When a big remains wants anything
it wants it quick. In any case, a big ’un is handy
to sleep in. 1 slept in one for years, and a more
natural, sleep y’ couldn’t have. No draught can
get y’, and y’ can't fall out. Once there was a
plague of mosquitoes came to Broken Hill and
everyone was stung mad. They had suckers on ’em
like can-openers. .They used to come prospectin’
round me, but as soon as they got their eye on the
coffin they hauled off, as they reckoned that wasn’t
what they were after.
“One day when Lord Northcote and his eyeglass
were up there —-oh, there’s the quorum bell!”
* * * #
“McNorth”: The voyage, of the barque
Inverclyde, which occupied a year from
New York to Port Adelaide (B. 14/12/TG),
is a rarity in the records of the Inver line.
These vessels, all graceful three-masted
barques built of steel, were constructed with
a special eye to speed, and they have
won many long-distance races. I was a
member of the ship’s crew when the Inver-
esk raced a steamer from the Cape of Good
Hope to Port Adelaide many years ago.
We ran well south for the easting down,
and saw nothing of the steamer till we
passed her anchored off Glenelg, near
Adelaide, waiting for a pilot. She had
beaten us by half an hour. Time, 23 days.
* * * *
“F.C.”: Who says we are not making
ourselves more efficient? Here is E: L.
Strange, gardener, of Ballarat, showing how
tomatoes can be budded on a potato stem
so that the one plant produces crops of two
different vegetables. I don’t grudge Strange
his laurels, but he merely bears out a
since-disputed discovery I made years ago
that by grafting a salt bush on a pepper tree
and budding thereon a spud sucker and an
onion bulb, you reap a crop of Irish stew.
W e’ve got to balance our accounts some-
how, and as we won’t decrease our imports
we’ve got to increase our production, even
if every law of Nature is capsized in the
process.
A neutral correspondent at Cologne de-
clares that “sometimes there are great
fights outside the boot shops.” They still
have bargain-sales in Berlin, apparently.
* * ’ *
“Marco Polo”: Apropos of the sinking
of the Itonus, “Gellibrand” (B. 11/1/T7)
is a trifle astray. Old Currie built all the
fleet except this one, which was the old
Anglia before he acquired her in the Cold
Country. . The “B” boat was the Buceph-
alus, well known here years ago. She was
sold to a Dutch crowd at Samarang and
dubbed Simongan, and if those long-suffer-
ing engines can scrape up six knots, she is
still doing something up there.
“Melb”: Another cry from Macedonia
because in the building of the new Newman
College it has been decided to furnish the
rotunda, assembly hall, gallery, library, re-
ception rooms, corridors, lecture hall, gym-
.nasium and billiard-room with Japanese
oak. Next to this foreigner, the committee
favors American white oak. Still, there are
a few Australians in Australia, wherefore
these establishments in Melbourne have been
fitted with our own incomparable timbers:
Commercial Travellers’ Club, Victorian
hardwood and Queensland maple; Mel-
bourne Steamship Co., Queensland maple;
Public Library, Queensland bean; Young
Women's Christian Association (a fine
place), Tasmanian hardwood and Aus-
tralian blackwood; Auditorium, Queens-
land maple. There are many others, but I
hand these up as the first lesson to the
enemy in our midst.
* * * *
“Exile”: Last mail from U.S.A. brought
a letter headed “Institute of Efficiency, New
York,” inquiring in letters a cow could read
without spectacles, “Are you making the
most of your powers?” On the Institute’s
own mailing-card I replied: “Yes, but next
time you post me a circular about Efficiency
see your clerk puts a 5-cent stamp on it, as
I paid 6 cents for tax.
DOUBLE BANKING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Poor Bridget Crowe had lost her Ned,
Who went away to fight the Prussian.
A big shell carried off his head.
The fact that Edward Crowe was dead,
The regimental surgeon said,
ould not permit of more discussion.
The widow wept a day and night.
A neighbor offered consolation.
“You have,” she said, “a pension right—-
Apply at once. They’ll give some slight
Bequest in money to requite
The country’s decent obligation.”
They fixed a pound a week before
Two days ,had gone, without contention.
Still Bridget lingered at the door.
Said sire, “If in this wicked war
I lose one darling husband more,
Ho I receive another pension?”
VICTORIA.

Cleaning Up.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f HE sun drooped low on rock and sand,
And also on the sea.
Matilda stroked with satin hand
The whiskered face of me.
The ocean rubbed the rugged land
With suds of surferte.
It was the world’s wide washing day;
The rain had scrubbed the trees ;
Tall branches with their coats of grey
Were hanging in the breeze.
The large cliffs standing round the bay
Were splashed about the knees.
A gentle summer steam arose,
The fishes pruned their fins.
The mermaids dipped their tender toes,
The mermen scraped their chins,
The earth’s large wash-tub seemed to close
Around‘ creation’s sins.
I said : “To-morrow will be clean,
As yesterday was fair;
To wliat a golden velvet sheen
You sandhill sleeks its hair,
While cloudy pillows softly lean
On new-made beds of air.”
Matilda’s eyes were wonder-wide,
She sat erect and tall,
The murmurous voices of the tide
Grew very faint and small,
She saw the drooping sun divide
The sea’s blue overall.
"See, see,” I cried, “the soap of dreams,
In foamy lather white,
Before the sun’s large razor gleams
To shave the sands aright,
Ere from the darkening East upstreams
The towel of the night.
“Observe,” I said, “the winds that sweep
Heaven’s second-storey flats,
See where the housemaids of the deep
Beat out'the bedroom mats”

CURSE O’ MOSES.
The Island Teacher's Wife.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE little grass-roofed house of the
Island administrator, or teacher, stood
amidst a cluster of waving, rustling
cocoanut palms 50 yards or so from high-
water mark. The clear blue water gently
lapped the clean white beach, and a line of
angry breakers marked the position of the
sheltering reef a mile to windward.
The island was one of the numerous lonely,
isolated specks that are dotted promiscuously
over the waters of Torres Strait. Away on
the western horizon the bold outline of the
Two Brothers showed faintly against the
sky, and the hazy shapes of many other
islands formed an irregular circle of varying
circumference.
A number of natives were seated on the
sand in front of the house. Some were
softly crooning a mournful, wailing chant;
others beat their bodies with the palms of
their hands, but gently and almost without
noise.
For their beloved teacher. “Matcha” Fer-
gusson, was lying sick inside the house-
sick with a fever aggravated by a scarcity of
proper medicines and invalid food; one of
the penalties paid by the good and courageous
white men and women who cheerfully accept
positions as teachers on isolated Torres
Strait islands, where there are no neighbors
and only occasional and haphazard means of
communication and transit. The beauties
and charms of tropical scene and surround-
ings pall before the dreary, monotonous
routine of teaching thick-headed niggers the
ways and virtues of the white man; and the
allegedly paternal Government that is re-
sponsible for their welfare leaves them to
shift mostly for themselves. Many are the
stories/that could be told of hardship, star-
vation, sickness and even death endured by
these brave people, whose existence is hardly
even suspected by city dwellers.
An emaciated white woman stepped
through the low doorway on to the verandah,
and, sheltering her eyes with her hand, gazed
earnestly seaward. Her eyes, bloodshot and
staring, told of long sick-bed vigils, but her
lips were pressed firmly in an effort to sup-
press all outward signs of weakness or
despair. She held up her hand to enjoin
silence.
“Do you see any boat yet, boys?” she
asked.
A big native stood up. “No, Mam,” he
answered respectfully, in good English.
“We are watching all the time. The signal-
fires are ready for lighting, but no boat has
yet come in sight. And oh, Mam,” he
asked appealingly, “is the Matcha (master)
getting better?”
The woman choked back a sob. “I don’t
know, Morto,” she said. “But let us hope
so. Tell the people to be as quiet as possi-
ble. And keep a good look-out for a lugger
or cutter to take the Matcha to Thursday
Island, where he can get doctors and medi-
cine.”
Morto raised his hand to his head in
imitation of the salute he had seen the
soldiers performing at Thursday Island,
and, taking a burning stick from a small
fire, went down to the beach, ready to make
smoke signals as soon as his keen eyes
detected a vessel.
Mrs. Fergusson tiptoed back into the dark
of the house. On a heap of mats &nd
blankets on the floor lay her husband, the
administrator of the island. He was a mere
yellow-skinned wreck with high cheek-bones
and sunken eyes. He muttered semi-deliri-
ously.
His wife knelt by his side and signed to a
native -woman to bring water.
The sick man turned his fevered eyes to
his wife.
“Any boat yet, Maggie?” he whispered.
“Not yet, dear,” she answered. “But
there is sure to be one soon. Cheer up ! All
the natives are on the lookout, and a craft
of some sort is bound to turn up soon. There
must be plenty of beehe-de-mer boats out this
fine weather, and some are sure to pass in
sight of us.” The wife spoke more bravely
than .she felt. The fine weather had lasted
for over a week, and no craft had come.
The place was too far away from the usual
pearling and beche-de-mer grounds to be
visited by any but casual or passing vessels.
She held a pannikin of water to her hus-
band's lii>s —water tepid and nauseating ;
cold water was out of the question on that
sun-blistered island.
Fergusson eagerly drained the pannikin,
and was refreshed. “Maggie,” he said in a
husky whisper, “this is a rotten life for you.
No life for any white woman. If I pass out,
you get away south and live as a decent
white woman should. This is a blackfellow’s
existence, and if the rotten Government did
its duty instead of”
“Don’t talk,” his wife interrupted. "It
only weakens you, and don’t, for goodness
sake, talk of dying. You’ll be all right in
a day or two.” She happened to glance
towards the door. “Oh. what is it Morto?”
Morto stood in the doorway impatiently
beckoning. His eyes were ablaze with sup-
pressed excitement.
“Mam, a cutter is in sight,” he said,
pointing to where the sapphire-tinted sea
showed between the boles of the cocoanut
palms. Right on the edge of the horizon
appeared a small, upright speck. At that
distance it looked about as big as a wax
match would 50ft. away.
Along the beach a dozen fires were already
sending columns of smoke at irregular in-
tervals as the natives controlled the signals
with mats.
Mrs. Fergusson went in to break the good
news to her husband.
Sam Meehan, master of the seven-ton
cutter Maori Witch, raised himself from the
hard bunk and looked long and anxiously at
the aneroid barometer hanging on the bulk-
head of the little, poky cabin.
“Somethin’s goin’ ter happen,” he mut-
tered. “We’re goin’ ter have a lump of
dirty weather soon. I’ll make a dart outer
this fer shelter. There’s too many blamed
islands and things about here fer an’ old
hooker like this with rotten gear in a nor’-
west blow.” He went up on deck, and wet-
ting a finger in his mouth held it over his
head.
“Wind’s shiftin’ already,” he said. “Iley !
you fellers for’rd there, stand by ter take a
reef in th’ mainsul.”
He took the tiller and jammed the vessel
up into the wind, while his crew of two
Binghis wrestled with the flapping canvas
and took in the reef.
Just as'all was secure, one of the boys
caught sight of the smoke signals on Fer-
gusson’s island.
‘'Boss,” he called. “You look 1 You see
singal longa island? Sumfin’ wrong! Them
feller make plenty singal.”
Captain Meehan looked at the signals.
He also looked at the barometer and the
weather horizon. He hesitated. He knew
that urgent signals are not made from an
island where there is a white man without
good cause. But what about the weather?
Grumbling and cursing to himself he
luffed up and steered for the smoke columns.
After carefully navigating the opening in
the reef, he anchored the Maori Witch close
in to the beach and went ashore.
Mrs. Fergusson met him on the beach.
“What’s up, Missus?” asked Meehan,
briefly.
Mrs, Fergusson explained. Her husband
was very -ill indeed, and in urgent need of
medical attention. He would hardly live
much longer under present conditions. Would
Captain Meehan take him to Thursday
Island? She was willing to pay anything in
reason.
Meehan didn’t hesitate.
“Get yer husband aboard at once,” lie
said, “an’ I’ll clear out to Thursday straight
away. You’ll want ter shake it up, because
there’s dirty weather cornin’, and I don’t
want ter be caught in here.” tie pointed to
the now threatening sky. “And, Missus,” he
added, “don’t yer worry yer head about
payin’. That’ll be all right.”
Carefully the sick man was carried down
to the beach and placed in the dinghy. Then
his wife stepped in with him.
“Wot!” said Meehan, “you ain’t cornin’,
are yer?”
Mrs. Fergusson looked up from placing a
pillow beneath her husband’s head.
“Of course, I am,” she replied. “Who’s
going to look after .him if I don’t go?”
- Meehan stood aghast.
“B-b-but, Missus,” he said, “it’s goin’ ter
blow like h 1 mean, like —like lianything.
You’ll die of fright. It’s a hundred miles to
Thursday, an’ this is only a dirty, stinkin’
cutter. Better stay at ’ome, Missus.. I’ll
look after ver husband.” He spoke appeal-
ingly. “Never no luck with a woman
aboard,” he muttered to himself.
But the lady stood firm, and fifteen
minutes later the Maori Witch was standing
out for the passage with the sick man in the
little cabin and his wife kneeling beside him
whispering words of comfort and telling him
all would now be well.
And Captain Meehan, steering with the
tiller between his knees, stated his belief to
the world in general that this would be a
bad-luck trip. “Never no luck with a
woman aboard,” he reasserted.
For the few remaining hours of the day
the cutter danced' through the steadily-
rising sea. Although the wind had not
freshened, a swell from the south-west,
coming at right angles to the sea, made its
presence felt. Before the sun set, in a blaze
of gloriously-colored but ominous cloud-
banks, Meehan took in the second reef of the
mainsail.
The barometer was falling steadily. The
western sky showed an inky-black patch low
down on the horizon. Despite the breeze
there was a curious, overpowering sense of
closeness and dread. Meehan saw to it that
the dinghy and galley were firmly lashed :
then he looked down the cabin hatchway and
told Mrs. Fergusson to make all movable
articles fast and hand up his oilskins.
At eight o’clock it was pitch dark, except
for the nhosphorescent flashes of the uneasy
sea,, and dead calm. The Maori Witch
rolled and pitched in the nasty cross sea, the
heavy canvas sails banged and flapped, and

hundred miles from medical assistance—need

It may be at the plough; in the workshop;
while splitting, fencing, branding, mustering or

receive nasty cuts and burns, and the body and
legs painful bruises and abrasions, which require
the immediate soothing, healing, antiseptic
powers of Zam-Buk to prevent festering or
poisoning, and ensure prompt and perfect heal-

the two black boys, seated on the main
hatch, softly chanted something that sounded
like a prayer.
Half an hour later an indescribable roar-
ing announced the proximity of the storm.
Little fitful gusts of wind took the tops off
m\ e waves a ll( l wet the deck with their spray,
i hen, as though struck by a heavy cannon
shell, the little cutter reeled suddenly, stag-
gered and plunged her lee-rail under.
Meehan pushed the tiller over with all his
strength, and the cutter, now tearing through
the water with her deck slanting at an
almost impossible angle, described a sharp
semi-circle and came up close to the wind.
Biting rain was now driving down the wind
in almost horizontal streams, and big black
seas rose and threatened to engulf this
Mom that dared to defy their power.
Solid, angry masses of foaming water
swept over the vessel, taking with them parts
of the bulwarks and leaving a trail of splin-
tered deck fittings in their wake.
Down fielow, Mrs. Fergusson, working by
the dim light of the swaying kerosene lamp
that illuminated the “tell-tale” compass and,
incidentally, the cabin, secured her husband
as best she could in the coffin-like bunk.
She stopped a heavy provision-box as it
careered across the floor, and lashed it, with
other articles that threatened to break away.
The thundering seas crashed on the top of
the cabin, and here and there a stream of
water descending from the roof told of ill-
fitting joints. For two hours she knelt on
the floor beside her husband, breathing a
prayer from her white lips, while the storm
banged and crashed outside.
Suddenly there was a lull. It only lasted
a few moments, but its death-like intensity
made it seem an hour to the woman shut up
in the cabin.
Then there was a mighty crash. An extra-
large sea had sw’ept down on the cutter.
What was that? A cry? The woman stood
iq) and listened intently. The Maori Witch
was tossing and jumping about in an alto-
gether strange manner—a manner that told
the woman that the vessel was no longer
under control.
She carefully pushed back the sliding
hatch-cover of the cabin, and, watching her
chance, climbed out on deck. The mainsail
was flapping and banging in an aimless
fashion.
There was no one steering! Mrs. Fer-
gusson felt for the tiller and pushed it over.
Instantly the sails filled and the cutter
forged ahead. She felt for the tackle
attached to the tiller handle and sat down
on the wet, sloping deck with her back
against the rail, her feet propped against a
cleat and the hauling part of the steering
tackle in her hand. Something bulky moved
alongside her.
“Pull th’ tiller—-to yer—a bit —Missus!”
shouted a. voice close to her.
Mrs. Fergusson heaved a sigh of relief
and obeyed.
“Oh. Captain,” she shouted, “what’s
wrong?”
“Big sea—come —aboard —broke—me leg
—an’ me arm—against—th’ rail. Boys
over—board —l reckon. Can —yer —steer?”
“Yes, I think,” Mrs. Fergusson shouted.
She had often handled pleasure-boats in fine
weather and had some knowledge of the
working of a rudder.
“Keep her—as —she is. I’ll —tell—yer—
what —ter do. I can—hang—on—here—
all right. Get—good hold. Don’t—leggo—
that rope. Yer might—go—over—board.”
Right throughout the long night the
woman hung on to the steering-rope and
obeyed the shouted orders of the crippled
man who lay beside her in the agony of
two broken limbs. The seas roared and
thundered incessantly, and the driving rain
and spray wet her to the skin. Her hair
streamed to leeward, her flimsy dress was
torn and tattered. Weak, shivering, at
times, half-fainting from the strain of the
rope in her hands and from the buffeting,
she listened ’for, and promptly obeyed,
Meehan’s orders. Her frail form seemed so
inadequate to the task, and several times
she nearly gave in. But the thought of her
sick husband in the cabin and tiie injured
man beside her and the fact that their only
hope of safety lay in her hands steeled her.
She looked straight ahead ; for the great
black rollers, as they reared their evil heads
on the weather quarter, unnerved The
steering rope cut her hands, and she tied it
round her arm for relief. Her cramped legs
tortured her and her teeth chattered inces-
santly. But she dared not move.
At length the eastern sky showed a dull
grey light which gradually became day. But
what a shamefaced dawn! A wide expanse
of whirling, foam-topped water beneath a
canopy of leaden, sullen clouds behind which
the sun hid his face.. The wind had gone
down, however, and was now a nice sailing
breeze. Straight ahead was a cluster of
islands.
The deck of the Maori Witch was a
shambles. Her dinghy and galley were gone
and splintered woodwork and torn hatch tar-
paulins told of the fury of the storm. Of
the two black boys there was no sign*
A bedraggled figure was still steering to
the orders of Meehan. The latter lay with
bis sound arm clasped round a remaining
portion of the after rail. He raised himself
slowly and painfully into a sitting posture
and looked ahead.
“Horn Island!” he said, exultingly, point-
ing to the islands ahead. “We’ve done th’
trick, Missus! Steer for that passage there
an’ we'll be into Thursday in an hour.”
The woman altered the course as directed,
and the Maori Witch sailed down the Horn
Island passage, dodging luggers and other
craft anchored in the harbor.
Some men aboard a schooner were struck
by the appearance of the Maori Witch
and her steersman. They saw something
was wrong, and put off to her. As they
climbed aboard the woman sank forward
as she lapsed into unconsciousness.
When she awoke in the little white-walled
hospital on the beach a nurse was bending
over her.
“My husband?” she asked, weakly—“is he
all right?.”
“Sssh ! you mustn’t talk. Yes, he’s doing
nicely. Now, drink this, and go to sleep
again.”
Mrs. Fergusson sighed happily and slept.
In the men’fs ward Captain Sain Meehan,
with leg and arm in splints, gave Fergusson
and everyone else in hearing the history of
recent events. “When she come aboard,”
he concluded, “I said as ’ow there never
was no luck with a woman aboard. But,
gorstruth, if she hadn’t come with us th’
ole Maori Witch would be bumpin’ along
th’ bottom of the Strait by now. and me an’
her husband would ha’ been fish feed.”
JOHN McLAREN.
A Terrible Thing.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HIS happened just after the coming
of six-o’clock closing, when the woe
of the publican was increased. That
really has nothing to do with the Dog Act;
but it serves, to introduce an appropriate
sense of tragedy.
Chiefly concerned were a youth and a girl.
The youth was known as the Death-adder
It is not certain that the girl was ever
christened.
The Death-adder was not nearly as
vicious as he sounds. As a matter of fact
he possessed a very keen sense of self-
preservation which kept him from aggres-
sive Christianity and the like. He hated
being hit and was the wofst lighter in his
street. But he could run and therefore sur-
vived to years of manhood. The Girl lived
in this street and used to watch him sur-
viving to years of manhood. And now he
began to cherish tender feelings towards her.
But it was done at a certain distance. The
Girl’s father possessed a terrible reputation.
The little boys believed that he filed his
teeth and that once he had killed a man for
spitting on his front fence. The Death-
adder despised the idea of enhancing this
reputation, so he avoided the father.
But manly thoughts kept coming to him,
and tumults of fierce passion surged through
his breast when he thought of her and did
not think of her father, so lie decided
to visit the Girl at her home. He chose
the evening when the man-killer was attend-
ing the Anti-Temperance rally. He made
liis way up the front path very stealthily
with a box of sweets that he had won in a
raffle. The Fates were against him. The
family dog sprang forth. The Death-adder
made him run, but the dog caught him,
winning by a trouser-leg. The Death-adder
pulled his other trouser-leg up as far as it
would go and ran swiftly home pretending
he was a harrier.
Thus it became necessary to win the con-
fidence of the dog. It was very hard to
approach the dog, which was claimed by its
owners to be a Great Dane. The dog him-
self had doubts about it, but never said any-
thing.
After a great deal of thought, the Death-
adder decided to bribe the dog with meat.
He therefore armed himself with as much
meat as he could carry externally, and met
the dog half-way. After lie had done this
for a few nights the dog began to swear by
him, and also to spring up at him and lick
his face. It was some time before the
Death-adder became used to the dog licking
him in the face. So he was now able to meet
the Girl of his desire on the doorstep. He
laid bare to her his inmost feelings, and even
displayed the part of his shirt next his
heart where he had written her name in
Indian ink. He then asked her if she
thought her father would kill him if he
sought his permission to pay her attentions.
The Girl wasn’t sure, but said she didn’t
think so.
Deriving what comfort he could from this,
the Death-adder wrote notifying the father
that he proposed seeing him on most solemn
business. Now the father had seven daugh-
ters, for he was very poor. When the Death-
added asked him if he might lay siege to the
Girl’s heart,, the father burst into tears and
said “Yes, please.” So he did so.
The lovers determined to celebrate the
occasion properly on the next Saturday,
which was a very hot day. They girded up
their loins, etc., and took themselves to
Sandringham. The dog went with them,
and the Death-adder also took his mixed-
bathing tent, which had two compartments.
He went into one compartment, and the Girl
went into the other. The Death-adder hung
his trousers outside so that they would know
which were their proper places. Then the
two spent a most delightful afternoon, with
about two thousand others. So long did they
tarry on the bosom of the mighty deep that
the Dog missed them and lifted up his voice
with dolor. Those near called him a b—
mong. Nevertheless, he cantered round the
tent until he caught sight of the Death-
adder’s trousers. He was struck by a light-
ning thought. Perceiving that the Death-
adder had gone and forgotten his trousers,
he decided to return them to their last place
of abode. He seized them as he blushed to
remember he had done once before, and made
his way homewards.
Some time afterwards the two lovers
shivered into their tent. Suddenly the
Death-adder discovered that his trousers
were gone. He wept aloud and wailed, “I
am undone.” To which the Girl replied,
“Well, so am I.” He completed his toilet as
far as possible, but not quite down to the
knees. Now the Death-adder had very
peculiar legs, and the Girl’s attention was
turned to them, and it was a great blow
to her. Still, her love triumphed, and she
built a castle of sand round him up to his
waistcoat.
When night fell they formed a scheme for
reaching home without arrest. They tore
the Death-adder’s shirt into strips, and wind-
ing them up his legs after the manner of
puttees they incorporated these strips with
his coat. The effect was startling. They
walked home, of course, and arrived about
two hours after the advent of the Death-
adder's trousers had created a profound sen-
sation. The very worst w T as feared, and the
Girl’s father prepared a piece of heavy wood
with a nail sticking out of it. Such was
their reception.
When he saw the piece of wood with the
nail in it, the Death-adder became very
eloquent. He spoke with great power, and
averted a scandal. He was greatly relieved
when the father announced that he would
not kill him this time, but he was warned off
the premises. Neither did the father yield
up the trousers, for they were not a bad fit.
